The Argument for Multiple Elements and the First Two-Element Theory
Argument for multiplicity and first two-element opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that elements cannot be one because composition requires multiplicity, and you will be able to describe the first two-element opinion that identifies fire and earth as the fundamental extremes.

Core Idea
Composition demands multiplicity — elements cannot be a single substance.

After exploring monist views, the text now turns to the position that elements must be multiple. The core reasoning is that anything composite requires multiple components. Among those who hold for two elements, there are three distinct opinions. The first opinion identifies fire and earth as the two fundamental extremes, with air as a tempered form of fire and water likely as a diluted form, though the text cuts off before fully describing the third opinion and the remaining two.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for multiplicity
Notice the logical claim: because generated things (المركبات) are composed, their elements must be multiple. This refutes monism.
This argument is foundational for all later theories.
2
Identify the first two-element opinion
The first opinion selects fire and earth as the two elements due to extreme qualities: fire extreme in lightness/heat, earth extreme in heaviness/cold.
Air is described as 'tempered fire' (نار مفترة).
3
Reflect on the incomplete text
The passage ends abruptly with water, likely part of the first opinion or the start of the second. Understand that this is only a partial view of the three opinions.
The source is cut off; only the first opinion is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : ( ليست واحدة بل لا بد من التعدد ) فيها لأن التركيب في يستدعي تعدد ما منه تركيبها ( فاثنان على ثلاثة أقوال الأول : ) هما ( النار لأنها في غاية الخفة والحرارة، والأرض لأنها في غاية الثقل ،والبرودة والهواء نار مفترة، والماء
And it is said: They are not one, but rather multiplicity is necessary in them because composition in [generated things] necessitates multiplicity of that from which their composition [comes]. So two [elements] [is a view] on three opinions: The first: They are fire, because it is in the extreme of lightness and heat, and earth, because it is in the extreme of heaviness and cold, and air is tempered fire, and water...
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that the elements cannot be one because composition requires multiple components. It then lists opinions that hold there are exactly two elements. The first opinion identifies fire and earth as the two primary elements: fire is the extreme of lightness and heat, earth the extreme of heaviness and cold, and air is considered a 'tempered fire' (fire that has been moderated) and water is implied to be a diluted version, though the text cuts off. The remaining two opinions are not fully given in this window, but the structure indicates three opinions for duality. This refutes the monist views and supports the necessity of at least two principles.

Key Terms
تعدد
اثنان
نار مفترة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'نار مفترة' means fire that is weak or insubstantial.
Correction: It means 'tempered or moderated fire', not weak. Air is fire that has been toned down from its extreme state.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion for two elements, what are the two fundamental elements?
A) Fire and air
B) Fire and earth
C) Air and water
D) Earth and water

Key Takeaway
The first two-element theory posits fire and earth as the two primary elements, with air as tempered fire and water as a derivative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument says that composition necessitates multiplicity. Your own existence is a composition of body and soul, of earth and fire. Does this truth make you feel fragile or dependent? In what way does the multiplicity of your being call you to acknowledge the One Who brought you together and sustains you?
?
If you are composite, you are not self-sufficient. How does the fact that you are made of multiple elements—each needing the other—humble your pride? What would change in your daily life if you genuinely lived acknowledging that your very composition points to a Creator?

Refuting the Sphere and Dryness of Fire
Sanusi denies proof for the sphere of fire and refutes the argument that fire's consumption of moisture proves its dryness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' arguments for a sphere of fire and the dryness of fire are not conclusive.

Core Idea
Observing fire does not prove a surrounding sphere, and consuming moisture does not prove inherent dryness.

Sanusi begins by challenging the philosophers' assertions about fire. He first denies any proof for a sphere of fire surrounding the earth, arguing that observed transformations into fire do not necessitate such a sphere. He then turns to the claim that fire is dry, showing that its consumption of moisture is an insufficient argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on fire
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the two main challenges: denial of the sphere of fire and refutation of dryness from moisture consumption.
Notice how Sanusi uses logical reasoning and counterexamples.
2
Identify the denial of the sphere
Find where Sanusi denies proof for the sphere of fire and his argument using observed transformations and the possibility of hot air causing meteors.
3
Analyze the dryness argument
Examine why consuming moisture does not prove dryness; note the counterexamples of air and sunlight.
Sanusi emphasizes that experiment is impossible for pure fire.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that without certainty, the philosophers' claims remain unsubstantiated.
This reinforces the methodological skepticism of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثالث : المقصد الأول : قول الحكماء في أقسام العناصر أحوالها بتفاوتها في مراتبه فبعضها يرسب في الماء إلى تحت وبعضها يغوص فيه ولا يرسب وبعضها يطفو عليه ( ثم) نقول : بعد تسليم وجود النار في الجملة (لم يقم دليل على وجود كرة النار عند المحيط) كما زعمتم ( وإنما المشاهد استحالات تحدث لبعض الأجسام) إلى النار ) كما عند الإبراد والإحراق ) . لا يقال : الشهب دالة على وجودها لأنا نقول : جاز أن يكون هناك هواء حار يقتضي استحالة الأدخنة المرتفعة إلى النار فلا يثبت وجود كرتها ( وإن سلم وجود كرة النار ( فما الدليل على أن البسيط منها يصعب تشكله حتى نثبت يبوسة النار ؟ ( وهل إلى ذلك طريق إلا التجربة ؟ وكيف تتصور (التجربة فيها ؟ و أما (إفناؤها الرطوبات ) عن الأجسام فلا يدل على كونها يابسة في جوهرها لأنه ( إفناء للأجزاء المائية ) التي هي رطبة بمعنى البلة ) ولا دليل فيه على اليبوسة) الطبيعية ( فإن الهواء أيضاً يعمل ذلك ) الإفناء مع أنه رطب الجوهر ( فإن قلت ذلك ) أي إفناء الهواء للرطوبات عن الأجسام إنما هو لما فيه من أجزاء نارية قلنا فيجب أن لا يكون الهواء البارد فاعلاً لذلك ) إذ لا يتصور فيه الأجزاء النارية مع أنه يفني الرطوبة ويجفف الثوب المبلول ( وبالجملة فلا يمكن القطع به أي بأن إفناء الرطوبة بمعنى البلة يدل على يبوسة المفني في ذاته موجود بدونها كما في الهواء . ( وعليكم الدليل ( الموجب للقطع به وكيف) يقطع به ( وشعاع الشمس يفعل ذلك مع أنه لا يوصف ( في نفسه ( بحر ولا يبوسة ولا غيرهما من الكيفيات ثم لا نسلم أن الهواء (حار بل هو بارد بطبعه، ( وإنما يستفاد أشعة الشمس المنعكسة إليه من الأرض. فلذلك كلما كان الهواء ( أرفع ) وأبعد عن الأرض ) كان أقل حراً لضعف الانعكاس إليه . وهكذا كلما زاد ارتفاعه قل حره وظهر برده ) حتى يصير زمهريراً) في غاية البرودة. (فلم قلتم : أن ذلك البرد الشديد في الهواء ليس) له بالطبع ) بل لمخالطة الأجزاء الرشية المائية التي عادت إلي برودتها الطبيعية ولم يصل إليها أثر الانعكاس؟ ولا نسلم أيضاً أنه رطب فإنكم اتفقتم على أن مخالطة الرطب باليابس تفيده استمساكاً) عن التشتت ) والهواء ليس كذلك فإن الأجزاء الترابية لا تستمسك بمخالطته. ( ثم لا نسلم أن طبيعة الماء الجمود ولو كان كذلك كان باطن الماء بالانجماد أحرى من ظاهره فظاهر) عند العاقل ( أن جموده ببرد الهواء)، المجاور له ( فالبارد بالطبع ) هو ( الهواء و) أما (الماء ) فإنه بطبعه ( لا) بارد ولا حار وكيف تجمعون بين قولكم طبيعته الجمود مع القول برطوبته . فإن قلتم : ( لا منافاة بين القولين ( لأنه سهل التشكل ) في نفسه ( إذ يكفي في ذوبانه ) الذي تظهر معه السهولة ( أدنى سبب ) من الحرارة فمثل هذا الجمود لا ينافي الرطوبة الجوهرية ( قلنا : ( هذا باطل قطعاً إذ مع الجمود الذي . (قلنا :) هو مقتضى طبعه لا سهولة له وذوبانه المستلزم لها مستنداً إلى أمر خارج، ولئن نزلنا عن هذا المقام . قلنا : ( فلم قلتم أن سائر العناصر كالأرض ( ليس كذلك ) أي قابلاً للذوبان بأدنى سبب من الأسباب . ( غاية ما في الباب أن تلك الأسباب لما قل وقوعها أو لم تقع ) أصلاً ( لم تقف عليها وعدم الوجدان لا يدل على العدم وحينئذ جاز أن تكون الأرض رطبة .

قوله : ( التي هي الرطوبة بمعنى البلة قد مر قبيل مباحث الاعتماد أن الرطب هو الذي يكون صورته النوعية مقتضية لكيفية الرطوبة وهي سهولة الالتصاق والانفصال، والمبتل هو الذي التصق بظاهرة ذلك الجسم الرطب، فالهواء يعني رطوبة الثوب التي هي البلة لا رطوبة الماء، تدبر .

قوله : (لا يوصف في نفسه بحر إلخ وإنما قيده بقوله : في نفسه لأن الكلام في الرطوبة واليبوسة الطبيعيتين كما لا يخفى .

قوله : (لو كان كذلك كان باطن الماء بالانجماد أخرى فإنه لو كان طبيعية الماء الجمود يلزم انجماد أعماق الغدران العظيمة، لا سيما في الشتاء يكون منجمداً في كثير من المواضع فيلزم أن يكون باطنها منجمداً بالطريق الأولى . اللهم إلا أن يقال : عدم انجماد باطنها إنما هو لعارض لكن نحن في صدد المنع وما ذكرناه هاهنا سنداً للمنع .
First Observatory - Third Section: First Objective: The statement of the philosophers about the divisions of the elements and their states according to their differences in ranks: some sink in water downwards, some dive in it and do not sink, and some float on it. Then we say: after conceding the existence of fire in general, no proof has been established for the existence of the sphere of fire at the periphery as you claimed. Rather, what is observed are transformations that occur to some bodies into fire, as in cases of cooling and heating. It should not be said that meteors indicate its existence, because we say: it is possible that there is hot air that necessitates the transformation of rising vapors into fire, so its sphere is not proven. And if the existence of the sphere of fire is conceded, what is the proof that its simple part is difficult to shape so that we may affirm the dryness of fire? Is there any way to that except experiment? And how can experiment be imagined in it? As for its consuming the moistures from bodies, it does not indicate that it is dry in its essence, because it is a consumption of watery parts that are moist in the sense of wetness, and there is no proof in it for natural dryness. Indeed, air also does that consumption despite being moist in essence. If you say that the air's consumption of moistures from bodies is due to the fiery parts in it, we say: then cold air must not be an agent of that, since fiery parts are not conceivable in it, yet it consumes moisture and dries a wet garment. In summary, it is not possible to be certain that consuming moisture in the sense of wetness indicates the dryness of the consuming agent in its essence, just as in air. And upon you is the proof necessitating certainty. How can one be certain, while the ray of the sun does that despite not being described in itself as hot or dry or any other qualities? Then we do not concede that air is hot; rather, it is cold by nature. It only benefits from the sun's rays reflected from the earth. Therefore, the higher and farther from the earth the air is, the less heat it has due to the weakness of reflection. And thus the more its altitude increases, the less its heat and the more its coldness appears, until it becomes extremely cold (zamharīr). So why do you say that that severe cold in the air is not natural, but due to the mixing of watery moist parts that have returned to their natural coldness and have not been reached by the effect of reflection? And we also do not concede that it is moist, because you have agreed that mixing the moist with the dry gives it cohesion against dispersion, and air is not like that, because earthy parts do not cohere by mixing with it. Then we do not concede that the nature of water is freezing. If it were so, then the interior of water would be more deserving of freezing than its surface. It is obvious to a rational person that its freezing is due to the cold of the air adjacent to it. So the naturally cold is air, while water is by nature neither cold nor hot. How can you combine your statement that its nature is freezing with the statement that it is moist? If you say: there is no contradiction between the two statements because it is easily shapeable in itself, since a minimal cause of heat suffices for its melting, so such freezing does not contradict essential moisture, we say: this is absolutely false, because with freezing that is the requirement of its nature, it has no ease, and its melting which necessitates ease is dependent on an external cause. And if we descend from this position, we say: why do you say that other elements like earth are not like that, i.e., capable of melting with a minimal cause? The utmost is that those causes are rare or have not occurred, so we have not come across them, and non-observation does not indicate non-existence. Then it is possible that earth is moist.

Statement: (the moisture in the sense of wetness) has passed before the discussions of adhesion that the moist is that whose specific form necessitates the quality of moisture, which is ease of adhesion and separation, and the wet is that which has adhered to the surface of that moist body. So the air means the moisture of the garment which is wetness, not the moisture of water. Reflect.

Statement: (it is not described in itself as hot etc.) He restricted it by saying 'in itself' because the discussion is about natural moisture and dryness, as is not hidden.

Statement: (if it were so, the interior of water would be more deserving of freezing) because if the nature of water were freezing, it would necessitate the freezing of the depths of great ponds, especially in winter, they would be frozen in many places, so it would necessitate that their interior be frozen a fortiori. Unless it is said that the non-freezing of their interior is due to an accident, but we are in the context of denial, and what we have mentioned here is a support for the denial.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claims about the elements, specifically the existence of a sphere of fire, the dryness of fire, and the nature of water. The author begins by denying that there is proof for a sphere of fire surrounding the earth, arguing that observed transformations into fire (e.g., by cooling or heating) do not necessitate such a sphere. He dismisses the argument from meteors by suggesting hot air could cause the transformation. Even if the sphere were conceded, he challenges the proof that fire is dry (yābisa), noting that experiment is impossible for such a pure substance. He then attacks the argument that fire consumes moisture, pointing out that air and sunlight also dry things without being dry themselves. He denies that air is hot by nature, explaining that its heat comes from reflected sunlight, and higher air is colder. He also denies that air is moist, because mixing with moist should give cohesion, but air does not give cohesion to earthy parts. Turning to water, he denies that its nature is freezing (jumūd), arguing that if it were, the interior of water would freeze more readily than the surface, which does not happen; freezing is caused by cold air. He rejects the claim that water is both moist and freezing, as freezing requires rigidity while moisture implies ease of shaping. The opponent's counter that freezing does not contradict essential moisture because minimal heat melts it is dismissed as false: if freezing is natural, it cannot be easily melted. Finally, he suggests that earth might be moist, since non-observation of its melting does not prove impossibility. The footnotes clarify definitions: moisture (ruṭūba) as ease of adhesion and separation, and wetness (balla) as superficial adhesion; and they explain the reasoning about freezing depths.

Key Terms
كرة النار
يبوسة
التجربة
الرطوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An object that dries other things must be inherently dry.
Correction: Air and sunlight dry things without being dry in essence, so consumption of moisture is not proof of dryness.

Checkpoint
What example does Sanusi use to refute the claim that consuming moisture proves dryness?
A) Ice
B) Air
C) Earth
D) Water

Key Takeaway
Fire's consumption of moisture does not prove its inherent dryness; air and sunlight perform the same function without being dry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You observe that fire dries things, and the philosophers concluded it is dry in itself. What assumption about your own character have you made based on outward actions, without examining the deeper reality? Will you question it today?
?
Shua' al-shams (sunlight) dries without being hot or dry. What in your life appears to cause a good effect, yet its inner state is different from what you assume? Are you willing to see with new eyes?
?
The philosophers were certain about the sphere of fire, but their proof collapsed. Is there a belief about your own righteousness or the state of others that you hold without solid proof? What would it take for you to surrender that certainty to truth?

The Nature of Air: Temperature and Moisture
Sanusi denies that air is hot and moist by nature, and refutes the claim that water's nature is freezing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the philosophers' claims that air is hot and moist, and his argument that cold air causes water to freeze.

Core Idea
Air is naturally cold and not moist; its heat comes from reflected sunlight, and it does not give cohesion to earthy parts.

In this section, Sanusi challenges the philosophers' assertions about air. He denies that air is hot by nature, explaining that its heat is derived from the sun's reflection and that higher air is colder. He also denies that air is moist, because moistness should provide cohesion, but air does not hold earthy parts together. Finally, he addresses the freezing of water, arguing that cold air is the cause, not a natural property of water.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on air and water
Read the Arabic passage focusing on Sanusi's arguments about air's temperature and moisture, and his refutation of water's freezing nature.
Pay attention to the logical structure: denial of heat, denial of moisture, then discussion of freezing.
2
Understand the argument about air's heat
Sanusi claims air is naturally cold and becomes hot only through reflected sunlight; higher air is colder.
He uses the example of extremely cold upper air (zamharīr) to support his point.
3
Analyze the denial of moisture
Sanusi argues that moisture should provide cohesion, but air does not cause earthy parts to cohere, so it cannot be naturally moist.
This is based on the agreed principle that mixing moist with dry gives cohesion.
4
Examine the freezing of water
Sanusi refutes the claim that water's nature is freezing; if it were, interior water would freeze first, but it freezes due to cold air.
He also denies the philosophers' reconciliation of moisture and freezing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وعليكم الدليل ( الموجب للقطع به وكيف) يقطع به ( وشعاع الشمس يفعل ذلك مع أنه لا يوصف ( في نفسه ( بحر ولا يبوسة ولا غيرهما من الكيفيات ثم لا نسلم أن الهواء (حار بل هو بارد بطبعه، ( وإنما يستفاد أشعة الشمس المنعكسة إليه من الأرض. فلذلك كلما كان الهواء ( أرفع ) وأبعد عن الأرض ) كان أقل حراً لضعف الانعكاس إليه . وهكذا كلما زاد ارتفاعه قل حره وظهر برده ) حتى يصير زمهريراً) في غاية البرودة. ( ثم لا نسلم أن طبيعة الماء الجمود ولو كان كذلك كان باطن الماء بالانجماد أحرى من ظاهره فظاهر) عند العاقل ( أن جموده ببرد الهواء)، المجاور له ( فالبارد بالطبع ) هو ( الهواء و) أما (الماء ) فإنه بطبعه ( لا) بارد ولا حار وكيف تجمعون بين قولكم طبيعته الجمود مع القول برطوبته . فإن قلتم : ( لا منافاة بين القولين ( لأنه سهل التشكل ) في نفسه ( إذ يكفي في ذوبانه ) الذي تظهر معه السهولة ( أدنى سبب ) من الحرارة فمثل هذا الجمود لا ينافي الرطوبة الجوهرية ( قلنا : ( هذا باطل قطعاً إذ مع الجمود الذي . (قلنا :) هو مقتضى طبعه لا سهولة له وذوبانه المستلزم لها مستنداً إلى أمر خارج، ولئن نزلنا عن هذا المقام . قلنا : ( فلم قلتم أن سائر العناصر كالأرض ( ليس كذلك ) أي قابلاً للذوبان بأدنى سبب من الأسباب . ( غاية ما في الباب أن تلك الأسباب لما قل وقوعها أو لم تقع ) أصلاً ( لم تقف عليها وعدم الوجدان لا يدل على العدم وحينئذ جاز أن تكون الأرض رطبة .

قوله : ( التي هي الرطوبة بمعنى البلة قد مر قبيل مباحث الاعتماد أن الرطب هو الذي يكون صورته النوعية مقتضية لكيفية الرطوبة وهي سهولة الالتصاق والانفصال، والمبتل هو الذي التصق بظاهرة ذلك الجسم الرطب، فالهواء يعني رطوبة الثوب التي هي البلة لا رطوبة الماء، تدبر .

قوله : (لا يوصف في نفسه بحر إلخ وإنما قيده بقوله : في نفسه لأن الكلام في الرطوبة واليبوسة الطبيعيتين كما لا يخفى .

قوله : (لو كان كذلك كان باطن الماء بالانجماد أخرى فإنه لو كان طبيعية الماء الجمود يلزم انجماد أعماق الغدران العظيمة، لا سيما في الشتاء يكون منجمداً في كثير من المواضع فيلزم أن يكون باطنها منجمداً بالطريق الأولى . اللهم إلا أن يقال : عدم انجماد باطنها إنما هو لعارض لكن نحن في صدد المنع وما ذكرناه هاهنا سنداً للمنع .
First Observatory - Third Section: First Objective: The statement of the philosophers about the divisions of the elements and their states according to their differences in ranks: some sink in water downwards, some dive in it and do not sink, and some float on it. Then we say: after conceding the existence of fire in general, no proof has been established for the existence of the sphere of fire at the periphery as you claimed. Rather, what is observed are transformations that occur to some bodies into fire, as in cases of cooling and heating. It should not be said that meteors indicate its existence, because we say: it is possible that there is hot air that necessitates the transformation of rising vapors into fire, so its sphere is not proven. And if the existence of the sphere of fire is conceded, what is the proof that its simple part is difficult to shape so that we may affirm the dryness of fire? Is there any way to that except experiment? And how can experiment be imagined in it? As for its consuming the moistures from bodies, it does not indicate that it is dry in its essence, because it is a consumption of watery parts that are moist in the sense of wetness, and there is no proof in it for natural dryness. Indeed, air also does that consumption despite being moist in essence. If you say that the air's consumption of moistures from bodies is due to the fiery parts in it, we say: then cold air must not be an agent of that, since fiery parts are not conceivable in it, yet it consumes moisture and dries a wet garment. In summary, it is not possible to be certain that consuming moisture in the sense of wetness indicates the dryness of the consuming agent in its essence, just as in air. And upon you is the proof necessitating certainty. How can one be certain, while the ray of the sun does that despite not being described in itself as hot or dry or any other qualities? Then we do not concede that air is hot; rather, it is cold by nature. It only benefits from the sun's rays reflected from the earth. Therefore, the higher and farther from the earth the air is, the less heat it has due to the weakness of reflection. And thus the more its altitude increases, the less its heat and the more its coldness appears, until it becomes extremely cold (zamharīr). So why do you say that that severe cold in the air is not natural, but due to the mixing of watery moist parts that have returned to their natural coldness and have not been reached by the effect of reflection? And we also do not concede that it is moist, because you have agreed that mixing the moist with the dry gives it cohesion against dispersion, and air is not like that, because earthy parts do not cohere by mixing with it. Then we do not concede that the nature of water is freezing. If it were so, then the interior of water would be more deserving of freezing than its surface. It is obvious to a rational person that its freezing is due to the cold of the air adjacent to it. So the naturally cold is air, while water is by nature neither cold nor hot. How can you combine your statement that its nature is freezing with the statement that it is moist? If you say: there is no contradiction between the two statements because it is easily shapeable in itself, since a minimal cause of heat suffices for its melting, so such freezing does not contradict essential moisture, we say: this is absolutely false, because with freezing that is the requirement of its nature, it has no ease, and its melting which necessitates ease is dependent on an external cause. And if we descend from this position, we say: why do you say that other elements like earth are not like that, i.e., capable of melting with a minimal cause? The utmost is that those causes are rare or have not occurred, so we have not come across them, and non-observation does not indicate non-existence. Then it is possible that earth is moist.

Statement: (the moisture in the sense of wetness) has passed before the discussions of adhesion that the moist is that whose specific form necessitates the quality of moisture, which is ease of adhesion and separation, and the wet is that which has adhered to the surface of that moist body. So the air means the moisture of the garment which is wetness, not the moisture of water. Reflect.

Statement: (it is not described in itself as hot etc.) He restricted it by saying 'in itself' because the discussion is about natural moisture and dryness, as is not hidden.

Statement: (if it were so, the interior of water would be more deserving of freezing) because if the nature of water were freezing, it would necessitate the freezing of the depths of great ponds, especially in winter, they would be frozen in many places, so it would necessitate that their interior be frozen a fortiori. Unless it is said that the non-freezing of their interior is due to an accident, but we are in the context of denial, and what we have mentioned here is a support for the denial.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claims about the elements, specifically the existence of a sphere of fire, the dryness of fire, and the nature of water. The author begins by denying that there is proof for a sphere of fire surrounding the earth, arguing that observed transformations into fire (e.g., by cooling or heating) do not necessitate such a sphere. He dismisses the argument from meteors by suggesting hot air could cause the transformation. Even if the sphere were conceded, he challenges the proof that fire is dry (yābisa), noting that experiment is impossible for such a pure substance. He then attacks the argument that fire consumes moisture, pointing out that air and sunlight also dry things without being dry themselves. He denies that air is hot by nature, explaining that its heat comes from reflected sunlight, and higher air is colder. He also denies that air is moist, because mixing with moist should give cohesion, but air does not give cohesion to earthy parts. Turning to water, he denies that its nature is freezing (jumūd), arguing that if it were, the interior of water would freeze more readily than the surface, which does not happen; freezing is caused by cold air. He rejects the claim that water is both moist and freezing, as freezing requires rigidity while moisture implies ease of shaping. The opponent's counter that freezing does not contradict essential moisture because minimal heat melts it is dismissed as false: if freezing is natural, it cannot be easily melted. Finally, he suggests that earth might be moist, since non-observation of its melting does not prove impossibility. The footnotes clarify definitions: moisture (ruṭūba) as ease of adhesion and separation, and wetness (balla) as superficial adhesion; and they explain the reasoning about freezing depths.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Water's nature is to freeze, and air is naturally hot and moist.
Correction: Sanusi argues that air is naturally cold and not moist, and water's freezing is caused by external cold air, not its nature.

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's main argument against the claim that water's nature is freezing?
A) Water never freezes naturally.
B) The interior of water freezes first if freezing were natural.
C) Water is naturally hot.
D) Freezing is caused by the sun.

Key Takeaway
Air is naturally cold and not moist; water's freezing is caused by cold air, not its own nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely assume many things about the world without questioning their basis. After seeing Sanusi challenge the 'obvious' nature of air and water, what is one deeply held belief about your own nature or habits that you have never examined? Will you investigate its true source today?
?
Sanusi argues that the heat in air comes from an external source, not from itself. Where do you find your spiritual warmth, energy, and passion? Do you attribute it to your own nature, or do you recognize it as a gift from Allah, and how will you remind yourself of this dependence in your next moment of need?
?
The philosophers claimed water's freezing was natural, but Sanusi showed it was caused by external cold. What external influences are causing you to 'freeze'—to become spiritually cold or stagnant—and are you mistakenly blaming your own nature? How can you change the environment instead of resigning yourself?

The Possibility of Earth's Moisture
Sanusi argues that earth might be moist, as non-observation does not prove impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that earth might be moist, as non-observation of its melting does not prove impossibility.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence; earth could be moist even if we do not observe its melting.

In the final part of this section, Sanusi extends the discussion to earth. He challenges the philosophers' assumption that earth is not easily melted or shaped by a minimal cause. He argues that just because we do not observe such melting does not mean it is impossible; non-observation does not indicate non-existence. Therefore, it is possible that earth is moist. This argument highlights a key methodological principle in kalam: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on earth
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on Sanusi's argument about earth's potential moisture.
This is a short but important methodological point.
2
Understand the principle of non-observation
Sanusi states that just because we have not observed earth melting or being shaped easily does not mean it is impossible; rarity of occurrence does not prove non-existence.
This is a key logical principle in theology.
3
Relate to the broader discussion
Consider how this principle applies to the previous arguments about fire, air, and water.
It reinforces the idea that the philosophers' claims are based on insufficient evidence.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claims about the elements, specifically the existence of a sphere of fire, the dryness of fire, and the nature of water. The author begins by denying that there is proof for a sphere of fire surrounding the earth, arguing that observed transformations into fire (e.g., by cooling or heating) do not necessitate such a sphere. He dismisses the argument from meteors by suggesting hot air could cause the transformation. Even if the sphere were conceded, he challenges the proof that fire is dry (yābisa), noting that experiment is impossible for such a pure substance. He then attacks the argument that fire consumes moisture, pointing out that air and sunlight also dry things without being dry themselves. He denies that air is hot by nature, explaining that its heat comes from reflected sunlight, and higher air is colder. He also denies that air is moist, because mixing with moist should give cohesion, but air does not give cohesion to earthy parts. Turning to water, he denies that its nature is freezing (jumūd), arguing that if it were, the interior of water would freeze more readily than the surface, which does not happen; freezing is caused by cold air. He rejects the claim that water is both moist and freezing, as freezing requires rigidity while moisture implies ease of shaping. The opponent's counter that freezing does not contradict essential moisture because minimal heat melts it is dismissed as false: if freezing is natural, it cannot be easily melted. Finally, he suggests that earth might be moist, since non-observation of its melting does not prove impossibility. The footnotes clarify definitions: moisture (ruṭūba) as ease of adhesion and separation, and wetness (balla) as superficial adhesion; and they explain the reasoning about freezing depths.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Sanusi shows that non-observation does not prove non-existence; earth might still be moist.

Checkpoint
What principle does Sanusi use to argue that earth might be moist?
A) Earth is naturally moist.
B) Non-observation does not indicate non-existence.
C) All elements are essentially water.
D) Experiment proves earth melts.

Key Takeaway
Non-observation does not prove non-existence; earth could be moist even if we do not observe its melting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an aspect of your faith that you struggle with because you haven't 'seen' it. Are you demanding sensory proof for something that can only be known through revelation? Would you reject a truth simply because you haven't observed it? What might you be missing?
?
Sanusi warns against assuming impossibility from lack of observation. In your life, have you dismissed a potential blessing or answer to du'a because it seemed unlikely or you hadn't seen it happen before? How can you train yourself to remain open to possibilities that are rare but real?
?
The deepest implication of this refutation is that the unseen realm is not impossible just because we don't see it. Do you live as if the Angels, the Day of Judgment, and Allah's mercy are as real as the objects in front of you? What would change today if you truly internalized that non-observation does not equal non-existence?

Proof of Earth's Sphericity from Lunar Eclipses
Proving earth sphericity via eclipse time differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observations of lunar eclipses at different longitudes demonstrate the spherical shape of the earth.

Core Idea
The proportional delay in eclipse times across longitudes proves the earth is a sphere.

This passage presents a classical astronomical proof for the earth's sphericity. Using the example of a lunar eclipse observed simultaneously in eastern and western locations, the author shows that the consistent time difference can only be explained by the curvature of the earth. The proof is based on the proportional delay in celestial events along the east-west axis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the technical terms for longitude and latitude.
2
Identify the observational data
The author uses a specific example: a lunar eclipse observed in an eastern country at the end of the night, and in a western country 1000 miles away an hour earlier. Find this in the text.
3
Understand the reasoning
The key claim is that this proportional time delay is only possible on a sphere. Reflect on why a flat earth would not produce such a uniform delay.
Consider how the curvature of the sphere causes the delay.
4
Review the footnote
The footnote clarifies 'agreeing in latitude' and defines the equator. Understand that the observation is for places on the same latitude.
The equator is the great circle perpendicular to the axis.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the core argument.
The question focuses on the phenomenon used as proof.

Source Text

Second Objective: Proving the Sphericity of the Earth. They claimed that the earth is spherical, at least in longitude (i.e., between east and west), because the countries agreeing in latitude or having no latitude, the closer they are to the west, the later the rise of the sun and all the stars upon them by a uniform proportion, and likewise for setting. And that delay in rising and setting in that proportion is not intelligible except in a sphere. We only say that delay because when we observed a specific eclipse at a time of night, we found it in eastern countries, for example, at the end of the night, and we found it in western countries distant from the first by a defined distance of one thousand miles, an hour before that, and we found it in...

Statement: ('the countries agreeing in latitude') i.e., the latitude of those countries from the equator. And the equator is the great circle on the surrounding surface of the earth, and it is the circle in the plane of the celestial equator, as will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the earth's sphericity based on astronomical observations. The author states the claim that the earth is spherical, at least in the east-west direction (longitude). The proof is empirical: for places at the same latitude, the times of sunrise and sunset differ proportionally to their longitudinal distance. This regular delay is only explicable if the earth is a sphere. The specific evidence comes from observing a lunar eclipse at a known time: in an eastern country it is seen at the end of the night, while in a western country 1000 miles away it is seen an hour earlier. This time difference corresponds to the curvature of the earth. The footnote clarifies that 'agreeing in latitude' means the same distance from the equator, and defines the equator as the great circle on the earth's surface corresponding to the celestial equator.

Refuting the Claim that Water is Naturally Solid
Refutation of natural solidity argument and defense of earth's humidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the claim that water is naturally solid and how he defends the possibility of earth's moisture.

Core Idea
Solidity is an accidental property of water, not its essential nature.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical refutation of an opponent who claims that water's natural state is solidity (جمود) and that earth is inherently dry. The author uses logical arguments to show that solidity is caused by external factors, not by water's essence, and extends the reasoning to challenge the opponent's view on earth. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial set-up
The author begins by referring to dispersion (التشتت) and contrasts air with earth: earthy particles do not adhere in air. This sets the stage for the discussion on water's nature.
The reference to 'التشتت' may be a continuation from a previous passage.
2
Examine the main argument against natural solidity
The author denies that water's nature is solidity. He argues that if it were naturally solid, the interior would freeze before the surface, but in fact freezing is caused by cold air. Thus, cold is an attribute of air, not water.
This is a key logical argument: an essential property would manifest uniformly from within.
3
Follow the opponent's attempted reconciliation
The opponent tries to reconcile solidity and moisture by claiming water is 'readily configurable' (سهل التشكل) and melting from minimal heat does not negate essential moisture.
This is a common dialectical move: the opponent attempts to show that two seemingly contradictory properties can coexist.
4
See the author's decisive refutation
The author responds that if solidity were from nature, there would be no ease (سهولة) in melting; melting would require an external cause. Hence the opponent's position is 'absolutely false.'
The author emphasizes that ease is incompatible with natural solidity.
5
Consider the extension to earth
The author extends the argument to earth: just because we do not observe earth melting easily does not prove it is not potentially moist. Non-observation is not proof of non-existence.
This uses a principle of logic: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التشتت) والهواء ليس كذلك فإن الأجزاء الترابية لا تستمسك بمخالطته. ( ثم لا نسلم أن طبيعة الماء الجمود ولو كان كذلك كان باطن الماء بالانجماد أحرى من ظاهره فظاهر) عند العاقل ( أن جموده ببرد الهواء)، المجاور له ( فالبارد بالطبع ) هو ( الهواء و) أما (الماء ) فإنه بطبعه ( لا) بارد ولا حار وكيف تجمعون بين قولكم طبيعته الجمود مع القول برطوبته . فإن قلتم : ( لا منافاة بين القولين ( لأنه سهل التشكل ) في نفسه ( إذ يكفي في ذوبانه ) الذي تظهر معه السهولة ( أدنى سبب ) من الحرارة فمثل هذا الجمود لا ينافي الرطوبة الجوهرية ( قلنا : ( هذا باطل قطعاً إذ مع الجمود الذي . [The dispersion] and air is not like that, for the earthy particles do not adhere by mixing with it. (Then we do not accept that the nature of water is solidity; and if it were so, then the interior of the water would be more deserving of freezing than its exterior, so it is apparent) to the rational person (that its freezing is due to the cold of the air) adjoining it. (So the cold by nature) is (the air, and) as for (the water), it is by its nature (not) cold nor hot. And how do you reconcile your statement that its nature is solidity with the statement of its moisture? If you say: (There is no contradiction between the two statements) because it is (readily configurable) in itself, (since a minimal cause) of heat (suffices for its melting) by which the ease appears, so such solidity does not contradict essential moisture, (we say: (This is absolutely false, for with solidity that is) (we say:) is required by its nature, it has no ease, and its melting, which necessitates ease, is based on an external cause. And if we descend from this position, we say: (Then why do you say that the other elements, like earth, are not like that?) i.e., susceptible to melting from the minimal of causes. (The most that can be said is that those causes, since their occurrence is rare or did not occur) at all, (we did not come to know them, and non-observation does not indicate non-existence, and then it is permissible for the earth to be moist.)

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of an opponent's argument. The opponent claims that water is naturally solid (جمود) and that earth is dry, not moist. The author begins by noting that air does not have the same dispersive property as earth. Then he argues that if water were naturally solid, its interior would freeze more readily than its surface, but in reality, freezing is caused by cold air. He denies that water is inherently cold or hot. He challenges the opponent's reconciliation of solidity and moisture, stating that if they claim 'easy configuration' allows solidity without contradicting moisture, he responds that true solidity from nature implies no ease, and melting requires an external cause. Finally, he extends the argument to earth, saying that just because we do not observe earth melting easily does not prove it is not potentially moist; non-observation is not proof of non-existence.

Proof of Earth's Sphericity from Lunar Eclipses and Pole Star Altitude
The author proves Earth's sphericity using empirical observations of lunar eclipses and pole star altitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observed proportional delays of lunar eclipses and changes in pole star altitude demonstrate that the Earth is spherical.

Core Idea
Eclipses and star altitudes reveal Earth's curvature through consistent proportional changes.

The author presents empirical evidence for the Earth's spherical shape. By examining the timing of a lunar eclipse across cities at different longitudes, and the altitude of the pole star at different latitudes, he shows that only a spherical Earth can explain the observed patterns. This lesson walks through these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument.
2
Understand the longitude proof
Focus on the lunar eclipse example: how the same eclipse is observed at different local times in cities separated by known distances. Notice the proportional delay.
3
Understand the latitude proof
Note how the pole star altitude increases as you travel north, and the visible stars change. This indicates a curved surface along the north-south axis.
4
Consider the combination
The author briefly discusses how traveling in an oblique direction combines both effects. Reflect on why this strengthens the overall proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعموا أن الأرض كروية أما في الطول ( أي فيما بين المشرق والمغرب (فلأن البلاد المتوافقة في العرض أو التي لا عرض لها ) كلما كانت أقرب إلى الغرب كان طلوع الشمس وسائر الكواكب عليها متأخراً بنسبة واحدة وكذا الحال في الغروب ) ولا يعقل ذلك ) التأخر في الطلوع والغروب بتلك النسبة إلا في الكرة وإنما قلنا : بذلك ) التأخر ( لأنا لما رصدنا خسوفا بعينه في وقت من الليل وجدناه في بلاد شرقية مثلاً آخر الليل و ) وجدناه ( في بلاد غربية عنها ) أي عن البلاد الأولى بمسافة معينة) هي ألف ميل ( قبله ) أي قبل آخر الليل ( بساعة و) وجدناه (في بلاد أخرى ( غربية عنها أي عن البلاد الثانية ( بتلك المسافة بعينها قبل الأول بساعتين وقبل الثاني بساعة ) . والحاصل أنه يوجد في هذه البلاد الأخرى قبل آخر الليل بساعتين ) وعلى هذا القياس (فعلمنا أن (طلوعها أي طلوع الشمس على الغربية متأخر) بنسبة واحدة لأن الخسوف المعين كان في البلاد الأولى عند طلوع الشمس وفي الثانية قبله بساعة وفي الثالثة قبله بساعتين . ( وأما في العرض) أي فيما بين الشمال والجنوب ( فلان السالك في الشمال كلما أوغل فيه ازداد القطب ارتفاعاً عليه ) بحسب إيغاله فيه على نسبة واحدة ) حتى يصير بحيث يراه قريباً من سمت رأسه ولذلك تظهر له الكواكب الشمالية) التي كانت مختفية عنه، وتخفى عنه) الكواكب ( الجنوبية) التي كانت ظاهرة عليه ( والسالك ) الواغل (في الجنوب بالعكس من ذلك وأما فيما بينهما ) أي بين الطول والعرض ( فلتركب الأمرين) فإن السالك فيما بين المشرق والشمال يتقدم عليه الطلوع بمقدار قربه من المشرق ويزداد ارتفاع القطب عليه بمقدار ولوغه في الشمال. وقس على هذا حال السالك فيما بين المغرب والشمال وحال السالك في السمتين المقابلين لهما
They claimed that the earth is spherical. As for in longitude (i.e., between east and west), (because the cities that agree in latitude or have no latitude) whenever they are closer to the west, the rising of the sun and other stars upon them is delayed by a single proportion, and similarly in the setting. And that) delay in rising and setting by that proportion is not conceivable except in a sphere. And we only said: that) delay (because when we observed a specific eclipse at a time of night, we found it in an eastern city, for example, at the end of the night, and) we found it (in a western city at a certain distance from it) i.e., from the first city (of a thousand miles) before it (i.e., before the end of the night) by an hour, and) we found it (in another city) western from it, i.e., from the second city (at that same distance before the first by two hours and before the second by one hour). In summary, it exists in these other cities two hours before the end of the night, and on this analogy, we know that (its rising, i.e., the sun's rising on the western city, is delayed) by a single proportion, because that specific eclipse was in the first city at sunrise, in the second an hour before it, and in the third two hours before it. (And as for in latitude) i.e., between north and south, (because the traveler in the north, whenever he penetrates into it, the pole star increases in altitude upon him) according to his penetration by a single proportion, until it becomes such that he sees it near his zenith. Therefore, the northern stars that were hidden from him appear, and the southern stars that were visible to him become hidden. (And the traveler) penetrating (in the south is the opposite of that. As for that which is between them) i.e., between longitude and latitude, (it is due to the combination of the two things). For the traveler between east and north, the rising occurs earlier according to his proximity to the east, and the altitude of the pole star increases according to his penetration into the north. Measure similarly the case of the traveler between west and north, and the case of the traveler in the two opposite directions.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the Earth's sphericity using two types of empirical evidence. The author first argues for sphericity along longitude (east-west). He states that cities with the same latitude experience a proportionate delay in sunrise and sunset as one moves westward. This is demonstrated by observing a specific lunar eclipse: at the same absolute time, the eclipse is seen at different local times in cities separated by known distances (e.g., 1000 miles). In the easternmost city it occurs at the end of the night; in a city 1000 miles west, it occurs one hour before the end of the night; in a city another 1000 miles west, two hours before. Since the delay is proportional to distance, this can only happen on a spherical surface. Second, along latitude (north-south), the altitude of the pole star increases as one travels north, and northern stars become visible while southern stars disappear. This is consistent with a curved surface. Third, combining both dimensions, the author notes that traveling in an oblique direction (e.g., northeast) combines both effects: earlier sunrise due to eastward movement and increased pole star altitude due to northward movement. The author concludes by instructing the reader to generalize to opposite directions.

العرض
الخسوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single observation of a lunar eclipse is enough to prove Earth's sphericity.
Correction: The proof relies on multiple observations across different longitudes showing a proportional delay; a single observation cannot distinguish shape.

Checkpoint

C) The sun rises later in the east.
D) The earth is flat but with variable time zones.

Key Takeaway
Consistent proportional changes in eclipse timings and star altitudes across the Earth's surface can only be explained by a spherical shape.

Answering the Mountain Objection to Sphericity
The author answers the objection that mountains disprove sphericity with a scale analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sphericity of the Earth can be reconciled with mountains and valleys through a powerful scale analogy.

Core Idea
Mountains are like tiny bumps on a very large ball – they don't break sphericity.

Some skeptics point to the Earth's uneven surface as proof that it cannot be round. The classical response, presented here, uses a striking analogy to show that small irregularities do not negate overall shape. Let's examine how this brief but powerful answer refutes the objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening line where the objection about surface irregularities is mentioned. Note that it's a natural question: how can the Earth be round when we see mountains?
2
Examine the answer analogy
The answer compares mountains to small bumps on a large sphere. Consider the scale: the ratio of a mountain to the Earth is like a fraction of a barley grain's width to a cubit-wide sphere.
This analogy shows that even the highest mountain is negligible in size compared to the whole Earth.
3
Appreciate the logic
The argument is that sphericity is a global property, not contradicted by local irregularities. Small deviations do not disprove the overall shape.
This is a standard scientific reasoning approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد عليهم الاختلاف الذي في سطحها فأجابوا عنه : ( بأنه كتضاريس صغيرة على كرة كبيرة فلا يقدح في أصل الكروية ( الحسية المعلومة بما ذكر ) فإن أعظم جبل على وجه الأرض نسبته إليها كخمس سبع عرض شعيرة على كرة قطرها ذراع . والصحيح كما أن يقال: فإن جبلاً يرتفع نصف فرسخ إلى آخر أو يحذف لفظ الخمس مر قوله : ( أو يحذف إلخ ) فإن أعظم جبل نسبته إلى الأرض نسبة سبع عرض شعيرة إلى كرة قطرها ذراع كما بين ذلك في محله . قوله : ( فعلمنا أن طلوعها على الغربية (متأخر ) لقائل أن يقول : إن وجود الخسوف في البلاد الشرقية في آخر الليل ووجودها في البلاد الغربية قبل آخر الليل بساعة أو بساعتين، لا يدل على تأخر طلوع الشمس على البلاد الغربية لم لا يجوز أن يكون حيلولة الأرض في البلاد الغربية مستندة إلى حركة القمر لكونها أسرع من حركة الشمس فتظهر الحيلولة في الغربية قبل الحيلولة الشرقية بساعة أو بساعتين، ولا شك أن الظل الناشئ من الأرض يتبدل نسبته بحركة القمر فالأولى بيان كروية الأرض أن يستدل بوجود الخسوف في البلاد الغربية قبل وجوده في البلاد الشرقية بنسبة واحدة فإنه لو لم تكن الأرض كروية لم تكن النسبة هناك مطردة فتأمل . قوله : ( وقس على هذا حال السالك فيما بين المغرب والشمال) لكن المناسب هاهنا أن يتأخر عليه الطلوع بمقدار قربه من المغرب . قوله : ( وحال السالك في السمتين المقابلين لهما أي وقس على هذا حال السالك فيما بين المشرق والمغرب وحال السالك فيما بين المغرب والجنوب.
And an objection was raised against them regarding the irregularities on its surface, and they answered about it (that it is like small bumps on a large sphere, so it does not detract from the sphericity (sensory sphericity known by what was mentioned). For the greatest mountain on the face of the earth, its ratio to it is like one fifth of a seventh of the width of a barley grain on a sphere whose diameter is a cubit. And the correct thing is as it is said: that a mountain that rises half a parasang to another, or drop the word 'fifth' etc. (His saying: (or drop etc.) the greatest mountain's ratio to the earth is the ratio of a seventh of the width of a barley grain to a sphere whose diameter is a cubit, as explained in its place. (His saying: (We know that its rising upon the western is delayed)) An objector may say: The occurrence of the eclipse in eastern cities at the end of the night and its occurrence in western cities an hour or two before the end of the night does not indicate the delay of the sun's rising in western cities; why may it not be that the interposition of the earth in the western cities is based on the motion of the moon because it is faster than the motion of the sun, so the interposition appears in the western [city] an hour or two before the eastern interposition? And there is no doubt that the shadow arising from the earth changes its proportion by the motion of the moon. So the better way to demonstrate the sphericity of the earth is to infer by the occurrence of the eclipse in western cities before its occurrence in eastern cities by a single proportion; for if the earth were not spherical, that proportion would not be consistent. So ponder. (His saying: (And measure similarly the case of the traveler between west and north)) But the appropriate here is that the rising should be delayed for him according to his proximity to the west. (His saying: (And the case of the traveler in the two opposite directions, i.e., and measure similarly the case of the traveler between east and west and the case of the traveler between west and south.)

Commentary
This passage consists of two main parts. First, the author presents an objection and its answer: critics point to mountains and valleys as evidence against a spherical earth. The response is that these irregularities are minuscule relative to the earth's size—like small bumps on a large sphere. The ratio of the highest mountain to the earth's diameter is compared to a tiny fraction of a barley grain's width on a cubit-wide sphere. This refutation uses analogy to show that such small irregularities do not negate overall sphericity. Second, a marginal note (labeled 'قوله') critiques the earlier proof from lunar eclipse timings. The objector argues that the observed delay in eclipses in western cities might be due to the moon's faster motion causing the earth's shadow to occur earlier in the west, not due to the earth's curvature. The note suggests a more rigorous proof: instead of comparing local times of the same eclipse, one should compare the absolute occurrence times of eclipses in eastern and western cities. If the time difference between the same eclipse in different longitudes is consistent, that cannot be explained by lunar motion and directly proves sphericity. The note concludes with minor corrections to the earlier combined case about travelers between intermediate directions.

Key Terms

كروية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mountains and valleys prove the Earth is flat or at least not spherical.
Correction: The scale analogy shows that even the highest mountain is negligible compared to the Earth's size, so they are consistent with sphericity.

Checkpoint

A) Craters on the moon
B) Small bumps on a large sphere
C) Waves on water

Key Takeaway
Surface irregularities do not disprove the Earth's sphericity because they are insignificant in scale.

An Improved Lunar Eclipse Proof for Sphericity
A marginal note presents an improved lunar eclipse proof for sphericity using consistent time differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the consistent time difference of a lunar eclipse across longitudes is a stronger proof of the Earth's curvature than simple observation of delays.

Core Idea
Consistent eclipse timings across longitudes directly prove the Earth is round.

The earlier proof of sphericity from lunar eclipses (delayed rising in the west) had a flaw: the moon's own motion could explain the timing differences. A marginal note in our text offers a more rigorous approach. Let's see how to refine the argument to eliminate doubt.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the flaw in the earlier proof
Read the objector's reasoning: the observed delay of an eclipse in western cities could be due to the moon's faster motion, not the Earth's curvature.
The objector presents a valid alternative explanation.
2
Learn the improved argument
The note suggests comparing the occurrence times of the same eclipse in different longitudes. If the time difference is consistent, that cannot be due to lunar motion because the moon's speed would cause variable differences.
Consistency of the proportion across longitudes is key.
3
Understand why it works
If the Earth were flat, the time difference would not be a constant proportion of the Earth's rotation; it would vary. The fixed proportion is exactly what a spherical Earth predicts.
This uses the geometry of a sphere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد عليهم الاختلاف الذي في سطحها فأجابوا عنه : ( بأنه كتضاريس صغيرة على كرة كبيرة فلا يقدح في أصل الكروية ( الحسية المعلومة بما ذكر ) فإن أعظم جبل على وجه الأرض نسبته إليها كخمس سبع عرض شعيرة على كرة قطرها ذراع . والصحيح كما أن يقال: فإن جبلاً يرتفع نصف فرسخ إلى آخر أو يحذف لفظ الخمس مر قوله : ( أو يحذف إلخ ) فإن أعظم جبل نسبته إلى الأرض نسبة سبع عرض شعيرة إلى كرة قطرها ذراع كما بين ذلك في محله . قوله : ( فعلمنا أن طلوعها على الغربية (متأخر ) لقائل أن يقول : إن وجود الخسوف في البلاد الشرقية في آخر الليل ووجودها في البلاد الغربية قبل آخر الليل بساعة أو بساعتين، لا يدل على تأخر طلوع الشمس على البلاد الغربية لم لا يجوز أن يكون حيلولة الأرض في البلاد الغربية مستندة إلى حركة القمر لكونها أسرع من حركة الشمس فتظهر الحيلولة في الغربية قبل الحيلولة الشرقية بساعة أو بساعتين، ولا شك أن الظل الناشئ من الأرض يتبدل نسبته بحركة القمر فالأولى بيان كروية الأرض أن يستدل بوجود الخسوف في البلاد الغربية قبل وجوده في البلاد الشرقية بنسبة واحدة فإنه لو لم تكن الأرض كروية لم تكن النسبة هناك مطردة فتأمل . قوله : ( وقس على هذا حال السالك فيما بين المغرب والشمال) لكن المناسب هاهنا أن يتأخر عليه الطلوع بمقدار قربه من المغرب . قوله : ( وحال السالك في السمتين المقابلين لهما أي وقس على هذا حال السالك فيما بين المشرق والمغرب وحال السالك فيما بين المغرب والجنوب.
And an objection was raised against them regarding the irregularities on its surface, and they answered about it (that it is like small bumps on a large sphere, so it does not detract from the sphericity (sensory sphericity known by what was mentioned). For the greatest mountain on the face of the earth, its ratio to it is like one fifth of a seventh of the width of a barley grain on a sphere whose diameter is a cubit. And the correct thing is as it is said: that a mountain that rises half a parasang to another, or drop the word 'fifth' etc. (His saying: (or drop etc.) the greatest mountain's ratio to the earth is the ratio of a seventh of the width of a barley grain to a sphere whose diameter is a cubit, as explained in its place. (His saying: (We know that its rising upon the western is delayed)) An objector may say: The occurrence of the eclipse in eastern cities at the end of the night and its occurrence in western cities an hour or two before the end of the night does not indicate the delay of the sun's rising in western cities; why may it not be that the interposition of the earth in the western cities is based on the motion of the moon because it is faster than the motion of the sun, so the interposition appears in the western [city] an hour or two before the eastern interposition? And there is no doubt that the shadow arising from the earth changes its proportion by the motion of the moon. So the better way to demonstrate the sphericity of the earth is to infer by the occurrence of the eclipse in western cities before its occurrence in eastern cities by a single proportion; for if the earth were not spherical, that proportion would not be consistent. So ponder. (His saying: (And measure similarly the case of the traveler between west and north)) But the appropriate here is that the rising should be delayed for him according to his proximity to the west. (His saying: (And the case of the traveler in the two opposite directions, i.e., and measure similarly the case of the traveler between east and west and the case of the traveler between west and south.)

Commentary
This passage consists of two main parts. First, the author presents an objection and its answer: critics point to mountains and valleys as evidence against a spherical earth. The response is that these irregularities are minuscule relative to the earth's size—like small bumps on a large sphere. The ratio of the highest mountain to the earth's diameter is compared to a tiny fraction of a barley grain's width on a cubit-wide sphere. This refutation uses analogy to show that such small irregularities do not negate overall sphericity. Second, a marginal note (labeled 'قوله') critiques the earlier proof from lunar eclipse timings. The objector argues that the observed delay in eclipses in western cities might be due to the moon's faster motion causing the earth's shadow to occur earlier in the west, not due to the earth's curvature. The note suggests a more rigorous proof: instead of comparing local times of the same eclipse, one should compare the absolute occurrence times of eclipses in eastern and western cities. If the time difference between the same eclipse in different longitudes is consistent, that cannot be explained by lunar motion and directly proves sphericity. The note concludes with minor corrections to the earlier combined case about travelers between intermediate directions.

Clarifying the Traveler's Direction and Rising Times
A marginal note corrects the rule for sunrise delay for travelers between intermediate directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the delay in sunrise for a traveler is related to their proximity to the west, and how to apply the same reasoning for opposite directions.

Core Idea
The delay in sunrise is proportional to the traveler's distance west.

The previous discussion about lunar eclipses led to a correction regarding travelers between intermediate directions. This short note clarifies that the rising time delay for a traveler depends on their proximity to the west, and it provides a rule for opposite directions.

Guided Path
1
Read the correction
The note says that for a traveler between west and north, the appropriate thing is that the rising is delayed according to his proximity to the west.
This is a slight correction to an earlier combined case.
2
Extend to other directions
The note asks to measure similarly for a traveler between east and west, and between west and south.
This shows the rule can be generalized.

Proofs for the Earth's Sphericity from Eclipses and Latitude Travel
Three proofs for earth's sphericity with objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three proofs for the Earth's sphericity derived from lunar eclipses, polar elevation, and their combination, and how the objection from mountains and valleys is answered.

Core Idea
Lunar eclipses and star visibility reveal Earth's curvature beyond doubt.

The classical scholars of kalam provided empirical arguments for the Earth's sphericity. This passage from al-Mawaqif presents three such proofs: the first from the timing of lunar eclipses at different longitudes, the second from the changing altitude of the pole star as one travels north-south, and the third combining both. It also addresses a natural objection about mountains and valleys, showing they are negligible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow from east-west proof to north-south proof to combination.
2
Identify each proof
Locate the three proofs in the text. For each, understand what observation is used and how it points to sphericity.
The first proof uses lunar eclipse timing; the second uses polar elevation; the third combines both directions.
3
Understand the objection and answer
Read the objection about mountains and the answer showing they are proportionally tiny. Relate this to the overall proof.
This shows that the proofs are based on sensory sphericity, not perfect geometric shape.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثالث : المقصد الثاني : إثبات كروية الأرض
بلاد أخرى ( غربية عنها أي عن البلاد الثانية ( بتلك المسافة بعينها قبل الأول بساعتين وقبل الثاني بساعة ) . والحاصل أنه يوجد في هذه البلاد الأخرى قبل آخر الليل بساعتين ) وعلى هذا القياس (فعلمنا أن (طلوعها أي طلوع الشمس على الغربية متأخر) بنسبة واحدة لأن الخسوف المعين كان في البلاد الأولى عند طلوع الشمس وفي الثانية قبله بساعة وفي الثالثة قبله بساعتين . ( وأما في العرض) أي فيما بين الشمال والجنوب ( فلان السالك في الشمال كلما أوغل فيه ازداد القطب ارتفاعاً عليه ) بحسب إيغاله فيه على نسبة واحدة ) حتى يصير بحيث يراه قريباً من سمت رأسه ولذلك تظهر له الكواكب الشمالية) التي كانت مختفية عنه، وتخفى عنه) الكواكب ( الجنوبية) التي كانت ظاهرة عليه ( والسالك ) الواغل (في الجنوب بالعكس من ذلك وأما فيما بينهما ) أي بين الطول والعرض ( فلتركب الأمرين) فإن السالك فيما بين المشرق والشمال يتقدم عليه الطلوع بمقدار قربه من المشرق ويزداد ارتفاع القطب عليه بمقدار ولوغه في الشمال. وقس على هذا حال السالك فيما بين المغرب والشمال وحال السالك في السمتين المقابلين لهما ( وأورد عليهم الاختلاف الذي في سطحها فأجابوا عنه : ( بأنه كتضاريس صغيرة على كرة كبيرة فلا يقدح في أصل الكروية ( الحسية المعلومة بما ذكر ) فإن أعظم جبل على وجه الأرض نسبته إليها كخمس سبع عرض شعيرة على كرة قطرها ذراع . والصحيح كما أن يقال: فإن جبلاً يرتفع نصف فرسخ إلى آخر أو يحذف لفظ الخمس
The First Observatory - Section Three: The Second Aim: Proving the Sphericity of the Earth
Other lands (western relative to them, i.e., relative to the second lands) by that same distance earlier than the first by two hours and earlier than the second by one hour). And the conclusion is that in these other lands it occurs (before the end of the night by two hours) and so on. So we know that (its rising i.e., the rising of the sun upon the western is delayed) by a fixed proportion, because the specific eclipse was in the first lands at sunrise, in the second an hour before it, and in the third two hours before it. (And as for latitude,) i.e., with respect to north and south, (it is because the traveler in the north, the more he goes into it, the more the pole rises upon him) according to his penetration in it by a fixed proportion, until it becomes such that he sees it near his zenith; therefore the northern stars that were hidden from him appear, and the southern stars that were visible to him become hidden. (And the traveler) who penetrates (into the south is the opposite of that. As for what is between them,) i.e., between longitude and latitude, (it is due to the combination of the two matters.) For the traveler between the east and north, sunrise advances upon him by the amount of his proximity to the east, and the elevation of the pole increases upon him by the amount of his penetration into the north. And analogize to this the case of the traveler between the west and north, and the case of the traveler in the two opposite directions to them. (And an objection was raised against them regarding the irregularities on its surface, and they answered it:) (That it is like small bumps on a large sphere, so it does not affect the principle of sphericity) (the sensory sphericity known by what was mentioned), for the greatest mountain on the face of the earth, its ratio to it is like five-sevenths of the width of a barley grain on a sphere whose diameter is a cubit. And the correct [view] is as if said: For a mountain that rises half a farsakh, up to the end, or the word 'five' is omitted.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs for the sphericity of the earth, followed by a response to an objection. First, the proof from lunar eclipses in the east-west direction: when a lunar eclipse is observed in different longitudes, the time of sunset or sunrise shifts proportionally, indicating a curved surface. Second, the proof from north-south travel: as one travels north, the celestial pole rises, and stars previously hidden become visible, while southern stars disappear; the opposite occurs when traveling south. Third, the combination of both directions leads to a compound effect. The objection concerning mountains and valleys (irregularities) on the earth's surface is answered by noting that these are negligible relative to the earth's size—the largest mountain is like a small bump on a sphere a cubit in diameter. The correct expression is that a mountain rising half a farsakh compares to the earth's diameter analogously, with a slight variation in wording.

تضاريس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mountains and valleys on the earth's surface disprove that the earth is spherical.
Correction: The passage explains that these irregularities are proportionally very small compared to the earth's size, so they do not affect the overall sensory sphericity; the earth is still spherical despite surface bumps.

Checkpoint

D) The shape of the moon changes.

Key Takeaway

?

?
The answer in this lesson compares mountains to bumps on a sphere. How often do you let small 'bumps' in your life—minor hardships or doubts—make you question the larger, established truths of faith? Are you willing to see them as insignificant next to the grand design?

Responding to the Submerged Parts Objection
Objection about submerged water and responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that water's submerged parts cannot be directly observed to be spherical, and the two responses: the argument from simplicity and the mathematical scholars' reliance on sensory sphericity.

Core Idea
Not all of water's surface is visible, but sphericity is inferred from its nature.

The discussion of water's sphericity faces an important objection: if we only see the apparent surface, what about the parts that are submerged? This lesson presents the responses given by scholars, including the use of simplicity of nature and the approach of mathematical scholars who accept sensory sphericity on the visible surface.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that if sphericity is proven only from the visible surface, then what about water that is submerged? The question challenges the extrapolation of sphericity to the whole body of water.
Notice the phrasing: 'what do you say about what is submerged in water?'
2
Understand the first response: simplicity
The first answer argues that if the apparent part is spherical, the rest must be likewise due to the simplicity of water's single nature. However, this is modified by noting that irregularities (tadharis) prevent actual perfect sphericity.
The word 'basaata' here refers to simplicity of nature, not simplicity in composition.
3
Grasp the second response: mathematical scholars' approach
The passage explains that mathematical scholars (arbab al-ta'alim) are content with observing sensory sphericity on the visible surface only. Therefore, the question about submerged parts does not even arise for them.
This is a practical point: they do not require absolute sphericity of the whole body.

The First Observatory - Section Three: The Third Aim: Water is Spherical Because it Exists on the Earth
And an objection against this answer is to say: 'Granted that what you mentioned is so, what do you say about what is submerged in water?' For that cannot be ascertained by this. (For if it is said: If the apparent is spherical, then the rest is the same because it is a single nature, we say: The reference) then (is to simplicity and its requirement of actual sphericity, and there is no doubt that irregularities prevent it even if those irregularities are not apparent to the senses) due to their extreme smallness. And know that the mathematical scholars are content with sensory sphericity in the apparent surface of the earth and water, so the question about the submerged part does not arise against them, nor is the answer by referring to simplicity befitting them.
[The Third Aim: Water is Spherical Because it Exists on the Earth]
They said: (And water) is also spherical for three reasons. The first: that the traveler at sea sees the top of the mountain before its base, meaning that the top of the mountain appears to him first, then what follows it gradually to its base, as if it is emerging from the water gradually at a fixed proportion. And it is nothing but due to the curvature of the water like a dome-shaped roundness (hindering it from sight). (It should not be said: Water is transparent, it has no color, so it does not conceal it like air; because we say: That which you mentioned is only in pure water, and this water of the sea is mixed with earthy particles, therefore it has salinity) so it has a color.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to a previous answer about water's sphericity and then provides the first of three proofs. First, an objection: if water is spherical only based on its apparent surface, what about submerged portions? The response is that the apparent surface implies the rest is spherical due to natural simplicity, but irregularities prevent absolute sphericity; however, mathematical scholars are content with sensory sphericity in the visible surface, so the objection does not apply to them. Then the three proofs are introduced. The first proof: a sailor at sea sees the top of a mountain before its base, indicating that the water surface curves and hides the base initially. An objection: water is transparent and colorless, so it should not obscure like air. The answer: pure water is transparent, but sea water is mixed with earthy parts (giving it salinity and color), so it can obstruct vision. This supports the curvature argument.

First Proof: Mountains Emerge Gradually at Sea
First proof for water's sphericity with transparency objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for water's sphericity: the gradual appearance of a mountain's top before its base as a ship approaches, and why sea water, not being pure, obscures vision.

Core Idea
The curvature of water hides the base of a mountain until the ship draws near.

The first proof for water's sphericity comes from a simple observation: when a ship approaches land, the top of a mountain is seen first, then gradually the lower parts. This indicates that the water surface curves, hiding the lower part. But one might object that water is transparent and should not hide anything. This lesson explains why sea water is opaque enough to do so.

Guided Path
1
Observe the phenomenon described
Imagine a ship sailing toward a mountain. The top of the mountain is visible from a distance, but as the ship gets closer, more of the mountain becomes visible until the base is seen. This suggests that something is blocking the view of the base initially, which is the curved water surface.
This is a common experience for sailors.
2
Understand why the curvature hides the base
The curvature of water forms a dome-like shape (تقبيب) that obstructs the line of sight to the lower parts of the mountain. The water's surface acts like a hill, hiding what lies behind it.
The term 'تقبيب' means forming a dome or arch.
3
Address the transparency objection
An objector might say: water is transparent and colorless like air, so it should not hide anything. The answer is that pure water is transparent, but sea water contains earthy particles, giving it salinity and color, which makes it opaque enough to block vision.
This is why the water of the sea has a distinct color.

How will you commit to deeper study so that you do not fall into hasty rejection?
