Objection and First Proof for the Sphericity of Water
Al-Jurjani addresses an objection to water's sphericity and presents the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to water's sphericity based on submerged parts and the first observational proof using the appearance of mountains at sea.

Core Idea
The curvature of water is observed by sailors before mountains emerge.

Al-Jurjani addresses a logical objection to the sphericity of water: if water is spherical because it lies on earth's surface, what about submerged parts? He then presents the first of three proofs based on nautical observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its answer
Carefully read the initial objection: if water is spherical due to its existence on earth, what about water that is submerged? The answer refers to simplicity and nature. Note the commentator's remark about mathematicians (arbāb al-taʿālīm).
The objection is about the submerged portion not being observable.
2
Understand the first proof
Read the first proof: a traveler at sea sees the top of a mountain before its base. This indicates the water's curvature (taqbīb) hides the lower part. Consider why this proves sphericity.
3
Examine the rebuttal to transparency
An objection says water is transparent, so it shouldn't hide anything. The rebuttal states that sea water is mixed with earthly particles, giving it color and opacity. Understand this distinction.
The passage says 'simple pure water' is transparent, but sea water is not.
4
Reflect on the commentator's glosses
The passage includes marginal notes (qāla) explaining terms like 'taqbīb' and conditions for concealment. Note the geometry reference and the correction about proportions.
These glosses clarify technical details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[ المقصد الثالث : الماء كروي لوجوده على الأرض ]
قالوا : ( والماء ) أيضاً ( كروي لوجوه ) ثلاثة الأول: إن السائر في البحر يرى رأس الجبل قبل أسفله ) يعني أنه يظهر عليه رأس الجبل أولاً ثم ما يليه شيئاً فشيئاً إلى أسفله كأنه يطلع من الماء متدرجاً على نسبة واحدة (وما هو إلا لستر تقبيب الماء ) على هيئة حدبة الاستدارة ( له ) عن الرؤية . ( لا يقال : الماء شفاف ) لا لون له ( فلا يستره ) كالهواء ( لأنا نقول : ذلك ) الذي ذكرتموه إنما هو ( في الماء البسيط ) الصرف ، ( وهذا ) الماء السائر ) يخالطه أي أجزاء من الأرضية ولذلك ملوحته ) فله لون
قوله : ( في السطح الظاهر من الأرض والماء إنما يتم لو كان الستر واقعاً في كل الأرض والماء في شرح التذكرة المحضري إذ الأدلة المذكورة لما دلت على استدارة القدر المكشوف من الأرض حدس أن الباقي كذلك.
قوله : ( على نسبة واحدة يثبت تحدب ظاهره بنسبة واحدة .
قوله : ( ولذلك يختلف إلخ) وذلك لأن دائرة الارتفاع تمر بطرفي هذين الخطين لأنها تارة برأس الشخص وعدم المحاذيين تغطي الأنف وبمركزي العالم والكواكب فيصير الناظر والمركزان في سطح هذه الدائرة فيكون ذلك الخطان أيضاً في سطحها فإذا أخرجا إلى سطح الفلك الأعلى قطعتا محيط دائرة الارتفاع فينحصر بينهما قوس منها .
قوله: (والصحيح كما مر أن يقال أي الصحيح أن يقال: فإن جبلاً يرتفع نصف فرسخ نسبته إلى قطر الأرض كخمس سبع عرض شعيرة إلخ . وقوله : النصف بالنصف على أنه مفعول مطلق أي يرتفع ارتفاع نصف فرسخ وقد مر تمام الكلام في مقدمة الفصل الثاني الذي نحن بصدده . قوله : (يمنعها ) أي يمنع البساطة التضاريس بمعنى أنا لا نسلم بساطتها وإن لم تكن فيها تضاريس وإن لم تكن تلك التضاريس ظاهرة للحس بسبب كونها في غاية الصغر.
قوله : ( تقبيب ) يقال : بيت مقبب أي جعل فوقه قبة .
The First Observatory - Section Three: Third Purpose: Water is spherical because it exists on earth. And the objection to this answer is that it is said: Granted what you mentioned is so, what is your statement regarding what is submerged in water? For that cannot apply there. If it is said: If the apparent part is spherical, then the rest is likewise because it is a single nature, we say: Then the reference is to simplicity and its necessitating a true sphere. And there is no doubt that the roughnesses prevent it, even if those roughnesses are not apparent to sense due to their extreme smallness. Know that the mathematicians are satisfied with sensible sphericity in the apparent surface of the earth and water, so the question about the submerged does not apply to them, and it is not proper for them to answer by referring to simplicity. The Third Purpose: Water is spherical because it exists on earth. They said: Water is also spherical for three reasons. First: The traveler at sea sees the top of the mountain before its base. Meaning that the top of the mountain appears to him first, then what follows it gradually to its base, as if it is rising from the water progressively in one proportion, and it is only due to the concealment by the arching of the water in the form of a curvature that hides it from vision. It should not be said: Water is transparent, having no color, so it does not hide it like air. For we say: That which you mentioned is only in simple pure water, while this sea water is mixed with earthly particles, and for that reason it has color. His saying: 'In the apparent surface of the earth and water' is only complete if the concealment occurred in the whole earth and water in the explanation of al-Tadhkira al-Mahdari, for the mentioned proofs indicate the curvature of the exposed part of the earth, so it is inferred that the rest is likewise. His saying: 'In one proportion' establishes the curvature of its apparent surface in one proportion. His saying: 'And therefore it differs etc.' That is because the altitude circle passes through the ends of these two lines, because it sometimes passes through the head of the person and the two points opposite the nose, and through the centers of the world and the planets, so the observer and the two centers are in the plane of this circle, and so these two lines are also in its plane. When they are extended to the surface of the outermost sphere, they cut an arc of the altitude circle, and between them is enclosed an arc of it. His saying: 'And the correct thing, as previously mentioned, is to say' i.e., the correct thing is to say: A mountain that rises half a parasang, its proportion to the diameter of the earth is like five-sevenths of a barleycorn's width, etc. And his saying: 'half by half' as a verbal noun, meaning it rises by an elevation of half a parasang. And the full discussion has passed in the introduction of the second chapter which we are discussing. His saying: 'Impedes it' i.e., impedes simplicity, the roughnesses, meaning we do not affirm its simplicity even if there are no roughnesses in it, even if those roughnesses are not apparent to sense due to their extreme smallness. His saying: 'Arching' (taqbib): It is said: a domed house (bayt muqabbab), meaning a dome was placed over it.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary on the third purpose (al-maqsad al-thalith) concerning the sphericity of water. It begins by addressing an objection: if water is spherical because it exists on earth, what about water that is submerged (ghayr muʿāyan) and cannot exhibit that property? The answer is that the apparent part's sphericity implies the rest due to a single nature, but the objector then points to the earth's roughness (tadārīs). The commentator explains that mathematicians (arbāb al-taʿālīm) rely on sensible sphericity of the apparent surface, so the objection does not apply to them. Then the original text states three proofs for water's sphericity, though only the first is fully given here. The first proof: a sailor sees the top of a mountain before its base, indicating the water's curvature (taqbīb) hides the lower part. An objection that water is transparent is rebutted: the water here is mixed with earthly particles, giving it color. The rest of the passage contains marginal glosses (qāla) explaining terms and concepts: the condition for concealment, the proportion of curvature, the geometry of altitude circles, a correction of a previous statement, and a definition of 'taqbīb' (arching) as a dome-like structure.

Key Terms
المقصد الثالث
كروية
المغمور
التضاريس
أرباب التعاليم
تقبيب
البساطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Water is transparent, so it cannot hide anything, and therefore the mountain's emergence cannot be due to water curvature.
Correction: The passage explains that sea water is not pure; it is mixed with earthly particles, giving it color and opacity, so it can conceal the lower part of the mountain.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the traveler at sea see the top of a mountain before its base?
A) Because the mountain is moving
B) Because the water is transparent
C) Because the curvature of the water hides the lower part
D) Because the mountain is higher

Key Takeaway
The sphericity of water is supported by the observation that the top of a mountain appears first at sea, due to the water's curvature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The traveler sees the mountain top before the base because of water's curvature. What 'mountains' of truth in your faith have you only glimpsed from a distance because your own assumptions or biases curved your vision? Name one such truth and commit to one concrete action this week to see it more fully.
?
Pure water is transparent, but sea water is opaque due to earthly particles. What 'earthly particles'—distractions, habits, attachments—have colored your spiritual vision, making it hard to perceive the clear guidance of Allah? What is one particle you can start removing today?

The Earth at the Center: Proof from Uniform Stars
Al-Jurjani presents the proof that Earth is at the center from uniform star sizes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the observational argument that the Earth must be at the center of the universe because celestial bodies appear equally sized from all directions.

Core Idea
Equal star sizes prove Earth is the universe's center.

Al-Jurjani presents a straightforward astronomical argument: the celestial bodies appear the same size from every part of the Earth. This uniformity would not occur if the Earth were not at the center. This lesson breaks down that argument and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the author’s claim and the evidence provided.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence stating that Earth is at the center of the universe. What phrase does the author use?
3
Understand the evidence
The author uses the equality of appearance of celestial bodies from Earth as proof. Why does uniform size imply centrality?
If Earth were off-center, stars would appear larger when nearer.
4
Consider the conditional case
Notice the conditional statement: 'Were it not for it being in the center, then...' This shows the argument is based on an observable consequence of deviation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثالث : المقصد الرابع : موقع الأرض من الكواكب
الأرض في وسط (الكل أي مركز حجمها منطبق على مركز العالم (لأن الكواكب في جميع الجهات والجوانب من الأرض ( ترى بقدر واحد لا تفاوت فيه، ولولا أنه أي الثقيل المطلق الذي هو الأرض ( في الوسط لكان في بعض الجوانب أقرب ) إلى السماء (فترى) الكواكب هناك ( أكبر وفي بعض الجوانب أبعد ) منها ( فترى ) الكواكب فيه ( أصغر . ونقول : ( نحن في رد ما ذكروه (لم لا يجوز أن يكون خروجها عن الوسط بقدر لا يكون التفاوت الموجب ؟ ) بفتح الجيم (له ) أي لذلك القدر (محسوساً) وهو أي قدر الخروج مع كونه موجباً لتفاوت غير محسوس في الكواكب ( مقدار غير قليل في نفسه ) بل هو كثير.
قوله : ( أي مركز حجمها منطبق على مركز العالم قيل إنه مخالف لما ذكره في المقصد الأول من أن الأرض تقتضي أن تكون مركز ثقلها منطبقاً على مركز العالم والحق هو هذا المذكور المقصد الأول : لا يخفى عليك أن ثقل الأرض كيفية سارية في حجم الأرض كلها . فإذا فرضنا أن نقطة كانت مركزاً لحجم الأرض كانت تلك النقطة أيضاً مركزاً لثقلها . وأما إذا فرضنا أمراً عارضاً يكون بحيث قد أخرج الأرض عن مركزها الأصلي فذلك العارض أيضاً يكون بحيث قد أخرج ثقلها عن مركزها الأصلي، نعم يمكن أن يكون بعض أطراف الأرض أثقل من البعض الآخر مع تساويها في المقدار والحجم لكن الكلام في الأرض البسيطة، فتأمل.
The First Observatory - Section Three: Fourth Purpose: The Position of the Earth Relative to the Celestial Bodies. The Earth is in the center of the whole, i.e., the center of its volume coincides with the center of the universe, because the celestial bodies in all directions and sides of the earth are seen in equal measure without variation. Were it not for it, i.e., the absolutely heavy which is the Earth, being in the center, then [the Earth] would be closer to the sky in some directions, and thus the celestial bodies there would be seen larger, and in some directions farther from it, and thus the celestial bodies there would be seen smaller. And we say in response to what they mentioned: Why is it not permissible that its deviation from the center be by an amount such that the variation caused thereby (with the jīm open) i.e., for that amount, is imperceptible? That is, the amount of deviation, while causing an imperceptible variation in the celestial bodies, is itself not a small amount; rather it is large. His saying: i.e., the center of its volume coincides with the center of the universe. It is said that this contradicts what he mentioned in the first purpose, that the Earth necessarily has its center of gravity coinciding with the center of the universe. The truth is what is mentioned in the first purpose: It is not hidden from you that the weight of the Earth is a quality pervading the entire volume of the Earth. So if we suppose a point to be the center of the Earth's volume, that point is also the center of its gravity. And if we suppose an accidental occurrence that has removed the Earth from its original center, then that accident also would have removed its gravity from its original center. Yes, it is possible that some parts of the Earth are heavier than others, despite equality in quantity and volume, but the discussion is about the simple Earth, so reflect.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an argument and commentary on the fourth purpose (al-maqsad al-rabiʿ) concerning the position of the Earth relative to the celestial bodies (kawākib). It presents a proof that the Earth is at the center of the universe: because celestial bodies are seen in equal size from all directions on Earth, any deviation from the center would cause them to appear larger when closer and smaller when farther. The commentator then anticipates an objection: perhaps the deviation is such that the resulting size difference is imperceptible. He responds by noting that even if the size difference is imperceptible, the amount of deviation itself is not small; it can be large while the variation remains undetectable. The commentary then addresses a potential contradiction with an earlier statement: here the center of volume is said to coincide with the center of the universe, but previously it was stated that the center of gravity coincides. The commentator resolves this by explaining that in the simple Earth (al-arḍ al-basīṭa), the center of volume and center of gravity are identical; only accidental departures could separate them, but the discussion assumes the simple Earth.

Key Terms
الوسط
مركز العالم
الثقيل المطلق

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence used to argue that the Earth is at the center?
A) The Earth is the heaviest element
B) Celestial bodies appear equally sized from all directions
C) The Earth is stationary
D) The sun sets in the west

Key Takeaway
The uniform apparent size of stars across the sky indicates Earth is at the universe's center.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe you are at the center of your own universe — your plans, your worries, your desires. But this lesson shows that the Earth's centrality is proven by the uniformity of the stars. Are your daily observations of life uniform and orderly, pointing to a Creator? Or are there inconsistencies you ignore because you assume you are the center? Will you step back and realign your perspective around Allah?
?
The proof hinges on a conditional: 'Were it not for the Earth being in the center, then the stars would appear different sizes.' What if you examined your own faith with the same logic? Is your worship consistent in all circumstances? If not, what does that say about your spiritual center? Will you seek to anchor yourself firmly in obedience to Allah so that your faith does not waver?

Objection: Could Deviation Be Imperceptible?
Al-Jurjani responds to the objection that deviation from center may be imperceptible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a deviation from the center could be so small that the size variation is imperceptible, and Al-Jurjani's response that the amount of deviation itself is not small.

Core Idea
Even a large deviation can cause imperceptible size changes.

The previous argument assumes that any deviation from the center would produce a noticeable difference in star sizes. But what if the deviation is so small that the difference in size is too tiny to detect? Al-Jurjani anticipates this objection and responds by pointing out that the amount of deviation could be substantial even if its effect appears insignificant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. The opponent asks: why can't the Earth be slightly off-center such that the size difference is imperceptible?
2
Identify the response
Find how Al-Jurjani refutes this. He distinguishes between the amount of deviation and the perceptibility of its effect.
3
Understand the key point
Note that the response does not deny the possibility of imperceptible variation; instead it argues that even a large deviation could cause imperceptible variation, so the objection fails to undermine the original proof.
The deviation itself can be large while the resulting size change is too small to notice.
4
Reflect on the logic
Think about why this response is significant. It shows that the argument from uniform appearance is not based on a small margin of error, but on a fundamental geometric principle.

Reconciling Volume Center and Gravity Center
Al-Jurjani reconciles the center of volume with the center of gravity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the center of volume and the center of gravity of the Earth coincide, and how this resolves a potential contradiction in the text.

Core Idea
For a simple Earth, volume center equals gravity center.

A careful reader might notice a potential contradiction: earlier it was said that Earth's center of gravity must coincide with the universe's center, but here we speak of the center of volume. Al-Jurjani's commentator resolves this by explaining that in the simple Earth (without irregularities), the two centers are identical. This lesson examines that resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the reconciliation
Read the passage that discusses the apparent contradiction. Identify the two statements that seem to conflict.
2
Understand the problem
One statement says center of volume = center of universe; another says center of gravity = center of universe. Why might these be considered contradictory?
Because volume and gravity are different properties.
3
Follow the resolution
The commentator argues that for the simple Earth, the center of volume and center of gravity are the same because weight pervades the entire volume uniformly. An accidental departure would shift both together.
4
Consider the qualification
Note the final remark that the discussion assumes the simple Earth, where parts are homogeneous. This explains why the centers are identical.

Source Text

Earth's Imperceptible Magnitude Relative to the Heavens
Al-Jurjani states and proves that the Earth has no perceptible magnitude relative to the celestial spheres, except for the Moon's sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Earth appears as a point relative to the celestial spheres, and how this is proven through the equality of true and sensible horizons and the simultaneous rising and setting of opposite points.

Core Idea
The Earth's size is negligible compared to the celestial spheres, so its radius does not affect celestial observations.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a key astronomical-geometric proof in Islamic kalam cosmology. The author demonstrates that despite the Earth having a measurable radius, its effect on the positions of celestial objects is imperceptible, except for the Moon. This conclusion has theological implications for the universe's structure and the Earth's place within it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the geometric language and the reference to the Moon as an exception.
2
Understand the geometric argument
The passage describes two lines: one from Earth's center to a celestial point, another from the observer's eye (surface) to the same point. These lines intersect at an acute angle near Earth but diverge, yet at the celestial sphere their endpoints are imperceptibly close.
This shows that the observer's position on Earth's surface does not affect the apparent position of stars.
3
Connect to the proof from simultaneous rising and setting
The proof is that opposite points of the celestial sphere rise and set at exactly the same time. If Earth's magnitude were perceptible, there would be a delay.
The example with Aries and Libra illustrates this point.
4
Note the exception for the Moon's sphere
The Earth's radius is perceptible relative to the Moon's sphere, causing a detectable difference between the two lines.
This exception is important for lunar observations and calculations.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument mattered in medieval cosmology: it affirmed that the Earth's size does not introduce observational errors, supporting a geocentric model with a central Earth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ليس للأرض عند الأفلاك قدر محسوس) فالخط الخارج من مركزها إلى نقطة ما على الفلك كمركز كوكب من الكواكب (و) الخط (الخارج من الباصرة) التي في حكم سطح الأرض ( وإن كانا يتقاطعان) على تلك النقطة ( ضرورة بزاوية حادة من جانب الأرض ثم يتفارقان على زاوية أخرى مساوية للأولى ذاهبين إلى سطح الفلك الأعلى فلا شك أنهما يقعان منه على موضعين بينهما بعد بحسب نفس الأمر (لكنهما موقعهما لا يتفاوت في الحس)، كان أحدهما انطبق على الآخر وصار موقعهما واحداً ( ولذلك ) أي ولأن الأرض ليس لها قدر محسوس بالنسبة إلى الأفلاك ( كان الظاهر والخفي من الفلك متساويين وكان الأفق الحقيقي المار بمركز العالم، والحسي المار بظاهر الأرض في حكم دائرتين متطابقتين مع أن مقدار نصف قطر الأرض واقع بينهما ( يدل على ذلك) التساوي ( طلوع كل جزء مع غروب نظيره لا قبل حتى يكون الظاهر أكبر ( ولا بعد) حتى يكون الخفي أكبر. (وهذا) الذي ذكرناه إنما هو ( بالنسبة إلى غير فلك القمر وأما فلك القمر فللأرض) بل لنصف قطرها ( عنده قدر محسوس ولذلك يختلف) في الحس ( موضع الخطين المذكورين)

قوله: (يدل على ذلك التساوي طلوع كل جزء مع غروب نظيره إلخ) مثلاً إذا كان كوكب [؟] مع [؟] في سمت الأوج وكان كوكب آخر مقابلاً له في سمت الحضيض كان طلوع الكوكب الأول غروب الكوكب الثاني وكان أيضاً غروب الكوكب الأول مع طلوع الكوكب الثاني لا قبل ولا بعد. وكذا طلوع الجزء الأول من الحمل مثلاً يكون مع غروب الجزء الأول مع الميزان لا قبل ولا بعد هذا الحكم بالنسبة إلى الأفق الحسي ظاهر معلوم بالمشاهدة وأما بالنسبة إلى الأفق الحقيقي فبالطريق الأولى
The Earth has no perceptible magnitude relative to the celestial spheres. So the line from its center to a point on the celestial sphere, like the center of a planet, and the line from the observer's eye (which is effectively on the Earth's surface), even though they intersect at that point necessarily at an acute angle from the Earth's side, then diverge at another angle equal to the first, going to the surface of the highest celestial sphere – there is no doubt that they fall from it on two positions between which there is a distance in reality, but their positions do not differ in sensation; one of them coincided with the other and their position became one. Therefore – because the Earth has no perceptible magnitude relative to the celestial spheres – the apparent and hidden parts of the celestial sphere are equal, and the true horizon passing through the center of the world and the sensible horizon passing through the Earth's surface are in the ruling of two coincident circles, even though the amount of the Earth's radius lies between them. The equality indicates that each part rises with the setting of its opposite, not earlier so that the apparent would be larger, nor later so that the hidden would be larger. What we have mentioned is relative to other than the Moon's sphere; as for the Moon's sphere, the Earth – rather, its radius – has a perceptible magnitude relative to it, and therefore the positions of the two mentioned lines differ in sensation. His saying: 'The equality indicates that each part rises with the setting of its opposite, etc.' For example, if a planet [؟] with [؟] in the apogee and another planet opposite it in the perigee, the rising of the first planet is the setting of the second planet, and also the setting of the first planet is with the rising of the second planet, neither earlier nor later. Likewise, the rising of the first part of Aries, for example, occurs with the setting of the first part of Libra, neither earlier nor later. This ruling is apparent and known by observation for the sensible horizon; as for the true horizon, it is even more so.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage states and proves that the Earth has no perceptible magnitude relative to the celestial spheres, except for the Moon's sphere. The author first gives the claim: the Earth has no perceptible size compared to the celestial spheres. Then he offers a geometric argument. Consider two lines: one from the Earth's center to a point on the celestial sphere (e.g., the center of a planet), and another from the observer's eye on the Earth's surface to the same point. These lines intersect at an acute angle near the Earth and then diverge, but when they reach the celestial sphere, their endpoints are distinct in reality but imperceptibly close to the observer. Therefore, the two positions appear as one. Because of this, the entire celestial sphere appears the same whether seen from the center or the surface: the true horizon (passing through the center) and the sensible horizon (tangent to the surface) are effectively coincident, even though the Earth's radius lies between them. The proof is that every part of the celestial sphere rises exactly when its opposite part sets, without any delay. Had the Earth's magnitude been perceptible, the apparent positions would be slightly shifted. The author then gives an example: if one planet is at the apogee and another opposite at the perigee, their risings and settings coincide exactly. Similarly, the rising of the first point of Aries coincides with the setting of the first point of Libra. This is observed for the sensible horizon and holds even more strictly for the true horizon. The passage ends by noting that this applies to all celestial spheres except the Moon's, where the Earth's radius is perceptible, causing a difference between the two lines.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The true and sensible horizons are always identical in every respect.
Correction: They are effectively identical only because the Earth's radius is negligible compared to the celestial spheres; for the Moon's sphere, they differ.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Earth's radius is imperceptible relative to the celestial spheres, so the true and sensible horizons coincide, proven by simultaneous rising and setting of opposite points.

?
The author shows that the Moon's sphere is an exception where Earth's size matters. In your life, where do you overestimate your own significance, thinking you are the center of everything? Where might you be underestimating your impact? Will you ask Allah to grant you humility to see your place correctly?

Will you take time today to observe your own patterns and align them with the reality of your smallness before Allah, so that you may turn to Him with sincerity?

Objection and Answer on Day and Night Equality
Al-Jurjani presents an objection about day/night equality and answers it by distinguishing rising/setting from day length.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the equality of the apparent and hidden parts of the celestial sphere against the objection that it would imply constant equal day and night.

Core Idea
Equality of rising and setting does not imply equality of day length.

This lesson presents an objection that challenges the earlier claim that the apparent and hidden parts of the celestial sphere are equal. The objector argues that such equality would force day and night to be equal year-round, which is false. Al-Jurjani’s response distinguishes between the coincidence of rising/setting and the actual duration of daylight, and provides two separate explanations for seasonal and equatorial equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice the objection's logical structure: if the apparent and hidden parts are equal, then day and night would always be equal, but they are not, so the premise must be false. Understand the challenge.
The objector uses modus tollens.
2
Identify the answer's move
Al-Jurjani rejects the implication. He says equality of rising/setting only means the sun rises exactly when the opposite point sets, not that day length is equal.
This is the core distinction.
3
Understand the two explanations
First, seasonal length differences are due to the sun's apogee and perigee. Second, equality at the equator or equinoxes is due to Earth's negligible size making horizons coincide.
These show that the objection's conclusion does not follow.
4
Reflect on the defense
Consider how this reasoning preserves the original claim about the equality of apparent and hidden parts while accounting for observed day/night variations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو كان الظاهر والخفي من الفلك متساويين وذلك التساوي يلزم تساوي الليل والنهار في الصيف والشتاء وليس كذلك، قلنا ممنوع بل اللازم من ذلك هو أن يكون طلوع الشمس مع غروب الجزء الذي يقابله وأن يكون غروبها مع طلوعه لا قبل ولا بعد، وأما كون النهار أطول من الليل أو بالعكس فمرجعه إلى صعود الشمس على الأوج وإلى هبوطها إلى الحضيض. وأما تساوي الليل والنهار في خط الاستواء في جميع السنة أو تساويهما في غير خط الاستواء حين ما حلت الشمس في إحدى الاعتدالين فذلك أيضاً لعدم القدر المحسوس للأرض بالنسبة إلى سائر الأفلاك العالية فلذلك كان الأفق الحقيقي والأفق الحس هناك في حكم دائرتين متطابقتين ومتحدتين.
If it is said: If the apparent and hidden parts of the celestial sphere are equal, that equality necessitates equality of night and day in summer and winter, and it is not so. We say: It is prevented. Rather, what follows from that is that the rising of the sun occurs with the setting of the part opposite to it, and its setting occurs with the rising of that opposite, neither earlier nor later. As for the day being longer than the night or vice versa, its cause is the sun's ascent to the apogee and its descent to the perigee. As for the equality of night and day at the equator throughout the entire year, or their equality at other latitudes when the sun is at one of the equinoxes, that is also due to the lack of perceptible magnitude of the Earth relative to the other high celestial spheres. Therefore, the true horizon and the sensible horizon there are in the ruling of two coincident and unified circles.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection claims that if the apparent and hidden parts of the celestial sphere are equal (as argued in the previous proof), then day and night would always be equal, even in summer and winter, which contradicts experience. The answer rejects this inference. The equality of rising and setting only entails that the Sun rises exactly when the opposite point of the celestial sphere sets. It does not imply that the length of day and night is equal. The length of day depends on the Sun's position on the ecliptic: when the Sun is near the apogee (summer), the day is longer; when near the perigee (winter), the night is longer. The equality of day and night at the equator throughout the year, or at other latitudes at the equinoxes, is explained by the Earth's imperceptible magnitude relative to the celestial spheres, which makes the true and sensible horizons effectively coincide. Thus, the objection is refuted.

هبوطها إلى الحضيض
الأفق الحقيقي
الأفق الحس
الاعتدالين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equality of rising and setting implies equality of day length.
Correction: Equality of rising and setting only means the sun rises exactly when the opposite point sets; day length depends on the sun's declination and the horizon's tilt.

Checkpoint

B) The sun's ascent to the apogee and descent to the perigee
C) The Earth's rotation speed
D) The Moon's phases

Key Takeaway
Day length depends on the sun's position on the ecliptic, not solely on the horizon coincidence.

The Moon's Sphere and Parallax (اختلاف المنظر)
Al-Jurjani explains the cause and effects of lunar parallax.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Moon exhibits perceptible parallax and how this difference between true and apparent positions affects its observed altitude, longitude, and latitude.

Core Idea
The Moon's proximity makes its parallax perceptible, shifting its apparent position relative to the true one.

Al-Jurjani explains that because the Earth's radius is perceptible relative to the Moon's sphere, the lines from Earth's center and from an observer's eye intersect at the Moon at an acute angle, causing a measurable difference. This lesson unpacks that geometry and its implications for astronomical observation.

Guided Path
1
Understand why parallax is perceptible for the Moon
The Earth's radius is large enough relative to the Moon's sphere to create a noticeable angle between lines from the Earth's center and the observer's eye.
This is because the Moon is the closest celestial body.
2
Distinguish between true and apparent position
The true position is determined by a line from Earth's center through the Moon's center; the apparent position by a line from the observer's eye through the Moon's center.
3
See how parallax varies with altitude
The difference in position depends on the angle of intersection: larger angle gives greater parallax. At the zenith, the lines coincide, so no parallax occurs.
The true position is always closer to the zenith than the apparent position.
4
Extend to longitude and latitude
Parallax in altitude can also cause differences in the Moon's longitude and latitude when considering circles of latitude passing through the endpoints of the two lines.
When the circle of altitude aligns with the ecliptic, only longitude differs; otherwise both longitude and latitude differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (عند قدر محسوس) فعلى هذا يكون النصف الظاهر من فلك القمر أقل من النصف تلك الدائرة (وهو ما في دائرة الارتفاع) على سطح الفلك الأعلى (فيكون الموضع الحقيقي للقمر) في ينتهي إليه الخط الخارج من مركز الأرض ماراً بمركز القمر (غير الموضع المرئي) له فيها وهو ما ينتهي إليه الخط الخارج من الباصرة ماراً بمركزه وإنما اختلف الموضعان في الحس (لأجل التقاطع المذكور) وهو تقاطعهما على مركز القمر بزاوية حادة من الجانبين على ما مر لكنها معتبرة في الحس هاهنا لقرب القمر الموجب لكبر الزاوية) وذلك الاختلاف في دائرة الارتفاع (بحسب زاوية التقاطع فكلما كانت الزاوية أكبر كان الاختلاف بين الموضعين أكثر وكلما كانت أصغر كان أقل، (وهذا التفاوت يسمى اختلاف المنظر. ولا شك أن الخطين المتقاطعين ما كان مبدؤه فوق يقع منتهاه تحت فالخط الخارج من الباصرة) منتهاه (أقرب إلى الأفق دائماً فموضعه الحقيقي فوق المرئي أبداً) فلو فرض أن القمر على سمت الرأس لم يكن له اختلاف منظر لاتحاد الخطين حينئذ. وإذا لم يكن عليه كان له ذلك ويكون موضعه الحقيقي أبعد عن الأفق وأقرب إلى سمت الرأس لما عرفت. ثم إن هذا الاختلاف الواقع في دائرة الارتفاع قد يقتضي اختلافاً في طول الكوكب وعرضه. فإنا إذا فرضنا دائرتي عرض تمران بطرفي الخطين المذكورين فهما إذا وقعتا

قوله: (قد يقتضي إلخ) أي يقتضي اقتضاء جزئياً أن يكون موضعا الكواكب في الطول والعرض الحقيقيان أي المقيسان إلى مركز العالم مخالفين لموضعيهما المرئيين المقيسان إلى سطح الأرض الذي هو موضع الإبصار. الحقيقي منه فلا يلزم أن يكون طلوع كل جزء مع غروب نظيره من فلك القمر أقل من النصف الحقيقي. فلا يلزم أن يكون طلوع كل جزء مع غروب نظيره بل يكون طلوعه بعد غروب نظيره. قوله: فالخط الخارج من الباصرة إلخ) يعني أن الخط الخارج من الباصرة كان مبدؤه فوق مبدأ الخط الخارج من مركز العالم فيكون منتهى الخط الخارج عن الباصرة أقرب إلى الأفق دائما. وقوله: فوق المرئي أي فوق الموضع المرئي.

قوله: (فإنا إذا فرضنا دائرتي عرض) قد عرفت أن دائرة العرض هي دائرة مارة بقطبي مركز القمر أم لا، ثم لا يخفى بيان الاختلاف في الطول لا يحتاج إلى اعتبار دائرتي عرض وإنما الذي يحتاج إلى اعتبارهما معاً هو بيان الاختلاف في العرض. وقوله فهما أي طرفا الخطين المذكورين. وقوله: إذا وقعتا على نقطتين من فلك البروج فحينئذ تكون دائرة الارتفاع منطبقة على منطقة البروج وقوله: كان ما بينهما أي بين النقطتين المذكورتين أو بين طرفي الخطين المذكورين أو بين الدائرتين المذكورتين والمآل واحد بمعنى أنه حينئذ كان بينهما اختلاف في الطولين فقط. ولم يوجد هناك عرض أصلاً فضلاً عن أن يوجد هناك اختلاف في العرضين. وقوله: الواقعتان يعني أن هاهنا اختلافين معاً هما اختلاف الطولين واختلاف العرضين، لكن
His saying: 'having a perceptible magnitude.' So according to this, the apparent half of the Moon's sphere is less than the half of that circle (which is in the circle of altitude) on the surface of the highest celestial sphere. So the true position of the Moon is that to which the line from the Earth's center passing through the Moon's center reaches, whereas its apparent position is that to which the line from the observer's eye passing through its center reaches. The two positions differ in sensation because of the mentioned intersection – that is, their intersection at the Moon's center at an acute angle on both sides as before, but it is considered in sensation here due to the Moon's proximity causing the angle to be large. This difference is in the circle of altitude according to the angle of intersection: the larger the angle, the greater the difference between the two positions; the smaller the angle, the less the difference. This variation is called parallax. There is no doubt that the two intersecting lines – their origin above and their endpoint below – the line from the observer's eye has its endpoint always closer to the horizon, so its true position is always above the apparent. If we suppose the Moon is at the zenith, it has no parallax because the lines coincide then. If it is not at the zenith, it has parallax, and its true position is farther from the horizon and closer to the zenith, as you know. Then this difference occurring in the circle of altitude may require a difference in the planet's longitude and latitude. For if we suppose two circles of latitude passing through the endpoints of the two mentioned lines, then when they fall... His saying: 'may require, etc.' means it requires partially that the true positions of the planets in longitude and latitude (i.e., measured from the center of the world) are different from their apparent positions (measured from the Earth's surface, the place of observation). The true one [of the Moon's sphere] – it does not follow that every part rises with the setting of its opposite; rather, its rising occurs after the setting of its opposite. His saying: 'the line from the observer's eye, etc.' means that the line from the observer's eye, its origin is above the origin of the line from the center of the world, so the endpoint of the line from the observer's eye is always closer to the horizon. And his saying: 'above the apparent' means above the apparent position. His saying: 'for we if we suppose two circles of latitude' – you know that the circle of latitude is a circle passing through the poles of the Moon's center or not. Then it is not hidden that explaining the difference in longitude does not require considering the two circles of latitude; rather, what requires considering both together is explaining the difference in latitude. And his saying: 'the two' means the two endpoints of the two mentioned lines. And his saying: 'if they fall on two points of the ecliptic, then the circle of altitude coincides with the plane of the ecliptic' and his saying: 'what is between them' means between the two mentioned points or between the endpoints of the two lines or between the two mentioned circles, and the result is one, meaning that then there is between them a difference in longitudes only, and there is no latitude at all, let alone a difference in latitudes. And his saying: 'the two falling' means that here there are two differences together: the difference in longitudes and the difference in latitudes, but... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage explains that for the Moon's sphere, the Earth's radius is perceptible in magnitude, leading to a measurable difference between the true and apparent positions of the Moon, called 'parallax' (اختلاف المنظر). The author begins by quoting the earlier statement that the Earth has a perceptible magnitude relative to the Moon's sphere. Consequently, the apparent half of the Moon's sphere is less than the true half (visible from Earth's center). The true position is determined by the line from the Earth's center through the Moon's center; the apparent position is determined by the line from the observer's eye on the Earth's surface through the Moon's center. These two lines intersect at the Moon's center at an acute angle, and because the Moon is close, that angle is large enough to cause a perceptible difference. This difference is measured along the circle of altitude and depends on the angle: larger angle gives greater difference. This variation is called 'parallax.' The line from the observer always ends closer to the horizon; thus the true position is always higher (farther from the horizon) than the apparent position. When the Moon is at the zenith, the two lines coincide, so there is no parallax. Otherwise, the true position is nearer the zenith and farther from the horizon. The parallax in altitude can also affect the Moon's longitude and latitude. The author then glosses the commentary: the difference in longitude and latitude arises because the true positions (measured from Earth's center) differ from apparent positions (measured from the observer). For the Moon's sphere, this means that the rising of a part does not coincide with the setting of its opposite; rather, it lags. The commentary further explains the geometry: the line from the observer originates above the line from the center, so its endpoint is always closer to the horizon. Regarding circles of latitude, the explanation distinguishes between a difference in longitude alone (when the circle of altitude coincides with the ecliptic) and a combined difference in longitude and latitude (when the circles of latitude are considered). The passage ends with a comment that there are two differences together, but the text is cut off.

Definition of Parallax and Its Effect on Altitude
Al-Jurjani defines parallax and explains its effect on the moon's altitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of parallax (اختلاف المنظر), its cause due to the intersection of lines from Earth's center and the observer, and how it affects the moon's apparent altitude.

Core Idea
Parallax is the apparent shift in position due to different viewpoints, causing true and apparent altitudes to differ.

Al-Jurjani begins by defining the true and apparent positions of the moon, introducing the concept of parallax. He explains that the difference arises from the geometric intersection of lines from the Earth's center and the observer, and that this difference is noticeable due to the moon's proximity. This lesson will establish the foundational understanding of parallax and its effect on altitude measurement.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the distinction between true and apparent positions.
Focus on the two lines: one from Earth's center, one from the observer.
2
Identify the cause of difference
Understand that the two lines intersect at the moon at an acute angle, and this angle is the reason for the difference in perceived position.
The angle is significant because the moon is relatively close to Earth.
3
Examine the zenith condition
Notice that when the moon is at the zenith, the lines coincide and parallax disappears.
This is a special case that reinforces the geometric explanation.

أصغر كان أقل ، ( وهذا التفاوت يسمى اختلاف المنظر. ولا شك أن الخطين المتقاطعين ما كان مبدؤه فوق يقع منتهاه تحت فالخط الخارج من الباصرة ) منتهاه ( أقرب إلى الأفق دائماً فموضعه الحقيقي فوق المرئي أبداً) فلو فرض أن القمر على سمت الرأس لم يكن له اختلاف منظر لاتحاد الخطين حينئذ . وإذا لم يكن عليه كان له ذلك ويكون موضعه الحقيقي أبعد عن الأفق وأقرب إلى سمت الرأس لما عرفت . ثم إن هذا الاختلاف الواقع في دائرة الارتفاع قد يقتضي اختلاقاً في طول الكوكب وعرضه. فإنا إذا فرضنا دائرتي عرض تمران بطرفي الخطين المذكورين فهما إذا وقعتا على نقطتين من فلك البروج كان ما بينهما اختلافاً بين الطولين الحقيقي والمرئي، وإذا اختلف القوسان الواقعتان منهما بين طرفي الخطين وبين فلك البروج كان مقدار التفاضل بينهما اختلاف العرضين الحقيقي والمرئي . وإذا كان الكوكب على وسط سماء الرؤية لم يكن له باختلاف منظره اختلاف في الطول لأن الدائرتين متحدتان حينئذ فتتحد النقطتان على فلك البروج، ويكون حينئذ اختلاف منظره هو العرض بعينه. وإذا لم يكن الكوكب عليها كان له اختلاف في الطول على ما أشار إليه بقوله : ( فإذا اعتبر) أي القمر ( نازلاً) والصواب أن يقال : صاعداً بأن يكون في الربع الشرقي من وسط سماء الرؤية ( كان ) الطول ( المرئي زائداً على ما ينزل ) والصحيح أن يقال على الحقيقي ) بذلك القدر من فلك البروج الذي يقتضيه اختلاف منظره من دائرة الارتفاع على ما صورناه ( فيزداد ) ذلك القدر ( على ) الطول (الحقيقي . قوله : ( وإذا كان الكوكب إلخ) أي على توسع المطالع كانت هذه الدائرة التي من دوائر العرض دائرة ارتفاع الكوكب حينئذ فلا يكون له اختلاف الطول لأن القطعتين اللتين هما موضعان الطول يتحدان على فلك البروج وذلك أن العرضين المارين بطرفي الخطين المذكورين منطبقتان حينئذ على دائرة ارتفاع الكوكب التي هي دائرة الرؤية فتقطعان منطقة البروج على نقطة هي موضع الكوكب في الطول . قوله : ( قد يقتضي إلخ) أي يقتضي اقتضاء جزئياً أن يكون موضعا الكواكب في الطول والعرض الحقيقيان أي المقيسان إلى مركز العالم مخالفين لموضعيهما المرئيين المقيسان إلى سطح الأرض الذي هو موضع الإبصار . الحقيقي منه فلا يلزم أن يكون طلوع كل جزء مع غروب نظيره من فلك القمر أقل من النصف الحقيقي . فلا يلزم أن يكون طلوع كل جزء مع غروب نظيره بل يكون طلوعه بعد غروب نظيره . قوله : فالخط الخارج من الباصرة إلخ) يعني أن الخط الخارج من الباصرة كان مبدؤه فوق مبدأ الخط الخارج من مركز العالم فيكون منتهى الخط الخارج عن الباصرة أقرب إلى الأفق دائما . وقوله : فوق المرئي أي فوق الموضع المرئي . قوله : ( فإنا إذا فرضنا دائرتي (عرض قد عرفت أن دائرة العرض هي دائرة مارة بقطبي مركز القمر أم لا، ثم لا يخفى بيان الاختلاف في الطول لا يحتاج إلى اعتبار دائرتي عرض وإنما الذي يحتاج إلى اعتبارهما معاً هو بيان الاختلاف في العرض. وقوله فهما أي طرفا الخطين المذكورين. وقوله : إذا وقعتا على نقطتين من فلك البروج فحينئذ تكون دائرة الارتفاع منطبقة على منطقة البروج وقوله : كان ما بينهما أي بين النقطتين المذكورتين أو بين طرفي الخطين المذكورين أو بين الدائرتين المذكورتين والمآل واحد بمعنى أنه حينئذ كان بينهما اختلاف في الطولين فقط. ولم يوجد هناك عرض أصلاً فضلاً عن أن يوجد هناك اختلاف في العرضين. وقوله : الواقعتان يعني أن هاهنا اختلافين معاً هما اختلاف الطولين واختلاف العرضين، لكن مقدار التفاضل بين الدائرتين هو اختلاف العرضين دون اختلاف الطولين وتوضيح المقام هاهنا أن الطول الحقيقي والطول المرئي هما قوسان من منطقة البروج مع الأفق في المغرب، ومنتهى الطول الحقيقي هو الموضع الحقيقي ومنتهى الطول المرئي هو الموضع المرئي، وإن العرض الحقيقي والمرئي هما قوسان من دائرتي العرض مبدؤهما أقرب جزء من أجزاء منطقة البروج ومنتهى العرض الحقيقي هو الموضع الحقيقي ومنتهى العرض المرئي هو الموضع المرئي. وقوله : على وسط سماء الرؤية وهي الدائرة المارة بقطبي الأفق وبقطبي المنطقة كما عرفت . وقوله : لأن الدائرتين متحدتان حينئذ يعني أن دائرتي العرض متحدتان وكذا دائرة ارتفاع القمر متحدة معهما فهاهنا كانت أربع دوائر متحدة بعضها مع بعض، وهي الدائرة المذكورة آنفاً. وقوله : فتتحد النقطتان على فلك البروج أي لا يكون حينئذ على منطقة البروج نقطتان من دائرتي العرضي بحيث يكون لكل واحدة منهما وضع مخصوص فلم يتصور هناك طول أصلاً. بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن الكوكب على وسط السماء كما ذكره . قوله : (والصواب أن يقال صاعداً ولعل المصنف نظر إلى الحركة الذاتية لحامل تدوير القمر فإن هذه الحركة الذاتية لما كانت على التوالي لزم أن يكون القمر بهذه الحركة نازلاً في الطرف الشرقي وصاعداً في الطرف الغربي. وقوله : فيكون الحاصل أي فيكون المجموع الحاصل من الطول الحقيقي ومن الزيادة الطول المرئي .
The First Observatory - Third Section: Fifth Objective: The Magnitude of the Sense between the Earth and the Spheres. That circle (and it is what is in the circle of altitude on the surface of the highest sphere) (so the true position of the moon) is that to which the line extending from the center of the Earth passing through the center of the moon ends (unlike the apparent position) of it in it, which is that to which the line extending from the observer passing through its center ends. The two positions differ in sense (because of the mentioned intersection), which is their intersection at the center of the moon at an acute angle from both sides as previously mentioned, but it is considered in sense here due to the proximity of the moon which necessitates the size of the angle. And that difference is in the circle of altitude (according to the angle of intersection: whenever the angle is larger, the difference between the two positions is greater, and whenever it is smaller, it is less). (And this variation is called parallax. There is no doubt that the two intersecting lines—their beginning is above, their end falls below—so the line extending from the observer) its end (is always closer to the horizon, so its true position is always above the apparent one). If the moon were at the zenith, it would have no parallax due to the union of the two lines then. And if it is not at the zenith, it has that, and its true position is farther from the horizon and closer to the zenith, as you have known. Then this difference occurring in the circle of altitude may necessitate a difference in the longitude and latitude of the celestial body. For if we assume two circles of latitude passing through the extremities of the two mentioned lines, then if they fall on two points of the ecliptic, what is between them is a difference between the true and apparent longitudes. And if the two arcs of them that lie between the extremities of the two lines and the ecliptic differ, then the amount of difference between them is the difference between the true and apparent latitudes. And if the celestial body is on the meridian of the apparent sky, it has no difference in its longitudinal parallax because the two circles are then united, so the two points coincide on the ecliptic, and then its parallax is precisely the latitude. And if the celestial body is not on it, it has a difference in longitude, as indicated by his saying: (If it is considered) i.e., the moon (descending) – and the correct thing is to say ascending, if it is in the eastern quadrant from the meridian of the apparent sky – (then) the (apparent longitude is greater than what descends) – and the correct thing is to say over the true longitude – by that amount from the ecliptic which its parallax from the circle of altitude necessitates, as we have depicted, (so that amount increases) over the (true) longitude. His saying: (And if the celestial body so on) i.e., on the expansion of the ascensions, this circle which is among the circles of latitude is the circle of altitude of the celestial body then, so it has no difference in longitude because the two segments which are the two longitudinal positions coincide on the ecliptic. That is because the two latitudes passing through the extremities of the two mentioned lines are then superimposed on the circle of altitude of the celestial body, which is the circle of vision, so they cut the zodiacal belt at a point that is the position of the celestial body in longitude. His saying: (It may necessitate etc.) i.e., it necessitates, in a partial way, that the positions of the celestial bodies in the true longitude and latitude, i.e., measured relative to the center of the world, are different from their apparent positions measured relative to the surface of the Earth, which is the place of sight. From the true one, so it does not necessarily follow that the rising of every part is accompanied by the setting of its counterpart from the sphere of the moon less than half of the true one. So it does not necessarily follow that the rising of every part is accompanied by the setting of its counterpart; rather, its rising occurs after the setting of its counterpart. His saying: (For the line extending from the observer etc.) means that the line extending from the observer had its beginning above the beginning of the line extending from the center of the world, so the end of the line from the observer is always closer to the horizon. And his saying: 'above the apparent' i.e., above the apparent position. His saying: (For if we assume two circles of latitude) – you have known that the circle of latitude is a circle passing through the poles of the center of the moon or not. Then it is not hidden that the explanation of the difference in longitude does not need to consider two circles of latitude; rather, what needs to consider them together is the explanation of the difference in latitude. And his saying: 'them both' i.e., the two extremities of the two mentioned lines. And his saying: 'if they fall on two points of the ecliptic, then the circle of altitude is superimposed on the zodiacal belt' – and his saying: 'what is between them' i.e., between the two mentioned points, or between the two extremities of the two mentioned lines, or between the two mentioned circles, and the outcome is one, meaning that then there is between them a difference only in the two longitudes, and there is no latitude at all, let alone a difference in the two latitudes. And his saying: 'the two falling' means that here there are two differences together, which are the difference in the two longitudes and the difference in the two latitudes, but the amount of difference between the two circles is the difference in the two latitudes without the difference in the two longitudes. The clarification of the position here is that the true longitude and the apparent longitude are two arcs from the zodiacal belt from the western horizon, and the end of the true longitude is the true position, and the end of the apparent longitude is the apparent position; and the true and apparent latitudes are two arcs from the two circles of latitude, their beginning is the nearest part of the parts of the zodiacal belt, and the end of the true latitude is the true position, and the end of the apparent latitude is the apparent position. And his saying: 'on the meridian of the apparent sky' – and it is the circle passing through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the zodiac as you have known. And his saying: 'because the two circles are then united' means that the two circles of latitude are united, and likewise the circle of altitude of the moon is united with them, so here there are four circles united one with another, and they are the circle mentioned above. And his saying: 'so the two points coincide on the ecliptic' i.e., then there are no two points on the zodiacal belt from the two circles of latitude such that each one has a specific position; thus there is no longitude at all there. Unlike when the celestial body is not on the meridian, as he mentioned. His saying: (And the correct thing is to say ascending) – and perhaps the author looked at the proper motion of the carrier of the epicycle of the moon, for since this proper motion is in sequence, it necessarily follows that the moon by this motion is descending in the eastern side and ascending in the western side. And his saying: 'so the result is' i.e., the total resulting from the true longitude plus the increase is the apparent longitude.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a definition and detailed explanation of parallax (اختلاف المنظر) as it applies to the moon, within the context of astronomical calculations relevant to kalam cosmology. The author, al-Jurjani, first defines the true position (الموضع الحقيقي) as the point where a line from the Earth’s center to the moon ends, and the apparent position (الموضع المرئي) as the point where a line from the observer’s eye to the moon ends. He states that the two positions differ in perception because these two lines intersect at the moon at an acute angle, and this angle is significant due to the moon’s proximity, causing a noticeable difference in the circle of altitude. This difference is called parallax, and it varies with the angle of intersection: the larger the angle, the greater the difference. He then explains that the line from the observer always ends closer to the horizon, so the true position is always above the apparent one. If the moon is at the zenith, the two lines coincide and there is no parallax; otherwise, the true position is farther from the horizon and closer to the zenith. Next, he introduces that this altitude difference can lead to differences in the moon’s longitude (طول) and latitude (عرض). He explains that if two circles of latitude pass through the ends of the two lines, then if these circles touch the ecliptic at two points, the difference between those points is the difference in longitude; if the arcs between the ends of the lines and the ecliptic differ, that difference is the difference in latitude. He notes special cases: when the moon is on the meridian (وسط سماء الرؤية), there is no longitudinal difference because the circles coincide, and the parallax is exactly the latitude difference. When not on the meridian, there is a longitudinal difference, and he clarifies a correction in terminology: the moon should be considered ascending (صاعداً) rather than descending (نازلاً) when in the eastern quadrant. The commentary then delves into detailed clarifications of the original text (matn), explaining the meaning of phrases, the geometry involved, and the relationship between the circles of altitude and latitude. He emphasizes that the true positions are measured from the Earth’s center, while apparent positions are measured from the observer’s surface, leading to differences in rising and setting times. He also explains that when the moon is on the meridian, the circles of latitude and altitude are all united, so there is no longitudinal difference. The passage overall provides a thorough astronomical explanation of lunar parallax, integrating it into the broader kalam framework.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the true and apparent positions of the moon are always the same.
Correction: The passage explains that they differ due to the different starting points of the lines, except at the zenith where they coincide.

Checkpoint
What happens to parallax when the moon is at the zenith?
A) It is maximized.
B) It is zero.
C) It is the same as at the horizon.
D) It is doubled.

Key Takeaway
Parallax is the difference between the true and apparent positions of the moon caused by the difference in viewpoint between Earth's center and the observer, and it disappears when the moon is at the zenith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the difference between your true spiritual state (known to Allah) and what you appear to be to others, what 'parallax'—what gap—exists? Are you closer to the 'true' or the 'apparent'?
?
The passage teaches that the true position is always above the apparent, and only at the zenith do they align. In what area of your life are you settling for the 'apparent' (close to the horizon) instead of striving for the 'true' (closer to the zenith)? What concrete step will you take today to realign your actions with your true beliefs?
?
Parallax occurs because the observer's viewpoint is different from the Earth's center. Whose perspective do you typically adopt when evaluating your own deeds—your own limited view, or the 'center' of Islamic truth? How might you shift your viewpoint to see more accurately?

intermediate
10 min
Effect of Parallax on Longitude and Latitude
Al-Jurjani explains how parallax in altitude causes differences in longitude and latitude, with special cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how parallax in altitude leads to differences in the moon's longitude and latitude, and how these differences behave when the moon is on the meridian.

Core Idea
Parallax not only affects altitude but also causes discrepancies in longitude and latitude, except on the meridian where only latitude is affected.

Having established the basic definition of parallax, al-Jurjani now extends the analysis to show how the same geometric difference translates into discrepancies in the moon's longitude and latitude coordinates. He explains the role of circles of latitude and the ecliptic, and highlights the special case when the moon is on the meridian.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on longitude and latitude
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand how altitude difference affects longitude and latitude.
Focus on the role of circles of latitude passing through the ends of the two lines.
2
Understand the meridian case
Notice that when the moon is on the meridian, the circles unite and only latitude is affected.
This is a key special case.
3
Grasp the ascending/descending correction
Al-Jurjani corrects the matin's wording: the moon should be considered ascending when in the eastern quadrant.
This clarifies the direction of the longitude difference.

اعتبارهما معاً هو بيان الاختلاف في العرض. وقوله فهما أي طرفا الخطين المذكورين. وقوله : إذا وقعتا على نقطتين من فلك البروج فحينئذ تكون دائرة الارتفاع منطبقة على منطقة البروج وقوله : كان ما بينهما أي بين النقطتين المذكورتين أو بين طرفي الخطين المذكورين أو بين الدائرتين المذكورتين والمآل واحد بمعنى أنه حينئذ كان بينهما اختلاف في الطولين فقط. ولم يوجد هناك عرض أصلاً فضلاً عن أن يوجد هناك اختلاف في العرضين. وقوله : الواقعتان يعني أن هاهنا اختلافين معاً هما اختلاف الطولين واختلاف العرضين، لكن مقدار التفاضل بين الدائرتين هو اختلاف العرضين دون اختلاف الطولين وتوضيح المقام هاهنا أن الطول الحقيقي والطول المرئي هما قوسان من منطقة البروج مع الأفق في المغرب، ومنتهى الطول الحقيقي هو الموضع الحقيقي ومنتهى الطول المرئي هو الموضع المرئي، وإن العرض الحقيقي والمرئي هما قوسان من دائرتي العرض مبدؤهما أقرب جزء من أجزاء منطقة البروج ومنتهى العرض الحقيقي هو الموضع الحقيقي ومنتهى العرض المرئي هو الموضع المرئي. وقوله : على وسط سماء الرؤية وهي الدائرة المارة بقطبي الأفق وبقطبي المنطقة كما عرفت . وقوله : لأن الدائرتين متحدتان حينئذ يعني أن دائرتي العرض متحدتان وكذا دائرة ارتفاع القمر متحدة معهما فهاهنا كانت أربع دوائر متحدة بعضها مع بعض، وهي الدائرة المذكورة آنفاً. وقوله : فتتحد النقطتان على فلك البروج أي لا يكون حينئذ على منطقة البروج نقطتان من دائرتي العرضي بحيث يكون لكل واحدة منهما وضع مخصوص فلم يتصور هناك طول أصلاً. بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن الكوكب على وسط السماء كما ذكره . قوله : (والصواب أن يقال صاعداً ولعل المصنف نظر إلى الحركة الذاتية لحامل تدوير القمر فإن هذه الحركة الذاتية لما كانت على التوالي لزم أن يكون القمر بهذه الحركة نازلاً في الطرف الشرقي وصاعداً في الطرف الغربي. وقوله : فيكون الحاصل أي فيكون المجموع الحاصل من الطول الحقيقي ومن الزيادة الطول المرئي .
The First Observatory - Third Section: Fifth Objective: The Magnitude of the Sense between the Earth and the Spheres. That circle (and it is what is in the circle of altitude on the surface of the highest sphere) (so the true position of the moon) is that to which the line extending from the center of the Earth passing through the center of the moon ends (unlike the apparent position) of it in it, which is that to which the line extending from the observer passing through its center ends. The two positions differ in sense (because of the mentioned intersection), which is their intersection at the center of the moon at an acute angle from both sides as previously mentioned, but it is considered in sense here due to the proximity of the moon which necessitates the size of the angle. And that difference is in the circle of altitude (according to the angle of intersection: whenever the angle is larger, the difference between the two positions is greater, and whenever it is smaller, it is less). (And this variation is called parallax. There is no doubt that the two intersecting lines—their beginning is above, their end falls below—so the line extending from the observer) its end (is always closer to the horizon, so its true position is always above the apparent one). If the moon were at the zenith, it would have no parallax due to the union of the two lines then. And if it is not at the zenith, it has that, and its true position is farther from the horizon and closer to the zenith, as you have known. Then this difference occurring in the circle of altitude may necessitate a difference in the longitude and latitude of the celestial body. For if we assume two circles of latitude passing through the extremities of the two mentioned lines, then if they fall on two points of the ecliptic, what is between them is a difference between the true and apparent longitudes. And if the two arcs of them that lie between the extremities of the two lines and the ecliptic differ, then the amount of difference between them is the difference between the true and apparent latitudes. And if the celestial body is on the meridian of the apparent sky, it has no difference in its longitudinal parallax because the two circles are then united, so the two points coincide on the ecliptic, and then its parallax is precisely the latitude. And if the celestial body is not on it, it has a difference in longitude, as indicated by his saying: (If it is considered) i.e., the moon (descending) – and the correct thing is to say ascending, if it is in the eastern quadrant from the meridian of the apparent sky – (then) the (apparent longitude is greater than what descends) – and the correct thing is to say over the true longitude – by that amount from the ecliptic which its parallax from the circle of altitude necessitates, as we have depicted, (so that amount increases) over the (true) longitude. His saying: (And if the celestial body so on) i.e., on the expansion of the ascensions, this circle which is among the circles of latitude is the circle of altitude of the celestial body then, so it has no difference in longitude because the two segments which are the two longitudinal positions coincide on the ecliptic. That is because the two latitudes passing through the extremities of the two mentioned lines are then superimposed on the circle of altitude of the celestial body, which is the circle of vision, so they cut the zodiacal belt at a point that is the position of the celestial body in longitude. His saying: (It may necessitate etc.) i.e., it necessitates, in a partial way, that the positions of the celestial bodies in the true longitude and latitude, i.e., measured relative to the center of the world, are different from their apparent positions measured relative to the surface of the Earth, which is the place of sight. From the true one, so it does not necessarily follow that the rising of every part is accompanied by the setting of its counterpart from the sphere of the moon less than half of the true one. So it does not necessarily follow that the rising of every part is accompanied by the setting of its counterpart; rather, its rising occurs after the setting of its counterpart. His saying: (For the line extending from the observer etc.) means that the line extending from the observer had its beginning above the beginning of the line extending from the center of the world, so the end of the line from the observer is always closer to the horizon. And his saying: 'above the apparent' i.e., above the apparent position. His saying: (For if we assume two circles of latitude) – you have known that the circle of latitude is a circle passing through the poles of the center of the moon or not. Then it is not hidden that the explanation of the difference in longitude does not need to consider two circles of latitude; rather, what needs to consider them together is the explanation of the difference in latitude. And his saying: 'them both' i.e., the two extremities of the two mentioned lines. And his saying: 'if they fall on two points of the ecliptic, then the circle of altitude is superimposed on the zodiacal belt' – and his saying: 'what is between them' i.e., between the two mentioned points, or between the two extremities of the two mentioned lines, or between the two mentioned circles, and the outcome is one, meaning that then there is between them a difference only in the two longitudes, and there is no latitude at all, let alone a difference in the two latitudes. And his saying: 'the two falling' means that here there are two differences together, which are the difference in the two longitudes and the difference in the two latitudes, but the amount of difference between the two circles is the difference in the two latitudes without the difference in the two longitudes. The clarification of the position here is that the true longitude and the apparent longitude are two arcs from the zodiacal belt from the western horizon, and the end of the true longitude is the true position, and the end of the apparent longitude is the apparent position; and the true and apparent latitudes are two arcs from the two circles of latitude, their beginning is the nearest part of the parts of the zodiacal belt, and the end of the true latitude is the true position, and the end of the apparent latitude is the apparent position. And his saying: 'on the meridian of the apparent sky' – and it is the circle passing through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the zodiac as you have known. And his saying: 'because the two circles are then united' means that the two circles of latitude are united, and likewise the circle of altitude of the moon is united with them, so here there are four circles united one with another, and they are the circle mentioned above. And his saying: 'so the two points coincide on the ecliptic' i.e., then there are no two points on the zodiacal belt from the two circles of latitude such that each one has a specific position; thus there is no longitude at all there. Unlike when the celestial body is not on the meridian, as he mentioned. His saying: (And the correct thing is to say ascending) – and perhaps the author looked at the proper motion of the carrier of the epicycle of the moon, for since this proper motion is in sequence, it necessarily follows that the moon by this motion is descending in the eastern side and ascending in the western side. And his saying: 'so the result is' i.e., the total resulting from the true longitude plus the increase is the apparent longitude.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a definition and detailed explanation of parallax (اختلاف المنظر) as it applies to the moon, within the context of astronomical calculations relevant to kalam cosmology. The author, al-Jurjani, first defines the true position (الموضع الحقيقي) as the point where a line from the Earth’s center to the moon ends, and the apparent position (الموضع المرئي) as the point where a line from the observer’s eye to the moon ends. He states that the two positions differ in perception because these two lines intersect at the moon at an acute angle, and this angle is significant due to the moon’s proximity, causing a noticeable difference in the circle of altitude. This difference is called parallax, and it varies with the angle of intersection: the larger the angle, the greater the difference. He then explains that the line from the observer always ends closer to the horizon, so the true position is always above the apparent one. If the moon is at the zenith, the two lines coincide and there is no parallax; otherwise, the true position is farther from the horizon and closer to the zenith. Next, he introduces that this altitude difference can lead to differences in the moon’s longitude (طول) and latitude (عرض). He explains that if two circles of latitude pass through the ends of the two lines, then if these circles touch the ecliptic at two points, the difference between those points is the difference in longitude; if the arcs between the ends of the lines and the ecliptic differ, that difference is the difference in latitude. He notes special cases: when the moon is on the meridian (وسط سماء الرؤية), there is no longitudinal difference because the circles coincide, and the parallax is exactly the latitude difference. When not on the meridian, there is a longitudinal difference, and he clarifies a correction in terminology: the moon should be considered ascending (صاعداً) rather than descending (نازلاً) when in the eastern quadrant. The commentary then delves into detailed clarifications of the original text (matn), explaining the meaning of phrases, the geometry involved, and the relationship between the circles of altitude and latitude. He emphasizes that the true positions are measured from the Earth’s center, while apparent positions are measured from the observer’s surface, leading to differences in rising and setting times. He also explains that when the moon is on the meridian, the circles of latitude and altitude are all united, so there is no longitudinal difference. The passage overall provides a thorough astronomical explanation of lunar parallax, integrating it into the broader kalam framework.

العرض
فلك البروج
دائرة العرض
وسط سماء الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That parallax only affects altitude and not longitude or latitude.
Correction: The passage explains that parallax in altitude directly leads to differences in both longitude and latitude, except on the meridian where it affects only latitude.

Checkpoint
What is true about the moon's parallax when it is on the meridian?
A) It affects both longitude and latitude.
B) It affects only longitude.
C) It affects only latitude.
D) It does not affect either.

Key Takeaway
The parallax that appears in altitude also causes discrepancies in the moon's longitude and latitude; on the meridian, only latitude is affected, while longitude difference becomes zero.
