من المرصد الأول - القسم الثالث : المقصد الثاني عشر : العناصر الأربعة والمركبات كل منهما إلى الآخر بلا وسط ( لاشتراكهما في الحرارة وإن اختلفا في اليبوسة (كما يصير الهواء ناراً في كير الحدادين بالإلحاح في النفخ مع سد المنافذ ( ثم تنطفئ ) النار ( فتصير هواء ) فهذه ست انقلابات بلا وسط بين المتشاركين في كيفية واحدة كيفيتهما ( وبعضها ) ينقلب إلى بعض آخر ( بواسطة وهو حيث يختلفان في الكيفيتين) معاً ( كالماء والنار والهواء والأرض فإنه لا ينقلب الماء ناراً ابتداء ) لشدة تخالفهما . ( نعم قد ينقلب هواء ثم ناراً بأن ينقلب ذلك الهواء إلى النار ) وعليه فقس انقلاب النار ماء وانقلاب الهواء أرضاً وعكسه وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكروه يقتضي أن تنقلب كل واحدة من الأرض والنار إلى الأخرى بلا واسطة لاشتراكهما في اليبوسة والمشهور أنه بواسطتين فالأولى أن يقال : إن كان العنصران متجاورين كان الانقلاب بغير وسط وإن كان بينهما عنصر ثالث كان بواسطة واحدة وإن توسط بينهما اثنان فلا بد من واسطتين ) وهذا كله يدل على أن هيولى العناصر الأربعة واحدة (مشتركة) بينها ( وقابلة لجميع الصور) العنصرية ( وإنما يعدها للصور) المختلفة التي هي النارية والهوائية والمائية والأرضية . ) والكيفيات الأربع المتنافية ما عرض لها من القرب والبعد بالنسبة إلى الفلك وكل ما كان أقرب إليه كان أسخن وألطف . وكلما كان أبعد كان أبرد وأكثف وقد تكلمنا على مثله مراراً ( فلا نعيده) . أي يمكن أن يقال : إن اختصاص بعض من الهيولى المشتركة بالقرب وبعضها بالبعد يحتاج إلى سبب من خارج فلا بد من الرجوع إلى المختار على أنا لا نسلم تركب الأجسام من الهيولى والصورة، ولا نسلم الانقلاب بين العناصر وما ذكروه من الأمثلة الدالة عليه يتطرق إليها احتمالات كثيرة .
From the first observation point - the third section: the twelfth objective: The four elements and compounds. Each of them changes into the other without an intermediate (due to their sharing in heat, even if they differ in dryness) (as air becomes fire in the blacksmith's bellows by persistent blowing with closing of openings, then the fire goes out and becomes air). So these are six transformations without an intermediate between those sharing in one quality, between the two of them in their quality. And some of them change into another with an intermediate, which is when they differ in both qualities together (like water and fire, and air and earth, for water does not change into fire directly due to the intensity of their opposition). Yes, it may change into air then into fire, by that air changing into fire. And similarly measure the change of fire into water and the change of air into earth and its opposite. And you are aware that what they mentioned requires that each of earth and fire change into the other without an intermediate due to their sharing in dryness, but the well-known is that it is by two intermediates. So it is better to say: if the two elements are adjacent, the change is without an intermediate; if there is a third element between them, it is by one intermediate; if two intervene between them, then two intermediates are necessary. And all of this indicates that the matter (hayula) of the four elements is one (shared) among them, and receptive to all elemental forms, and it is prepared for the different forms, which are fiery, airy, watery, and earthy. And the four mutually exclusive qualities arise from what occurs to them of nearness and distance relative to the celestial sphere. Whatever is closer to it is hotter and finer, and whatever is farther is colder and denser. And we have spoken about similar matters repeatedly, so we will not repeat it. That is, it can be said: the specialization of some of the shared matter to nearness and some to distance requires an external cause, so one must refer to the Choiser. Moreover, we do not accept the composition of bodies from matter and form, nor do we accept transformation between elements, and the examples they mention indicating it are subject to many possibilities.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the transformations among the four elements (fire, air, water, earth). The author first describes six transformations that occur without an intermediate because the two elements share one quality (e.g., air and fire share heat, so air can become fire directly; fire can become air). Then he describes transformations that require an intermediate when the two elements differ in both qualities (e.g., water and fire are opposite in heat and cold, dryness and wetness, so water cannot become fire directly but must first become air, then fire). He notes an apparent contradiction: earth and fire share dryness, so according to the principle they should transform without an intermediate, but the well-known view says it is by two intermediates. He resolves this by saying it depends on adjacency: if adjacent, no intermediate; if there is one element between, one intermediate; if two, then two intermediates. From all this, he concludes that the underlying matter (hayula) of the four elements is one and the same, receptive to all elemental forms, and differentiation arises from nearness to the celestial sphere. He then raises an objection: if the matter is one, then the difference in nearness/distance needs an external cause, leading back to God (al-Mukhtar). He also rejects the very premise of composition from matter and form and transformation between elements, stating that the examples given are open to many possibilities.

Key Terms
انقلاب (transformation)
هيولى (matter)
عناصر (elements)
كيفيات (qualities)
فلك (celestial sphere)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transformations between elements occur arbitrarily without any underlying unity or external cause.
Correction: The passage shows that transformations follow regular patterns based on shared qualities, and they all point to one underlying matter. Moreover, differences in qualities require an external cause (God), so the process is not arbitrary.

Checkpoint
How many direct transformations (without intermediate) does the author mention?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The patterns of element transformation demonstrate that all four elements share a single underlying matter (hayula), with differences in qualities arising from proximity to the celestial sphere, ultimately pointing to an external cause (God).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that differences in the material world arise from mere proximity to the celestial sphere. In your own life, what 'proximities' (to people, places, habits) are shaping your character? Are you aware of how your environment affects your inner state? What would it mean for you to actively 'draw near' to what is hotter (spiritually alive) and flee from what is colder?
?
The author ultimately questions the entire framework of matter and form, admitting uncertainty. When you study a theological or philosophical argument that seems solid, how do you handle the possibility that it might be based on flawed premises? Are you willing to say 'I do not know' when the evidence is inconclusive, or do you cling to comfortable explanations? What is at stake for your faith if you hold on to uncertain doctrines too tightly?

The Analytical Proof of the Four Elements in Compounds
The author presents the analytical proof that compounds contain the four elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analytical proof that shows every compound must contain the four elements: earth, water, air, and fire.

Core Idea
Distillation reveals earth and water; logic reveals air and fire.

This lesson examines the first of two proofs for the composition of compounds from the four elements. By observing what happens when a compound is heated in distillation apparatus, and then reasoning from necessity, the author demonstrates that all four elements are present. The argument is both empirical and rational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Focus on the overall flow of the argument before diving into details.
Notice that the passage starts with a claim and then proceeds through a series of observations and inferences.
2
Identify the empirical evidence
The proof begins with an experiment: placing a compound in a flask and alembic. Heat causes separation into aqueous and earthy parts. This shows that water and earth were present in the compound.
This is direct empirical evidence for two elements.
3
Trace the inference for air
The author infers the presence of air from the fact that the compound is not extremely dense and that the volume of separated parts does not equal the original volume. Without air, the compound would be too dense and the volumes would match.
This step uses logical necessity rather than direct observation.
4
Understand the necessity of fire
The different natures of the elements would cause the compound to disintegrate. A unifier is needed to cook and ripen the mixture, creating a temperament and specific form. That unifier can only be intense heat from fire, proving fire's presence.
Thus all four elements are established: earth and water by observation, air and fire by inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعموا أن هذه العناصر الأربعة ( هي الأركان التي تتركب منها المركبات ويثبتونه بطريق التحليل تارةً والتركيب أخرى. فالأول : أنا إذا جعلنا مركباً في القرع والإنبيق انفصل عنه أجزاء مائية و أجزاء ( أرضية ) فدل ذلك على أن هذين العنصرين كانا موجودين فيه مختلطين ففرقتهما الحرارة ) ولا شك أن ثمة ) أي في ذلك المركب ( أجزاء هوائية بها تخلخل الأجزاء الأرضية والمائية التي فيه ( وإلا لكان ) ذلك المركب ( في غاية الاندماج والرصانة ولكان ما يحصل بالتفريق) من العنصرين ( حجمه ( إذا ضم بعضه إِلى بعض ( كالذي ) كان للمركب ( عند التركيب )، فيثبت وجود الهواء فيه ( ولا شك أنها أي الأركان المذكورة الموجودة في المركب  مختلفة بالطبع يطلب كل منها حيزه الطبيعي ) وذلك يوجب التفرق) في المركب وعدم بقائه ( فلا بد ) فيه ( من جامع يفيده طبخاً ونضجاً يوجب حصول مزاج يستتبع له صورة نوعية مانعة من التفرق وما هو أي ذلك الجامع الذي يطبخ ( وينضج (إلا الحرارة الشديدة القائمة بالنار فلا بد من وجودها فيه .
They claimed that these four elements are the pillars (arkan) from which compounds are composed, and they prove it by analysis (tahlil) sometimes and by synthesis (tarkib) at other times. The first: if we place a compound in a flask and alembic (qar' wa inbiq), there separate from it aqueous parts and earthy parts. That indicates that these two elements were present in it mixed, and heat separated them. And there is no doubt that in that compound there are airy parts by which the earthy and aqueous parts are rarefied, otherwise that compound would be in utmost solidity and compactness, and the volume obtained by separation of the two elements, when some of it is added to some, would be like that which the compound had at the time of composition. Thus the existence of air in it is established. And there is no doubt that these mentioned pillars present in the compound are different in nature, each seeking its natural place, and that necessitates separation in the compound and its non-endurance. So there must be in it a unifier that gives it cooking (tabkh) and ripening (nudj) that necessitates the acquisition of a temperament (mizaj) entailing a specific form (sura naw'iyya) preventing separation. And what is that unifier which cooks and ripens except the intense heat subsisting in fire? So its existence in it is necessary.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (analysis) that compounds are composed of the four elements. By placing a compound in distillation apparatus, we observe separation into water and earth, indicating they were mixed. The presence of air is inferred because otherwise the compound would be too dense and its volume upon separation would equal the original volume, which it does not. The different natures of these elements would cause the compound to disintegrate, so a unifier is needed to cook and ripen the mixture, creating a temperament that yields a specific form holding it together. This unifier can only be the intense heat from fire, proving fire's presence. Thus the compound contains all four elements.

Key Terms
تحليل (analysis)
قرع وإنبيق (flask and alembic)
ركن (pillar)
طبخ ونضج (cooking and ripening)
مزاج (temperament)
صورة نوعية (specific form)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof relies solely on direct observation of earth and water, without need for inference.
Correction: While earth and water are observed, air and fire are inferred through logical necessity: air from density considerations, and fire from the need for a unifier.

Checkpoint
In the analytical proof, what is the evidence for the presence of air in the compound?
A) Air is visible upon distillation
B) The compound would be too dense without air and volume upon separation would equal original
C) Air is necessary for combustion
D) The compound floats in air

Key Takeaway
The analytical proof uses physical separation and logical necessity to establish that all compounds contain the four elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that without the unifier (fire), the compound would disintegrate because its parts are different by nature and seek their own places. What 'unifier' holds your own life together? Is it the heat of faith and obedience to Allah, or are you letting the different pulls of your desires separate you? Ask yourself: What would my life look like if that unifier were removed? Then commit to strengthening it today.
?
The proof uses both what is directly observed (earth and water) and what must be necessarily inferred (air and fire). In your own relationship with Allah, do you only follow what you can see and feel, or do you also act on what reason and revelation necessarily imply—even when it's unseen? What step of faith are you avoiding because you're demanding to see before you believe? Make a decision to act on one unseen truth you've been neglecting.

The Objection That Heat Separates Differents
The objector states that heat separates differents and unites similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that heat actually separates different things rather than uniting them, and why this challenges the analytical proof.

Core Idea
Heat separates differents and unites similars — the opposite of what the proof assumes.

The analytical proof claimed that heat is necessary to unite the compound. But the objector points out a basic observation: heat actually separates different substances and brings similar ones together. This contradicts the foundational premise of the proof. Let's examine this objection closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the directness of the objection.
2
Identify the claim
What does the objector say about heat's effect on differents and similars? Contrast it with the proof's assumption.
The claim is empirical: heat separates differents and unites similars.
3
Ask: how does this undermine the proof?
If heat separates differents, then it cannot be the unifier of the compound, which is made of different elements. This directly refutes the need for a fiery part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : الحرارة لا المختلفات بل تفرقها وتجمع المتماثلات ( كما مر . ( ثم الحرارة القائمة لا تؤثر في الجزء الآخر إلا بمجاورة (وله أي وللجواز بينهما ( دوام وذلك ) الجوار الدائم ( لا بد له من سبب فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك السبب سببا للاجتماع) في حال بقاء المركب ( ومانعاً من التفرق ابتداءً أي بلا توسط شيء فلا يحتاج حينئذ إلى الجزء الناري وحرارته الطابخة المؤدية إلى المزاج المستتبع للصورة النوعية الحافظة للتركيب. على أن اختلاط الرطب باليابس يفيده استمساكاً عن التفرق فلا حاجة إلى جامع آخر، وقد يقال : الهواء حار فجاز أن يكون منضجاً ( ووجود الأجزاء الهوائية ) في المركب ( مما لم يتحقق) إذ يجوز أن يكون تخلخل أجزاء المركب بوقوع الخلاء فيما بينها (وكون تلك الأجزاء) الباقية بعد التحليل (ماء أو تراباً بالحقيقة غير معلوم لجواز أن يكون التشابه في الصورة المحسوسة دون الحقيقة.
We say: heat does not unite differents, rather it separates them and unites similars, as has passed. Moreover, the subsisting heat does not affect the other part except by adjacency, and that adjacency has duration, and that permanent adjacency must have a cause. So why is it not permissible that that cause be a cause for union in the state of the compound's endurance and a preventer of separation from the beginning, i.e., without anything mediating, so then there is no need for the fiery part and its cooking heat leading to the temperament entailing the specific form preserving the composition? Moreover, mixing of the wet with the dry gives it cohesion against separation, so there is no need for another unifier. And it may be said: air is hot, so it could be the ripening agent. And the existence of airy parts in the compound is not established, for it is possible that the rarefaction of the compound's parts is by the occurrence of void between them. And that those remaining parts after analysis are really water or earth is unknown, for it is possible that the similarity is in the sensible form, not in reality.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents objections to the analytical proof. First, the objector argues that heat actually separates different things and unites similar ones, contrary to the claim that it unites the compound. Second, even if heat is present, its effect on other parts is through adjacency, and that adjacency itself needs a cause; perhaps that cause is what holds the compound together, not heat. Third, the mixing of wet and dry can provide cohesion without an external unifier. Fourth, air is hot and could serve as the ripening agent, so fire is not necessary. Fifth, the existence of airy parts is not proven; rarefaction could be due to void. Finally, the separated parts after analysis may not be truly water or earth but only resemble them in form. So the analytical proof is not conclusive.

Key Terms
تفرق (separation)
اجتماع (union)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what does heat do to different things?
A) It unites them
B) It separates them
C) It transforms them
D) It destroys them

Key Takeaway
The analytical proof's claim that heat unites the compound is not universally true; heat can also separate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a force or principle always works one way, only to discover an exception that forces you to rethink your entire framework? How did you respond?
?
This objection hinges on empirical observation challenging a deductive claim. In your own beliefs, are you more swayed by logical arguments or observable facts? What would it take for you to abandon a cherished assumption when faced with counterevidence?

The Objection About Adjacency and Duration
The objector argues that the cause of adjacency, not heat, may be the true unifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the effect of heat via adjacency and duration requires a separate cause, which might be the true unifier.

Core Idea
Adjacency and duration need a cause — maybe that cause, not heat, holds the compound together.

Even if heat is present, its effect on other parts is limited to adjacency and time. But that adjacency and duration themselves require a cause. The objector suggests that this cause could be what actually unites the compound, rendering heat unnecessary. Let's see the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Notice the logical progression: heat affects by adjacency, adjacency has duration, duration needs a cause.
2
Identify the key points
What are the conditions for heat's effect? What does the objector conclude about the cause of adjacency?
The cause of adjacency could be the real unifier, not the heat itself.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the cause of adjacency is sufficient for union, then the fiery part with its heat becomes unnecessary.

Wet and Dry Cohesion as an Alternative Unifier
The objector states that wet-dry mixture provides cohesion, obviating a separate unifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the mixing of wet and dry elements provides sufficient cohesion, eliminating the need for a separate unifier.

Core Idea
Mixing wet and dry gives cohesion — no need for fire to hold the compound together.

The objector offers a simple alternative: when wet and dry are mixed, they naturally cohere. This cohesion prevents separation without requiring an external agent like fire. This objection directly challenges the necessity of a fiery part.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
The passage is one sentence. Read it and the translation carefully.
2
Identify the alternative explanation
What does the objector claim about wet and dry mixing? How does it provide cohesion?
Mixing wet and dry gives the compound cohesion against separation.
3
Consider the implications
If wet-dry mixing is sufficient, then the analytical proof's need for a fiery unifier is unnecessary.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : الحرارة لا المختلفات بل تفرقها وتجمع المتماثلات ( كما مر . ( ثم الحرارة القائمة لا تؤثر في الجزء الآخر إلا بمجاورة (وله أي وللجواز بينهما ( دوام وذلك ) الجوار الدائم ( لا بد له من سبب فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك السبب سببا للاجتماع) في حال بقاء المركب ( ومانعاً من التفرق ابتداءً أي بلا توسط شيء فلا يحتاج حينئذ إلى الجزء الناري وحرارته الطابخة المؤدية إلى المزاج المستتبع للصورة النوعية الحافظة للتركيب. على أن اختلاط الرطب باليابس يفيده استمساكاً عن التفرق فلا حاجة إلى جامع آخر، وقد يقال : الهواء حار فجاز أن يكون منضجاً ( ووجود الأجزاء الهوائية ) في المركب ( مما لم يتحقق) إذ يجوز أن يكون تخلخل أجزاء المركب بوقوع الخلاء فيما بينها (وكون تلك الأجزاء) الباقية بعد التحليل (ماء أو تراباً بالحقيقة غير معلوم لجواز أن يكون التشابه في الصورة المحسوسة دون الحقيقة.
We say: heat does not unite differents, rather it separates them and unites similars, as has passed. Moreover, the subsisting heat does not affect the other part except by adjacency, and that adjacency has duration, and that permanent adjacency must have a cause. So why is it not permissible that that cause be a cause for union in the state of the compound's endurance and a preventer of separation from the beginning, i.e., without anything mediating, so then there is no need for the fiery part and its cooking heat leading to the temperament entailing the specific form preserving the composition? Moreover, mixing of the wet with the dry gives it cohesion against separation, so there is no need for another unifier. And it may be said: air is hot, so it could be the ripening agent. And the existence of airy parts in the compound is not established, for it is possible that the rarefaction of the compound's parts is by the occurrence of void between them. And that those remaining parts after analysis are really water or earth is unknown, for it is possible that the similarity is in the sensible form, not in reality.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents objections to the analytical proof. First, the objector argues that heat actually separates different things and unites similar ones, contrary to the claim that it unites the compound. Second, even if heat is present, its effect on other parts is through adjacency, and that adjacency itself needs a cause; perhaps that cause is what holds the compound together, not heat. Third, the mixing of wet and dry can provide cohesion without an external unifier. Fourth, air is hot and could serve as the ripening agent, so fire is not necessary. Fifth, the existence of airy parts is not proven; rarefaction could be due to void. Finally, the separated parts after analysis may not be truly water or earth but only resemble them in form. So the analytical proof is not conclusive.

Key Terms
رطب ويابس (wet and dry)
استمساك (cohesion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what does mixing wet and dry provide?
A) Heat
B) Cohesion
C) Separation
D) Ripeness

Key Takeaway
Natural cohesion from wet-dry mixing eliminates the need for a separate unifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector points to a simple physical fact—wet and dry stick together—to challenge a complex theory about needing a separate unifier. Where in your own belief system do you overlook plain evidence that contradicts deeply held assumptions? Are you willing to let go of an unnecessary 'unifier' in your understanding?
?
If wet and dry can hold together without an external agent, what does that teach you about the sufficiency of Allah's creation? In what relationship or community are you striving to force unity when natural cohesion is already present? How can you shift from forcing to trusting?

Objections Regarding Air, Void, and Elemental Identity
The objector presents three objections concerning air, void, and elemental identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections that air could be the ripener, that rarefaction might be due to void, and that the identity of separated parts is uncertain.

Core Idea
Air might ripen, void might cause space, and separated parts might not be what they seem — undermining the proof's assumptions.

The final set of objections target specific assumptions of the analytical proof. Perhaps air, not fire, is the ripening agent. The existence of airy parts is not proven; rarefaction could be due to void. And after analysis, the separated parts might not truly be water or earth but only resemble them. These uncertainties weaken the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
This passage contains three separate objections. Read through each carefully.
2
List the three objections
Identify each objection: (1) air as ripener, (2) void causing rarefaction, (3) uncertainty of elemental identity after analysis.
3
Consider how each undermines the proof
Each objection challenges a different assumption of the analytical proof: the necessity of fire, the existence of airy parts, and the certainty of elemental composition.

Proof by Synthesis from Plants: The Four Elements
The author presents the synthetic proof from plant growth showing the four elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the growth of plants demonstrates that all compounds are composed of four elements (earth, water, air, fire).

Core Idea
Plants need all four elements to grow, showing that all compounds are composed of them.

The author presents a second proof known as synthesis (tarkib), using the familiar example of plant growth. By examining what plants require to thrive, we can infer that they consist of four fundamental elements. This argument is then extended to animals and humans through the food chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal English translation. Notice the conditional structure and the logical chain.
2
Identify the four elements
The passage explicitly mentions earth, water, air, and heat (fire). Underline or note each element as it appears.
Heat is referred to as 'cooking heat' (harara tabikha), which corresponds to the element fire.
3
Understand the conditional reasoning
The author states that if any of the elements is missing or not as it should be, the plant spoils. This shows that all four are necessary.
The example of sowing a seed where air and sun heat do not reach illustrates the point.
4
Trace the extension to animals and humans
The author argues that because animals eat plants and humans eat animals or plants, the same composition applies to them as well.
This is a chain: plants → food for animals → blood → sperm → human.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : ( وهو التركيب ( أنه يتكون من اجتماع الماء والأرض النبات ) وذلك ظاهر ) ولا بد ) في النبات ( من هواء يتخلل) بين أجزائه (و) من ( حرارة طابخة إذ لو فقد أحدهما أو لم يكن على ما ينبغي فسد الزرع ) كما إذا ألقينا البذر في موضع لا يصل إليه الهواء وحر الشمس، أولا يكونان على ما ينبغي فإنه يفسد ولا ينبت . فدل ذلك على أن النبات مركب من الأربعة ) ومن النبات يحصل بعض الحيوان لأنه غذاؤه، ومنها يحصل الإنسان لأنه متولد من المني المتكون من الدم المتكون من الغذاء
And the second: which is synthesis (tarkib): that the plant comes into being from the joining of water and earth. That is apparent. And there must be in the plant air penetrating between its parts, and a cooking heat, for if either is missing or not as it should be, the crop is ruined. Just as if we sow the seed in a place where air and the heat of the sun do not reach it, or they are not as they should be, then it spoils and does not sprout. So that indicates that the plant is composed of the four. And from the plant comes some animals because it is their food, and from them comes man because he is generated from sperm, which is formed from blood, which is formed from food.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (synthesis) that compounds are composed of the four elements, starting with plants. Plants obviously come from water and earth. They also require air to penetrate their parts and heat to cook them; if these are lacking, the seed does not grow. Thus plants contain all four elements. Then, animals feed on plants, so they also contain these elements. Humans are generated from sperm, which comes from blood, which comes from food (plants or animals). So by extension, all compounds are composed of the four elements.

Key Terms

بذر (seed)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the synthesis proof only applies to plants, not to animals or humans.
Correction: The author explicitly extends the proof: animals eat plants, so they contain the same elements; humans are generated from food-derived materials, so they also contain them.

Checkpoint

A) Seeds do not sprout in airless conditions
B) Plants are light
C) Leaves absorb air
D) Plants produce oxygen

Key Takeaway
Plants require earth, water, air, and heat to grow, proving that they are composed of all four elements; this applies to all compounds via the food chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a compound of body and soul. Just as the plant requires water, earth, air, and heat, what are the essential 'elements' your soul needs to grow? If one is missing or 'not as it should be', what might be the result in your spiritual life? This week, identify one of those elements you have neglected and take a concrete step to restore it.
?
The proof shows that humans ultimately come from plants through food. Read the source: 'man is generated from sperm, which is formed from blood, which is formed from food.' Does this chain humble you to recognize your total dependence on other creations? How does this affect your sense of gratitude toward the Creator every time you eat?
?
The author argues that if any element is missing or not as it should be, the crop is ruined. In your own life, is there an area where you are missing a necessary 'element'—like patience, sincerity, or knowledge—that is causing ruin? What specific change will you make today to ensure all necessary elements are present and as they should be?

The Seven Layers of Elements
Al-Jurjani enumerates the seven layers of elements from pure fire to pure earth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven layers of elements in order from highest to lowest and understand why water is not a separate layer.

Core Idea
Elements are layered from pure fire to pure earth, with water combined with earth.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani details the seven layers of the elements according to Aristotelian cosmology. Starting from the pure fire adjacent to the moon's sphere down to the pure earth at the center, he describes each layer's composition and notable features. Understanding this layered structure helps clarify medieval Islamic views on the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the order of layers from highest to lowest.
The passage uses parentheses for commentary interjections.
2
Identify each layer
As you read, identify each of the seven layers mentioned. Make a mental note of their positions and compositions.
The layers are: pure fiery, mixed fiery, freezing, vaporous, naziyah, muddy, pure earthy.
3
Understand the exception about water
Notice that water is not listed as a separate layer. The passage explains why—it forms a sphere with earth.
This is a distinctive point.
4
Reflect on the final remark
The author ends by saying exhaustive inquiry into these layers is not beneficial. Consider why he might say this.
This reflects a pragmatic approach to cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طبقات العناصر سبع (أعلاها الطبقة ( النارية الصرفة ومحدبها مماس لمقعر ( فلك القمر وتحته أي تحت الأعلى المذكور طبقة نارية مخلوطة من النار ( الصرفة و الأجزاء ( الهوائية ( الحارة تتلاشى في هذه الطبقة الأدخنة المرتفعة، وتتكون فيها الكواكب ذوات الأذناب والنيازك وما يشبهها (ثم) الطبقة (الزمهريرية وهي الهواء الصرف) الذي ( يرد بمجاورة الأرض والماء ولم يصل إليه أثر انعكاس الأشعة والمشهور أن هذه الطبقة منشأ السحب والرعد والبرق والصواعق فلا تكون هواء صرفاً . ( ثم) الطبقة ( البخارية وهي الهوائية المخلوطة مع المائية ثم الطبقة ) ( النزية وهي ما فيه أرضية وهوائية ثم ) الطبقة (الطينية وهي أرضية مع مائية ثم) الطبقة الأرضية الصرفة التي هي قريبة من المركز ، ولم يعد الماء طبقة على حده لأنه مع الأرض ككرة واحدة وفي طبقات العناصر مختلفة لا فائدة في الاستقصاء عنها.
The layers of elements are seven: the highest is the pure fiery layer, and its convexity touches the concavity of the sphere of the moon; and beneath it, i.e., beneath the aforementioned highest, is a fiery layer mixed from pure fire and the hot airy parts; in this layer, the rising vapors dissolve and comets, meteors, and the like are generated; then the freezing layer, which is pure air that cools by the proximity of the earth and water and to which the effect of the reflection of rays does not reach, but the well-known opinion is that this layer is the origin of clouds, thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts, so it is not pure air; then the vaporous layer, which is the airy mixed with the watery; then the 'naziyah' layer, which contains earthiness and airiness; then the muddy layer, which is earthy with wateriness; then the pure earthy layer, which is near the center. And water was not counted as a separate layer because it is with the earth as one sphere. And in the different layers of elements there is no benefit in exhaustive inquiry.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage enumerates the seven layers of the elements according to the Aristotelian cosmology. It begins with the highest layer, pure fire, whose convex surface is contiguous with the concave surface of the moon's sphere. Beneath that is a mixed fiery layer containing hot airy parts, where vapors dissolve and celestial phenomena like comets occur. Then comes the freezing layer of pure air, which is cooled by earth and water without solar reflection; however, the common view makes it the source of weather phenomena, contradicting its purity. Next is the vaporous layer (air mixed with water), then the naziyah layer (earthy and airy), then the muddy layer (earthy and watery), and finally the pure earthy layer near the center. Water is not a separate layer because it forms a sphere with earth. The author notes that exhaustive study of these layers is not beneficial.

Key Terms
طبقات العناصر
النارية الصرفة
الزمهريرية
البخارية
النزية
الطينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Water is a separate element layer distinct from earth.
Correction: The passage states that water is not a separate layer because it forms one sphere with earth.

Checkpoint
How many layers of elements are enumerated in this passage?
A) Five
B) Six
C) Seven
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
The seven layers of elements descend from pure fire to pure earth, with water considered part of the earth's sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author notes that the freezing layer is commonly thought to be the origin of clouds and thunder, but he doubts its purity, acknowledging that common opinion may not align with deeper analysis. Is there an aspect of your deen where you have accepted a common understanding without verifying it against core texts? What would you need to examine to be sure?

The passage concludes that exhaustive inquiry into the different layers of elements has no benefit. Have you ever persisted in researching a matter of religion that yielded no fruit? How can you discern which knowledge is truly beneficial for your faith and action?

The seven layers descend from pure fire to pure earth, with mixtures in between. If you were to analogize these layers to stages of spiritual purity, which layer best describes your current state? What would it take to move even one step closer to the pure fire of sincere devotion?

Division of Compounds into Ensouled and Inanimate
Al-Jurjani introduces the division of compounds with mixture into those with souls and minerals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of compounds with mixture into ensouled (plants and animals) and minerals, and the structure of the upcoming section.

Core Idea
Compounds with mixture are either ensouled or inanimate.

In this passage, al-Jurjani sets the stage for discussing compounds that possess mixture (mizāj). He notes that such compounds form the majority, and they are divided into two fundamental categories: those endowed with a soul (vegetative or animal) and those without a soul, namely minerals. This classification organizes the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get an overall sense of the classification being made.
Notice the use of brackets indicating the commentator's additions.
2
Identify the main division
The passage divides compounds with mixture into two types: those with a soul and those without. Find where this division is stated.
The phrase 'ينقسم إلى ما له نفس... وإلى ما لا نفس له' is the key.
3
Understand the basis
The passage mentions that compounds without mixture are few, so the focus is on the majority with mixture. Why is this distinction important?
It sets the scope for the rest of the section.
4
Note the upcoming chapters
The passage ends by stating that this section contains three chapters. This prepares you for the detailed discussion that follows.
Keep this structure in mind as you proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الأول : الصور الحالة في الجسم [ القسم الرابع في المركبات التي لها مزاج وهي الأكثر ] من المركبات لأن مالا مزاج له منها قليل بالقياس إلى ماله مزاج ( وهو ) أي هذا الأكثر ( ينقسم إلى ما له نفس إما نباتية أو حيوانية ) وإلى ما لا نفس (له وهو المعدنيات ( وفيه ثلاثة فصول ) .

[The fourth section is about compounds that have mixture, and they are the majority] of compounds, because those without mixture are few in comparison to those with mixture. And this majority is divided into that which has a soul, either plant or animal, and that which has no soul, which is minerals. And it contains three chapters.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the fourth section of the first observatory, focusing on compounds that possess mixture (mizāj). The author notes that such compounds are the majority; those without mixture are rare. These majority compounds are then divided into two categories: those with a soul (either vegetative or animal) and those without a soul, i.e., minerals. The section will include three chapters.

The Form Acts on Its Own Matter First
Al-Jurjani states the principle of form acting first on its own matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a corporeal form first affects its own matter before affecting adjacent matter, and that this principle applies to all elemental qualities.

Core Idea
A form's first effect is on its own substance, then on its neighbor.

This passage introduces a fundamental principle of physical interaction: every specific form (like heat, cold, moisture, dryness) acts first on the matter it inheres in, then on the matter of adjacent bodies. This establishes the priority of self-effect before external effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the first sentence carefully. The author states that the corporeal form acts first on its own matter, then on the matter of what is adjacent.
Note that 'corporeal form' is glossed as 'specific form'.
2
Examine the example
Look at the example of fire. Fire first heats its own matter (the fiery substance) and then heats adjacent matter.
Consider how this applies to other elements like water or earth.
3
Generalize the principle
The author states that this pattern holds for all qualities (heat, cold, etc.) and all elements.
This is a general law of elemental interaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : الصورة الجسمية ) أي الصورة الحالة في الجسم التي هي مبدأ الآثار وهي الصورة النوعية ) تفعل أولاً في (مادتها التي حلّت هي فيها (ثم في مادة ما يجاورها ) . فالصورة النارية تسخن مادتها ثم مادة ما يجاورها، وكذا الحال في سائر الكيفيات وباقي العناصر ) فالمجاورة شرط للتفاعل ) الواقع بين الأجسام. ألا ترى أنّ النار لا تسخن إلا ما له وضع مخصوص وقرب معين بالنسبة إليها فإذا حصلت المجاورة بلا مماسة أمكن التفاعل بين الجسمين ) وأبلغ من ذلك ) التفاعل الحاصل بمجرد المجاورة. ( ما كان أي التفاعل الذي كان ( بالمماسة ) التي هي الغاية في المجاورة ( والمماسة إنما تكون بالسطوح و لا شك في أنه ( كلما كان السطوح أكثر كانت المماسة بها ( أتم، وذلك ) أي تكثر السطوح (إنما هو بحسب تصغر الأجزاء و إذا تحققت ما صورناه لك فنقول : ( العناصر المختلفة الكيفية ) التي هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ( إذا) تصغرت أجزاؤها جداً واختلطت اختلاطاً تاماً ( حتى حصل التماس الكامل ( بين أجزائها فعل صورة (كل) منها ( في مادة الآخر فكسرت منه سورة كيفية ) المضادة لكيفيتها ( حتى نقص ( العنصر البارد بفعل صورته قوله: فكسرت من سورة (كيفيته هاهنا احتمالان أحدهما وهو الأنسب بظاهر العبارة أن يبقى كيفية كل من العناصر الممتزجة. وإنما تنكسر سورة تلك الكيفية فقط بحيث تلتئم من الكيفيات المنكسرة كيفية متشابهة في جميع الأجزاء. وثانيهما أن ينخلع كيفية كل واحد منها ويفيض على مجموعها من المبدأ الفياض كيفية واحدة متشابهة في جميع أجزاء ذلك الممتزج ومع كل واحد من الاحتمالين كانت صورها النوعية باقية في أنفسها. هذا ثم إن الاحتمال الأول ينسب إلى الأطباء والاحتمال الثاني ينسب إلى الحكماء.
They said: The corporeal form – i.e., the form subsisting in the body, which is the principle of effects, and it is the specific form – acts first on its matter in which it is subsisting, then on the matter of what is adjacent to it. Thus the fiery form heats its matter, then the matter of what is adjacent to it, and similarly for the other qualities and the remaining elements. Therefore, contiguity is a condition for the interaction occurring between bodies. Do you not see that fire only heats what has a specific position and a certain proximity to it? So when contiguity occurs without contact, interaction between the two bodies is possible. And more powerful than that is the interaction that occurs by mere contiguity, i.e., the interaction that occurs by contact, which is the ultimate degree of contiguity. Contact only occurs by surfaces, and there is no doubt that the more surfaces there are, the more complete the contact by them. That is, the multiplication of surfaces happens only by the smallness of the parts. And when you understand what we have depicted for you, we say: The elements differing in quality – which are heat, cold, moisture, and dryness – when their parts become very small and mix completely until complete contact occurs between their parts, the form of each acts on the matter of the other, and thus the force of the quality opposite to its quality is broken, so that the cold element decreases by the action of its form. His saying: 'the force of its quality is broken' – here there are two probabilities: The first, which is more appropriate to the apparent wording, is that the quality of each of the mixed elements remains, but only the force of that quality is broken, such that from the broken qualities a uniform quality arises in all parts. The second is that the quality of each one is stripped off, and from the Bestowing Principle a single uniform quality flows over the whole aggregate in all parts of that mixture. And with each of the two probabilities, their specific forms remain in themselves. Then note that the first probability is attributed to the physicians, and the second to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage explains the mechanism of how substances interact through their forms. It states that the form (specific form) acts first on its own matter and then on adjacent matter, using the example of fire heating itself then its surroundings. Contiguity is a necessary condition for interaction; even without direct contact, interaction can occur if bodies are contiguous. However, interaction via direct contact (tamās) is stronger. Contact occurs through surfaces, and the more surfaces (i.e., the more finely divided the parts), the more complete the contact. When the elements' qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) are mixed, their forms act on each other, weakening the opposing qualities. This is illustrated by the cold element being diminished. Then the commentary discusses two interpretations of the phrase 'the force of its quality is broken'. The first interpretation holds that the original qualities remain but their force is dampened, resulting in a uniform mixture. The second holds that the original qualities are replaced by a new single quality from the divine principle. Both maintain that the specific forms persist. The first view is attributed to physicians, the second to philosophers.

Key Terms
الصورة الجسمية
الصورة النوعية
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the corporeal form act on first?
A) Its own matter
B) Adjacent matter
C) Both simultaneously
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The corporeal form first affects its own material substrate before influencing adjacent matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you try to change others (your neighbor), do you honestly examine and address the condition of your own soul (your own matter)? What would it look like today to let the form of faith first transform your own substance before you speak into someone else's life?
?
When you feel the 'heat' of anger or passion, does it first consume you before it affects others? How can you ensure that the first effect of any quality within you is on your own character, not on those around you?
?
If your actions truly begin with your own inner state, what is one habit or attitude you will consciously work on today, knowing that your influence on others depends on it?

Contiguity, Contact, and Surface Area
Al-Jurjani explains contiguity as condition and the role of surface area.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that contiguity is necessary for interaction, contact is more powerful, and smaller parts increase surface area for better contact.

Core Idea
More surfaces mean stronger contact and interaction.

Having established that forms act on adjacent matter, the author now explains the conditions for this interaction. Contiguity (neighboring) is required, even without direct touch, but contact (tamās) is more effective. The extent of contact depends on the surface area, which increases when parts are smaller.

Guided Path
1
Understand contiguity as a condition
The author states that contiguity (jīwār) is necessary for interaction between bodies. Even without direct contact, interaction can occur if bodies are adjacent.
Example: fire heats nearby air without touching it.
2
Distinguish contact from mere contiguity
Interaction via direct contact (tamās) is more powerful than mere proximity. Contact is the highest degree of contiguity.
Contact occurs by surfaces meeting.
3
Relate surface area to smallness
The more surfaces involved, the more complete the contact. This happens when parts become smaller, increasing total surface area.
Smaller particles have more surface area per volume.

Mixing Elements and Two Interpretations
Al-Jurjani explains mixing and two interpretations of quality change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how forms of different elements interact during mixing, and the two scholarly views on whether qualities remain or are replaced.

Core Idea
When elements mix, their forms break each other's opposing qualities, but their specific forms persist.

The final part of the passage deals with the actual mixing of elemental parts. When elements with opposite qualities (e.g., hot and cold) are finely divided and mixed, each form acts on the other's matter, weakening the opposing quality. The passage then presents two interpretations of what exactly happens to the qualities, with physicians and philosophers disagreeing.

Guided Path
1
Read the mixing scenario
The author describes elements with different qualities (heat, cold, etc.) being divided into fine parts and thoroughly mixed until complete contact occurs.
This results in each form acting on the matter of the other.
2
Understand the effect on qualities
The action of the form 'breaks the force' of the opposite quality, leading to a balanced mixture. The example says the cold element decreases.
The specific forms themselves are not destroyed.
3
Compare the two probabilities
First view (physicians): The original qualities remain but their force is broken, producing a uniform mixture. Second view (philosophers): The original qualities are stripped off and a new single quality is bestowed by the divine principle.
Both views agree that the specific forms continue to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson presents two views: your vices can be merely weakened (physicians) or entirely replaced by divine grace (philosophers). Which view challenges you more? ?

?
If your soul is a mixture of traits, and only God knows which ones are really yours, how would your life change if you believed that the qualities you dislike could be not just suppressed but actually replaced by a single divine quality? What would that require you to do starting today?

The Objection and Answer on the Perfectly Balanced Temperament
Al-Jurjani records and refutes the objection that a perfectly balanced temperament cannot exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection against the existence of a perfectly balanced temperament and how it is refuted by appealing to external causes and the possibility of rare occurrence.

Core Idea
The objection assumes impossibility, but the answer shows rarity—a crucial distinction.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents an objection that a perfectly balanced temperament cannot exist due to the equal resistance of its parts. He then provides a refutation showing that external causes can enable such a temperament, even if it is rare. This lesson walks through the argument and its response, clarifying a common misconception.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the premise that equal parts cannot force each other to stay together because their natural inclinations drive them apart.
Notice that the objection relies on the assumption of no external interference.
2
Identify the conclusion of the objection
The objection concludes that separation occurs before any interaction, so no temperament can form.
3
Read the answer
Pay attention to the external cause that reverses the positions of the parts, causing them to hinder each other and remain together.
This is the key move in the refutation.
4
Understand the concession and rebuttal
The answer admits such a temperament is rare and impermanent, but denies absolute impossibility.
The cause of persistence may be external, not limited to elemental dominance.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why the objection's claim of impossibility is too strong and how the answer corrects it.
This illustrates a common pattern in kalam: refuting an opponent's claim of impossibility by showing a possible scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قالوا: وإنه لا يوجد) في الخارج (إذ أجزاؤه متساوية) في الميل إلى أحيازها متقاومة (فلا يقسر بعضها بعضاً على الاجتماع لامتناع أن يغلب بعض من الأمور المتساوية المتقاومة بعضاً آخر منها، وطبائعها داعية إلى الافتراق) بالتوجه إلى أحيازها الطبيعية المختلفة (فيحصل الافتراق قبل حصول الفعل والانفعال فإنه حادث يستدعي مدة معتداً بها لأنه حركة من كيفية إلى أخرى بعيدة عنها بخلاف) الافتراق الذي يكفيه أدنى حركة مع كونه موجوداً في كل آن من زمانها (فلا يحصل بينها مزاج) لتوقفه على حصول تلك الحركة وحدوثه عند انقطاعها. (والجواب: أنه ربما تقع الأجزاء لأسباب خارجية (بحيث تكون المائلة إلى العلو) كالنار والهواء (في جهة السفل والعكس. أي وتقع الأجزاء المائلة إلى السفل كالأرض والماء في جهة العلو، فتتمانع) الأجزاء وتتقاوم لتساوي قواها في الميول وتبقى مجتمعة (فيحصل المزاج بتفاعلها. (نعم يندر وجود (ذلك) المعتدل ولا يكون باقياً مستمراً إما لسرعة التحلل أو لسرعة غلبة بعض أجزائه على بعض (وأما الامتناع فلا، كيف وبقاء الاجتماع قد يكون لمنفصل كأصل الاجتماع الذي لا بد له من مقتض سوى الأجزاء (إذ لسبب (لبقاء الاجتماع (غير منحصر في غلبة عنصر) وهو ظاهر.
They said: And it (the perfectly balanced temperament) does not exist in the external world because its parts are equal in inclination to their natural places, mutually resisting. So one part does not compel another to aggregate because it is impossible for one of the equal, mutually resisting things to overcome another. And their natures call for separation by heading to their different natural places. Thus separation occurs before the occurrence of action and passion, for they (action and passion) are an event that requires a considerable duration because they are a movement from one quality to another far from it, unlike separation which is satisfied with the least movement while it exists in every moment of its time. So no temperament occurs between them because it is dependent on the occurrence of that movement and its happening at its termination. The answer: It is possible that the parts, due to external causes, are placed such that those inclining upward (like fire and air) are in the direction of downward, and vice versa. That is, the parts inclining downward (like earth and water) are placed in the direction of upward. So they hinder each other and resist each other due to the equality of their forces in inclination, and they remain aggregated. Thus temperament occurs by their interaction. Indeed, the existence of such a balanced temperament is rare and not permanent, either due to its rapid dissolution or the rapid predominance of some of its parts over others. But impossibility is not the case; how can it be, since the persistence of aggregation may be due to something separate, like the original aggregation which must have a cause other than the parts? For the cause of persistence of aggregation is not limited to the predominance of an element. This is clear.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the existence of a perfectly balanced temperament (al-mizaj al-mu'tadil al-haqiqi). The objectors argue that such a temperament cannot exist in the external world because its constituent parts are equal in their natural inclinations toward their own spheres, and they mutually resist each other. Due to this equality, no part can overpower another to force them to stay together; rather, their natures drive them to separate, heading to their natural places. Separation occurs before any action and passion (i.e., qualitative change) can take place, because action and passion require a measurable duration, while separation happens instantly. Therefore, they conclude, no temperament can form. The answer counters that external causes can position the parts so that those tending upward (fire, air) are placed downward, and those tending downward (earth, water) are placed upward, causing them to hinder each other. With equal forces, they remain together and interact, producing a temperament. However, it is admitted that such a balanced temperament is rare and impermanent, either dissolving quickly or having one part dominate. But impossibility is denied: the persistence of aggregation may be due to an external cause beyond the parts themselves, and the cause of aggregation is not confined to the dominance of one element. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
مزاج
معتدل
أجزاء
تقاوم
اجتماع
افتراق
فعل وانفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that a perfectly balanced temperament is absolutely impossible.
Correction: The answer shows that it is merely rare and not impossible, as external causes can enable it.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the objection against the existence of a perfectly balanced temperament?
A) The parts are exactly equal in strength, so they cannot overcome each other to stay together.
B) The parts are too different to interact.
C) External forces always prevent aggregation.
D) Action and passion occur too quickly for temperament to form.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it ignores external causes and confuses rarity with impossibility.

Classification of Temperaments: Medical Balance and Eightfold Division
Al-Jurjani defines medically balanced temperament and divides all temperaments into eight categories based on four aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of medically balanced temperament and how all temperaments are classified into eight categories through four aspects and two perspectives.

Core Idea
Temperamental balance is about proportional justice, not equal parts.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces the medical concept of balanced temperament (al-mu'tadil bihasab al-tibb). Al-Jurjani explains that true balance is not absolute equality but a proportional mix suited to each being's nature. He then presents an eightfold classification of temperaments derived from considering four aspects—type, species, individual, and organ—each evaluated in relation to internal perfection and external existence. This classification provides a sophisticated framework for understanding the natural temperaments of living beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall flow. Notice the definition of 'medically balanced' and the subsequent classification.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of medically balanced
Identify that balance here means a predominance of qualities appropriate to the being, not equality. This is distinct from absolute equilibrium.
Derived from 'justice in distribution' (al-'adl), not al-ta'adul.
3
Grasp the eightfold division
Note that both the balanced and unbalanced each divide into eight categories. The eight arise from four aspects (type, species, individual, organ) each considered in two ways (internal and external).
4
Analyze the type equilibrium example
Study how type equilibrium is explained: it has a range (width) with extremes, and an optimum at the center. The external version is necessary for existence; the internal is for best perfection.
This pattern is analogized to species, individual, and organ.
5
Reflect on the classification's practical value
Consider why this classification is important for medicine and natural philosophy: it allows precise discussion of temperament variation.
It shows that balance is relative to context.

ثم قالوا: وماليس معتدلاً حقيقياً إن غلب عليه من الأجزاء في الكمية ومن الكيفيات في الشدة ما ينبغي له ويليق به في خواصه وآثاره كالحرارة الغالبة في الأسد لشجاعته، والبرودة الغالبة في الأرنب لجبنه فهو المعتدل بحسب الطب وهو موجود وليس مشتقاً من التعادل الذي هو التساوي بل من العدل في القسمة على أنه قد توفر على الممتزج من العناصر القسط اللائق به في مزاجه وإلا أي وإن لم يغلب عليه ذلك بل غلب ما لا ينبغي فغير المعتدل وكل من القسمين أي المعتدل الطبي وقسيمه ينقسم إلى ثمانية أقسام. فالمعتدل لأنه قد يعتبر بالنسبة إلى أمور أربعة النوع والصنف والشخص والعضو ويعتبر كل من هذه الأربعة بالنسبة إلى الداخل تارة وإلى الخارج أخرى فكل نوع من المركبات المزاجية مزاج لايمكن أن توجد فيه الصورة النوعية إلا معه، وليس ذلك المزاج على حد واحد لا يتعداه وإلا كان جميع أفراد النوع الواحد كالإنسان مثلا متوافقة في المزاج وما يتبعه من الخلق والخُلُق بل له عرض فيما بين الحرارة والبرودة وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة ذو طرفين إفراط وتفريط إذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك النوع فهو اعتداله النوعي وأليق أمزجته بالنسبة إلى الأنواع الخارجة عنه فالمزاج الحاصل لبدن من أبدان الناس هو اللائق به من حيث إنه إنسان دون مزاج الفرس والحمار وغيرهما وذلك لأنه المناسب لآثاره المطلوبة منه حتى إذا خرج إلى شيء من هذه الأمزجة مات. وله أي ولكل نوع أيضاً مزاج واقع فيما بين ذلك العرض أي يكون في حاق الوسط فيما بين طرفي المزاج العرضي النوعي هو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة في ذلك العرض به وبه يكون حاله فيما خلق له من صفاته وآثاره المختصة به أجود ما يتصور منه وذلك اعتداله النوعي بالنسبة إلى ما يدخل فيه من صنف أو شخص فالاعتدال النوعي المقيس إلى الخارج يحتاج إلى النوع في وجوده ويكون حاصلاً لكل فرد من أفراده على تفاوت مراتبه. والمقيس إلى الداخل يحتاج إليه النوع في أجودية كمالاته ولا يكون حاصلاً إلا لأعدل شخص أعدل صنف من ذلك النوع ولا يكون أيضاً حاصلاً له إلا في أعدل حالاته وعليه أي على ما ذكرنا من حال الاعتدال النوعي قس الثلاثة الباقية فالاعتدال الصنفي بالقياس إلى الخارج هو الذي يكون لائقاً بصنف من نوع مقيساً إلى أمزجة سائر أصنافه وله عرض ذو طرفين هو أقل من العرض النوعي إذ هو بعض منه وإذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك الصنف وبالقياس إلى الداخل هو المزاج الواقع في حاق Then they said: And what is not truly balanced: if there predominates over it from the parts in quantity and from the qualities in intensity what is appropriate and suitable for it in its properties and effects, like predominant heat in the lion for its courage, and predominant cold in the rabbit for its cowardice, then it is the balanced according to medicine. It exists and is derived not from equilibrium (al-ta'adul) which is equality, but from justice (al-'adl) in distribution, as if the mixture has received the due share of elements appropriate to its temperament. Otherwise, i.e., if it is not predominant over it by what is appropriate, but rather predominates by what is not appropriate, then it is not balanced. And each of the two categories, i.e., the medically balanced and its opposite, divides into eight divisions. For the balanced, because it may be considered relative to four matters: type (naw'), species (sinf), individual (shakhs), and organ ('udw). And each of these four is considered relative to the internal sometimes and to the external other times. So every type of compound temperaments has a temperament without which the specific form cannot exist. And that temperament is not fixed to one limit that it cannot exceed; otherwise all individuals of one type, e.g., man, would be alike in temperament and consequent character and morality. Rather, it has a width between heat and cold and between moisture and dryness with two extremes: excess and deficiency. If the temperament exits that width, it would not be that type. So that is its specific equilibrium (i'tidal naw'i). And the most appropriate of its temperaments relative to the types external to it: the temperament of a human body is appropriate to it insofar as it is human, not the temperament of a horse or donkey. That is because it is suitable for its required effects, so that if it exits to any of those other temperaments, it dies. And for each type there is also a temperament lying within that width, i.e., at the exact middle between the two extremes of the type's width. It is the most appropriate of the temperaments within that width, and through it its state in what it was created for (its specific qualities and effects) is the best possible. That is its specific equilibrium relative to what enters into it of species or individual. So the specific equilibrium measured externally is needed for the type's existence and occurs for every individual of it with varying degrees. The one measured internally is needed for the type's best perfection and occurs only for the most balanced individual of the most balanced species of that type, and also only in its most balanced states. Upon that, analogize the remaining three: the species equilibrium measured externally is that which is appropriate for a species of a type measured against the temperaments of its other species, and it has a width with two extremes that is smaller than the type width, being part of it. If it exits that, it would not be that species. Measured internally, it is the temperament at the exact middle of this width, and it is the most appropriate for the species, because through it its state is best in what it was created for. It occurs only for the most balanced individual of that species in its most balanced states, whether that species is the most balanced or not. The individual equilibrium measured externally is that which the individual needs for its continued healthy existence, and it is appropriate to it measured against the temperaments of individuals of its species. It also has a width that is part of the species width. Measured internally, it is that through which the individual is in its best states. And the organ equilibrium measured externally...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage classifies temperaments. It begins by defining the 'medically balanced' (al-mu'tadil bihasab al-tibb) as a temperament that is not absolutely equal but has a predominance of qualities appropriate to the being's characteristics and effects, like heat in the lion and cold in the rabbit. This balance is derived from justice in distribution, not from mathematical equality. The opposite is the 'not balanced' where inappropriate qualities predominate. Then it states that each of these two main categories divides into eight divisions. This eightfold division arises because the balanced (and similarly the unbalanced) can be considered relative to four aspects: type (naw'), species (sinf), individual (shakhs), and organ ('udw), and each aspect is considered in two ways: relative to the internal (what perfects the thing itself) and relative to the external (what allows it to exist among others). The passage then explains each: for type equilibrium, there is a range (width) within which the type exists; if the temperament falls outside, the type ceases. Within that range, there is an optimum at the exact middle (the most balanced) that gives the best effects. Examples are given for human type. The same logic applies to species, individual, and organ equilibria: each has an external range for existence and an internal optimum for perfection. The passage follows the order: type, species, individual, and organ, and asks the reader to analogize the remaining three from the explanation of type equilibrium.

خارج

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'medically balanced' means absolute equality of qualities.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly states it is derived from justice in distribution, not equality, and gives examples of lions (hot) and rabbits (cold) as balanced for their species.

Checkpoint
How many subdivisions arise from considering the four aspects (type, species, individual, organ) each in two ways (internal and external)?
A) Four
B) Eight
C) Sixteen
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The classification of temperaments into eight categories shows that balance is relative to the being's nature and purpose, and each level (type, species, individual, organ) has its own range and optimum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one dominant quality in your own character—something like heat or cold, courage or timidity. Is it appropriate for the roles Allah has placed you in (as a parent, friend, worker, worshipper)? How might you seek justice (not equality) in balancing this quality so it serves your purpose rather than hinders it? What concrete step will you take today to adjust that quality toward the 'just' middle?
?
You are a human being, yet your temperament is not the same as the 'best possible' human temperament. That optimum is rare and belongs to the most balanced individuals in their best states. Does that fact humble you? Or does it drive you to seek a better balance in your inner and outer life? Which state of your soul—anger, desire, intellect—is farthest from that optimum, and what will you do to bring it closer?
?
Al-Jurjani says that if a human were to exit its type's temperament width and take on the temperament of a horse or donkey, it would die. In your spiritual life, have you ever tried to 'be' something you are not—to adopt qualities that are not appropriate for your nature as a human created to know and worship Allah? What 'foreign temperament' have you been imitating, and how can you return to your own fitting balance today?
