Generic Moderation (al-Iʿtidāl al-Nawʿī)
Al-Jurjani defines generic moderation and its two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of generic moderation and its two aspects: external (shared by all individuals of a species) and internal (the most balanced temperament at the midpoint).

Core Idea
Generic moderation is a range, not a fixed point, with two aspects: existence and perfection.

This lesson introduces the concept of generic moderation, which is the temperament necessary for a species to exist. Al-Jurjani explains that it is not a single fixed point but a range between extremes, and distinguishes between the external aspect (which keeps the species alive) and the internal aspect (which enables its best possible state). Understanding this distinction is foundational for the other three types of moderation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on generic moderation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how Al-Jurjani first defines generic temperament as a range, then explains its two aspects.
Pay attention to the terms 'عرض' (range) and 'حاق الوسط' (midpoint).
2
Identify the external and internal aspects
In the passage, find where Al-Jurjani discusses the external aspect (مقيس إلى الخارج) and the internal aspect (مقيس إلى الداخل). Note how each is defined and what it requires.
The external aspect is necessary for the species' existence; the internal aspect is for its perfection.
3
Understand the instruction to apply the same logic
The passage ends with 'قس الثلاثة الباقية' — measure the remaining three types similarly. This sets up the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الثاني : في أقسام المزاج

المزاجية ( مزاج لايمكن أن توجد فيه الصورة النوعية إلا معه، وليس ذلك المزاج على حد واحد لا يتعداه وإلا كان جميع أفراد النوع الواحد كالإنسان مثلا متوافقة في المزاج وما يتبعه من الخلق والخُلُق ( بل له عرض) فيما بين الحرارة والبرودة وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة ذو طرفين)، إفراط وتفريط . ( إذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك النوع فهو اعتداله ) النوعي ( وأليق أمزجته بالنسبة إلى الأنواع الخارجة عنه) فالمزاج الحاصل لبدن من أبدان الناس هو اللائق به من حيث إنه إنسان دون مزاج الفرس والحمار وغيرهما . وذلك لأنه المناسب لآثاره المطلوبة منه حتى إذا خرج إلى شيء من هذه الأمزجة مات. (وله) أي ولكل نوع ( أيضاً مزاج واقع فيما بين ذلك العرض) أي يكون في حاق الوسط فيما بين طرفي المزاج العرضي النوعي ( هو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة في ذلك العرض (به وبه يكون حاله فيما خلق له) من صفاته وآثاره المختصة به ( أجود ) ما يتصور منه ( وذلك اعتداله ) النوعي ( بالنسبة إلى ما يدخل فيه من صنف أو ( شخص ) فالاعتدال النوعي المقيس إلى الخارج يحتاج إلى النوع في وجوده ويكون حاصلاً لكل فرد من أفراده على تفاوت مراتبه. والمقيس إلى الداخل يحتاج إليه النوع في أجودية ،كمالاته ولا يكون حاصلاً إلا لأعدل شخص أعدل صنف من ذلك النوع ولا يكون أيضاً حاصلاً له إلا في أعدل حالاته

( وعليه) أي على ما ذكرنا من حال الاعتدال النوعي (قس الثلاثة الباقية ) فالاعتدال الصنفي بالقياس إلى الخارج هو الذي يكون لائقاً بصنف من نوع مقيساً إلى أمزجة سائر أصنافه وله عرض ذو طرفين هو أقل من العرض النوعي إذ هو بعض منه وإذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك الصنف وبالقياس إلى الداخل هو المزاج الواقع في حاق وسط هذا العرض، وهو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة فيما بين طرفيه بالصنف إذ به يكون حاله أجود فيما خلق لأجله ولا يكون حاصلاً لأعدل شخص منه في أعدل حالاته سواء كان هذا الصنف أعدل الأصناف أو لا. والاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الخارج هو الذي يحتاج إليه الشخص في بقائه موجوداً سليماً وهو اللائق به مقيساً إلى أمزجة الأشخاص صنفه وله أيضاً عرض هو بعض من العرض الصنفي. وبالنسبة إلى الداخل هو الذي يكون به الشخص على أفضل حالاته والاعتدال العضوي مقيساً إلى الخارج

وقوله : إذا خرج عنه أي إذا خرج ذلك المزاج لا عن ذلك العرض. وقوله : لم يكن ذلك النوع فذلك النوع بالنصب على أنه خبر لم يكن ذلك النوع . وقوله : وهو أي ذلك المزاج بقرينة . قوله : وأليق أمزجته وهو أعني قوله : وأليق أمزجته عطف على قوله : اعتداله النوعي. وقوله : ما يدخل فيه أي في ذلك النوع .

ما يتعلق به وجود العضو سالماً، وهو اللائق به دون أمزجة سائر الأعضاء. وله أيضاً عرض إلا أنه ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي ومقيسا إلى الداخل. وهو الذي ينبغي للعضو حتى يكون على أحس أحواله وأكمل زمانه )
The first observatory - the fourth section: the second purpose: on the divisions of temperament. Temperamental [temperament] (a temperament in which the specific form cannot exist except with it, and that temperament is not on a single limit that cannot be exceeded; otherwise all individuals of the same species, like man for example, would be uniform in temperament and what follows from it of character and disposition. Rather it has a range) between heat and cold and between moisture and dryness having two extremes), excess and deficiency. (If it departs from it, that species would not exist; therefore it is its) generic (moderation, and the most suitable of its temperaments with respect to the species external to it). So the temperament occurring in the body of any human is the one suitable to him insofar as he is a human, not the temperament of a horse, donkey, or others. That is because it is appropriate for the effects required from him; if he departs to any of these temperaments, he dies. (And it has) i.e., each species (also a temperament lying within that range) i.e., it is at the very midpoint between the two extremes of the generic accidental temperament (which is the most suitable of the temperaments lying within that range (to it, and with it its state concerning what it was created for) of its attributes and effects specific to it (is the best) that can be conceived of it (and that is its) generic (moderation with respect to what enters into it of sub-species or) individual. So the generic moderation measured externally is necessary for the species in its existence and occurs for every individual of its individuals with variation in its degrees. And measured internally, the species needs it for the bestness of its perfections, and it does not occur except for the most moderate individual of the most moderate sub-species of that species, and also does not occur for him except in his most moderate states. (And based on it) i.e., on what we have mentioned of the state of generic moderation (measure the remaining three). So sub-specific moderation in relation to the external is that which is suitable for a sub-species of a species measured against the temperaments of its other sub-species, and it has a range with two extremes that is less than the generic range, since it is a part of it; and if it departs from it, that sub-species would not exist. And in relation to the internal, it is the temperament lying at the very midpoint of this range, and it is the most suitable of the temperaments lying between its two extremes for the sub-species, since with it its state is best concerning what it was created for, and it does not occur for the most moderate individual from it in his most moderate states, whether this sub-species is the most moderate of sub-species or not. And personal moderation in relation to the external is that which the individual needs for his continued existence sound, and it is the one suitable to him measured against the temperaments of the individuals of his sub-species; and it also has a range that is a part of the sub-specific range. And in relation to the internal, it is that by which the individual is in his best states. And organic moderation measured to the external is that by which the existence of the organ is connected to being sound, and it is the one suitable to it apart from the temperaments of the other organs. And it also has a range, but it is not a part of the personal range; and measured to the internal, it is that which is fitting for the organ so that it may be in its best states and its most complete time.)
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a classification of moderate temperament (al-iʿtidāl) into four types: generic (al-nawʿī), sub-specific (al-ṣinfī), personal (al-shakhṣī), and organic (al-ʿuḍwī). The author first defines generic temperament as that without which the specific form cannot exist, emphasizing that it is not a single fixed point but a range (ʿaraḍ) between the extremes of heat-cold and moisture-dryness. He distinguishes two aspects for each type: the external (bi-l-qiyās ilā al-khārij) and the internal (bi-l-qiyās ilā al-dākhil). The external aspect of generic moderation is the temperament shared by all individuals of a species in relation to other species; if it departs, the species ceases to exist. The internal aspect is the most balanced temperament within that range, which only the most balanced individual of the most balanced sub-species possesses in his most balanced state. The author then instructs to apply the same logic to the remaining three types. For sub-specific moderation, the external aspect distinguishes one sub-species from others; the internal aspect is the temperament at the exact midpoint of its range, achievable only by the most balanced individual. For personal moderation, the external aspect ensures the individual's survival, while the internal aspect brings him to his best state. For organic moderation, the external aspect keeps the organ healthy relative to other organs, and the internal aspect is the optimal temperament for that organ in its best state. The glosses clarify grammatical points and ensure the reader understands the referents of pronouns and the structure of the argument.

Key Terms
مزاج
اعتدال
نوعي
عرض
حاق الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all individuals of a species have the exact same temperament.
Correction: The passage explains that there is a range (عرض) and each individual has its own temperament within that range; the internal aspect is only for the most balanced.

Checkpoint
What is the external aspect of generic moderation?
A) The midpoint of the range
B) The range itself necessary for the species' existence
C) The temperament of the most balanced individual
D) The specific form

Key Takeaway
Generic moderation has an external aspect (the range essential for species existence) and an internal aspect (the midpoint for perfection), and this pattern applies to all four types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that within the human range of temperament, there is a midpoint of perfection that only the most balanced individual achieves. In your own character, where are you settling for 'just within the acceptable range' rather than pressing toward that midpoint? What is one concrete change you will make this week to move closer to that perfect balance?
?
Consider your daily habits: your speech, your reactions, your desires. Are they merely within the bounds of what keeps you human, or are they aligned with the most excellent state you were created for? Which single habit, if brought to its midpoint, would most transform your relationship with Allah?
?
The text says the internal moderation occurs only in the most moderate state of the most moderate individual. Are you content with being 'average' in your character, or do you feel the weight of responsibility to pursue the highest excellence? What will you sacrifice today to step toward that peak?

Sub-specific Moderation (al-Iʿtidāl al-Ṣinfī)
Al-Jurjani explains sub-specific moderation and its two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand sub-specific moderation, its external aspect (distinguishing one sub-species from others) and internal aspect (the most balanced temperament within that sub-species).

Core Idea
Sub-specific moderation is a narrower range within the generic range, with its own midpoint for perfection.

After establishing generic moderation, Al-Jurjani moves to sub-specific moderation, which applies to a sub-group within a species. He explains that it has its own range, smaller than the generic range, and its own internal aspect that only the most balanced individual of that sub-species can achieve. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sub-specific moderation
Read the Arabic and translation for sub-specific moderation. Notice the comparison to generic moderation: the range is smaller and it is a part of the generic range.
The term 'صنف' means sub-species, like a race or variety.
2
Differentiate external and internal aspects
Identify the external aspect (what makes the sub-species distinct from others) and the internal aspect (the midpoint that gives the best state).
The internal aspect only occurs for the most moderate individual, regardless of whether the sub-species is the most moderate overall.
3
Compare with generic moderation
Reflect on how sub-specific moderation parallels generic moderation but at a lower level. The external aspect ensures existence of the sub-species, the internal aspect its perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الثاني : في أقسام المزاج

المزاجية ( مزاج لايمكن أن توجد فيه الصورة النوعية إلا معه، وليس ذلك المزاج على حد واحد لا يتعداه وإلا كان جميع أفراد النوع الواحد كالإنسان مثلا متوافقة في المزاج وما يتبعه من الخلق والخُلُق ( بل له عرض) فيما بين الحرارة والبرودة وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة ذو طرفين)، إفراط وتفريط . ( إذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك النوع فهو اعتداله ) النوعي ( وأليق أمزجته بالنسبة إلى الأنواع الخارجة عنه) فالمزاج الحاصل لبدن من أبدان الناس هو اللائق به من حيث إنه إنسان دون مزاج الفرس والحمار وغيرهما . وذلك لأنه المناسب لآثاره المطلوبة منه حتى إذا خرج إلى شيء من هذه الأمزجة مات. (وله) أي ولكل نوع ( أيضاً مزاج واقع فيما بين ذلك العرض) أي يكون في حاق الوسط فيما بين طرفي المزاج العرضي النوعي ( هو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة في ذلك العرض (به وبه يكون حاله فيما خلق له) من صفاته وآثاره المختصة به ( أجود ) ما يتصور منه ( وذلك اعتداله ) النوعي ( بالنسبة إلى ما يدخل فيه من صنف أو ( شخص ) فالاعتدال النوعي المقيس إلى الخارج يحتاج إلى النوع في وجوده ويكون حاصلاً لكل فرد من أفراده على تفاوت مراتبه. والمقيس إلى الداخل يحتاج إليه النوع في أجودية ،كمالاته ولا يكون حاصلاً إلا لأعدل شخص أعدل صنف من ذلك النوع ولا يكون أيضاً حاصلاً له إلا في أعدل حالاته

( وعليه) أي على ما ذكرنا من حال الاعتدال النوعي (قس الثلاثة الباقية ) فالاعتدال الصنفي بالقياس إلى الخارج هو الذي يكون لائقاً بصنف من نوع مقيساً إلى أمزجة سائر أصنافه وله عرض ذو طرفين هو أقل من العرض النوعي إذ هو بعض منه وإذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك الصنف وبالقياس إلى الداخل هو المزاج الواقع في حاق وسط هذا العرض، وهو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة فيما بين طرفيه بالصنف إذ به يكون حاله أجود فيما خلق لأجله ولا يكون حاصلاً لأعدل شخص منه في أعدل حالاته سواء كان هذا الصنف أعدل الأصناف أو لا. والاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الخارج هو الذي يحتاج إليه الشخص في بقائه موجوداً سليماً وهو اللائق به مقيساً إلى أمزجة الأشخاص صنفه وله أيضاً عرض هو بعض من العرض الصنفي. وبالنسبة إلى الداخل هو الذي يكون به الشخص على أفضل حالاته والاعتدال العضوي مقيساً إلى الخارج

وقوله : إذا خرج عنه أي إذا خرج ذلك المزاج لا عن ذلك العرض. وقوله : لم يكن ذلك النوع فذلك النوع بالنصب على أنه خبر لم يكن ذلك النوع . وقوله : وهو أي ذلك المزاج بقرينة . قوله : وأليق أمزجته وهو أعني قوله : وأليق أمزجته عطف على قوله : اعتداله النوعي. وقوله : ما يدخل فيه أي في ذلك النوع .

ما يتعلق به وجود العضو سالماً، وهو اللائق به دون أمزجة سائر الأعضاء. وله أيضاً عرض إلا أنه ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي ومقيسا إلى الداخل. وهو الذي ينبغي للعضو حتى يكون على أحس أحواله وأكمل زمانه )
The first observatory - the fourth section: the second purpose: on the divisions of temperament. Temperamental [temperament] (a temperament in which the specific form cannot exist except with it, and that temperament is not on a single limit that cannot be exceeded; otherwise all individuals of the same species, like man for example, would be uniform in temperament and what follows from it of character and disposition. Rather it has a range) between heat and cold and between moisture and dryness having two extremes), excess and deficiency. (If it departs from it, that species would not exist; therefore it is its) generic (moderation, and the most suitable of its temperaments with respect to the species external to it). So the temperament occurring in the body of any human is the one suitable to him insofar as he is a human, not the temperament of a horse, donkey, or others. That is because it is appropriate for the effects required from him; if he departs to any of these temperaments, he dies. (And it has) i.e., each species (also a temperament lying within that range) i.e., it is at the very midpoint between the two extremes of the generic accidental temperament (which is the most suitable of the temperaments lying within that range (to it, and with it its state concerning what it was created for) of its attributes and effects specific to it (is the best) that can be conceived of it (and that is its) generic (moderation with respect to what enters into it of sub-species or) individual. So the generic moderation measured externally is necessary for the species in its existence and occurs for every individual of its individuals with variation in its degrees. And measured internally, the species needs it for the bestness of its perfections, and it does not occur except for the most moderate individual of the most moderate sub-species of that species, and also does not occur for him except in his most moderate states. (And based on it) i.e., on what we have mentioned of the state of generic moderation (measure the remaining three). So sub-specific moderation in relation to the external is that which is suitable for a sub-species of a species measured against the temperaments of its other sub-species, and it has a range with two extremes that is less than the generic range, since it is a part of it; and if it departs from it, that sub-species would not exist. And in relation to the internal, it is the temperament lying at the very midpoint of this range, and it is the most suitable of the temperaments lying between its two extremes for the sub-species, since with it its state is best concerning what it was created for, and it does not occur for the most moderate individual from it in his most moderate states, whether this sub-species is the most moderate of sub-species or not. And personal moderation in relation to the external is that which the individual needs for his continued existence sound, and it is the one suitable to him measured against the temperaments of the individuals of his sub-species; and it also has a range that is a part of the sub-specific range. And in relation to the internal, it is that by which the individual is in his best states. And organic moderation measured to the external is that by which the existence of the organ is connected to being sound, and it is the one suitable to it apart from the temperaments of the other organs. And it also has a range, but it is not a part of the personal range; and measured to the internal, it is that which is fitting for the organ so that it may be in its best states and its most complete time.)

Commentary
This passage is a classification of moderate temperament (al-iʿtidāl) into four types: generic (al-nawʿī), sub-specific (al-ṣinfī), personal (al-shakhṣī), and organic (al-ʿuḍwī). The glosses clarify grammatical points and ensure the reader understands the referents of pronouns and the structure of the argument.

Personal Moderation (al-Iʿtidāl al-Shakhṣī)
Al-Jurjani defines personal moderation and its two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand personal moderation, which ensures an individual's survival (external aspect) and optimal state (internal aspect).

Core Idea
Personal moderation is the individual-specific range within the sub-specific range, with its own midpoint for best health and function.

Al-Jurjani now applies the same scheme to the individual person. Personal moderation has an external aspect that keeps the individual alive and healthy, and an internal aspect that brings him to his best possible state. This lesson focuses on how this type of moderation fits within the hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on personal moderation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the range is a part of the sub-specific range.
'شخص' means an individual person.
2
Identify the external and internal aspects
The external aspect is for survival and soundness; the internal aspect is for the best state. Note that the internal aspect is defined as being in 'أفضل حالاته' (his best states).
3
Connect to the overall pattern
Observe how this fits the pattern of the previous two types: each has a range and a midpoint, with external for existence and internal for perfection.

Organic Moderation (al-Iʿtidāl al-ʿUḍwī)
Al-Jurjani defines organic moderation and its two aspects, with glosses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand organic moderation, which applies to individual organs, with an external aspect for soundness and an internal aspect for optimal function.

Core Idea
Organic moderation is the organ-specific temperament range, not part of the personal range, with its own midpoint for best performance.

The final type of moderation is organic moderation, which applies to individual organs. Al-Jurjani explains that the external aspect keeps the organ healthy relative to other organs, and the internal aspect brings it to its best state. Notably, its range is not a part of the personal range; it is independent. This lesson completes the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on organic moderation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the special feature: the range is not part of the personal range.
The glosses after the main text clarify earlier points, but focus on the organic moderation part.
2
Identify the external and internal aspects
The external aspect is for the organ to remain sound; the internal aspect is for it to be in its best state.
3
Contrast with personal moderation
Understand why the organic range is not a part of the personal range. This is because the organ has its own requirements independent of the individual's overall temperament.

The first observatory - the fourth section: the second purpose: on the divisions of temperament. Temperamental [temperament] (a temperament in which the specific form cannot exist except with it, and that temperament is not on a single limit that cannot be exceeded; otherwise all individuals of the same species, like man for example, would be uniform in temperament and what follows from it of character and disposition. Rather it has a range) between heat and cold and between moisture and dryness having two extremes), excess and deficiency. (If it departs from it, that species would not exist; therefore it is its) generic (moderation, and the most suitable of its temperaments with respect to the species external to it). So the temperament occurring in the body of any human is the one suitable to him insofar as he is a human, not the temperament of a horse, donkey, or others. That is because it is appropriate for the effects required from him; if he departs to any of these temperaments, he dies. (And it has) i.e., each species (also a temperament lying within that range) i.e., it is at the very midpoint between the two extremes of the generic accidental temperament (which is the most suitable of the temperaments lying within that range (to it, and with it its state concerning what it was created for) of its attributes and effects specific to it (is the best) that can be conceived of it (and that is its) generic (moderation with respect to what enters into it of sub-species or) individual. So the generic moderation measured externally is necessary for the species in its existence and occurs for every individual of its individuals with variation in its degrees. And measured internally, the species needs it for the bestness of its perfections, and it does not occur except for the most moderate individual of the most moderate sub-species of that species, and also does not occur for him except in his most moderate states. (And based on it) i.e., on what we have mentioned of the state of generic moderation (measure the remaining three). So sub-specific moderation in relation to the external is that which is suitable for a sub-species of a species measured against the temperaments of its other sub-species, and it has a range with two extremes that is less than the generic range, since it is a part of it; and if it departs from it, that sub-species would not exist. And in relation to the internal, it is the temperament lying at the very midpoint of this range, and it is the most suitable of the temperaments lying between its two extremes for the sub-species, since with it its state is best concerning what it was created for, and it does not occur for the most moderate individual from it in his most moderate states, whether this sub-species is the most moderate of sub-species or not. And personal moderation in relation to the external is that which the individual needs for his continued existence sound, and it is the one suitable to him measured against the temperaments of the individuals of his sub-species; and it also has a range that is a part of the sub-specific range. And in relation to the internal, it is that by which the individual is in his best states. And organic moderation measured to the external is that by which the existence of the organ is connected to being sound, and it is the one suitable to it apart from the temperaments of the other organs. And it also has a range, but it is not a part of the personal range; and measured to the internal, it is that which is fitting for the organ so that it may be in its best states and its most complete time.)
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a classification of moderate temperament (al-iʿtidāl) into four types: generic (al-nawʿī), sub-specific (al-ṣinfī), personal (al-shakhṣī), and organic (al-ʿuḍwī). The author first defines generic temperament as that without which the specific form cannot exist, emphasizing that it is not a single fixed point but a range (ʿaraḍ) between the extremes of heat-cold and moisture-dryness. He distinguishes two aspects for each type: the external (bi-l-qiyās ilā al-khārij) and the internal (bi-l-qiyās ilā al-dākhil). The external aspect of generic moderation is the temperament shared by all individuals of a species in relation to other species; if it departs, the species ceases to exist. The internal aspect is the most balanced temperament within that range, which only the most balanced individual of the most balanced sub-species possesses in his most balanced state. The author then instructs to apply the same logic to the remaining three types. For sub-specific moderation, the external aspect distinguishes one sub-species from others; the internal aspect is the temperament at the exact midpoint of its range, achievable only by the most balanced individual. For personal moderation, the external aspect ensures the individual's survival, while the internal aspect brings him to his best state. For organic moderation, the external aspect keeps the organ healthy relative to other organs, and the internal aspect is the optimal temperament for that organ in its best state. The glosses clarify grammatical points and ensure the reader understands the referents of pronouns and the structure of the argument.

Key Terms
اعتدال
عضوي
عرض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that organic moderation's range is part of the personal range, like the other types.
Correction: The text explicitly says: 'ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي' — it is not a part of the personal range.

Checkpoint
What is unique about organic moderation's range compared to the previous types?
A) It is the same as the personal range
B) It is not a part of the personal range
C) It is larger than the generic range
D) It has no external aspect

Key Takeaway
Organic moderation follows the same pattern but its range is independent of the personal range, ensuring each organ has its own optimal temperament.

Simple and Compound Non-Moderate Temperaments with Objection and Response
Al-Jurjani divides non-moderate temperaments and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of non-moderate temperaments into simple and compound, and the author's response to an objection about compound temperaments involving opposing qualities.

Core Idea
Non-moderate temperaments are either simple or compound; compound opposites are impossible.

In this passage, al-Jurjani builds on the concept of moderate temperament by examining its opposite. He classifies non-moderate temperaments into two categories and then addresses a clever objection about compound temperaments involving both heat and cold. His response reveals the crucial role of ratio rather than absolute quantities.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Identify the two main types of non-moderate temperaments: simple (departure in one quality) and compound (departure in two non-opposing qualities). Note the four subtypes of each.
Simple: hot, cold, moist, dry. Compound: hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry.
2
Understand the objection
The imaginary objector suggests that if a compound should have ten hot and five cold parts for moderation, finding twelve hot and six cold would make it both hotter and colder than moderate. Grasp why this seems plausible.
The objection uses absolute numbers to challenge the impossibility of opposites.
3
Follow the response
Al-Jurjani shifts the criterion from the number of parts to the intermediate quality and its inclination to one side only. The ratio between hot and cold parts determines moderation, not the absolute numbers.
If the ratio (e.g., hot double cold) is maintained, the temperament is moderate regardless of scale.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما غير المعتدل فلأنه إما أن يكون خارجاً عما ينبغي ( في كيفية) واحدة (ويسمى البسيط، وهو أربعة: حار وبارد ورطب ويابس (أو يكون خارجاً عنه ( في كيفيتين غير متضادتين ويسمى المركب (وهو) أيضاً :( أربعة حار رطب وحار يابس وبارد رطب وبارد يابس . وأما الحار البارد مثلاً أو الرطب اليابس ) أي خروج المركب عما هو حقه في كيفيتين متضادتين ( أو اجتماع ثلاث أو أربع من تلك الكيفيات (فلا يتصور) إذ يلزم اجتماع المتضادين ) لا يقال : إذا كان يجب أن للمركب عشرة أجزاء حارة وخمسة باردة فوجد اثنا عشر حارة وستة باردة فهو أحر مما ينبغي وأبرد منه ). وقس على ذلك الأجزاء الرطبة واليابسة والازدواجات العقلية ) لأنا نقول : الاعتبار فيما ليس معتدلاً طبياً إنما هو ( بالكيفية المتوسطة وميلها إلى أحد الطرفين) المتضادين وذلك أي ميلها ( لا يكون إلا إلى طرف واحد ( منهما ( ضرورة) أي إذا مالت الكيفية المتوسطة عما ينبغي فإما أن تميل عنه إلى جانب الحرارة فقط أو إلى جانب البرودة فقط إذ ميلانها إليهما معا محال بديهة وكذا الحال في الرطوبة واليبوسة ( وأما الأجزاء فلا عبرة ) فيما نحن فيه ( بعددها ومقدارها بل مداره على النسبة بينهما . ( وإذا كانت ( الأجزاء ( الحارة ضعف الباردة أي عدد كان فالمزاج واحد ) فإذا فرض أن الاعتدال الطبي مبني على هذه النسبة فالأجزاء الحارة إذا كانت عشرة والباردة خمسة كان المركب معتدلاً ، وكذا إذا كانت الحارة عشرين والباردة عشرة إلى غير الأعداد التي توجد فيها هذه النسبة. وما قيل من أن المعتدل هو الذي وفر

قوله : ( وأما غير المعتدل فلأنه إلخ) أي وأما انقسام غير المعتدل الطبي إلى ثمانية أقسام فلانه إلخ . وقوله : لا يقال إما معارضة لقوله : فلا يتصور وإما منع له مع السند . وقوله : إذا كان يجب إلخ . أي إذا كان الشأن أنه يجب للمركب في اعتداله الطبي عشرة أجزاء حارة وخمسة باردة إلخ . وقوله : فوجد أي فإذا خرج عن الاعتدال الطبي حتى وجد اثني عشر جزءاً حارة وستة باردة إلخ فحينئذ يكون الاثنان من اثني عشر خارجاً عما ينبغي من الحرارة، وكذا الواحد من الستة يكون خارجاً عما ينبغي من البرودة. وقوله : فهو أي ذلك المركب. قوله : والازدواجات العقلية إما باجتماع ثلاثة منها فيكون المركب أحر وأبرد وأرطب مما مثلاً. ينبغي وإما باجتماع أربعة منها فيكون أحر وأبرد وأرطب وأيبس مما ينبغي. وقوله بل مداره أي مدار ما نحن فيه . وقوله : مبني على هذه النسبة أي النسبة الضعيفة. فما ذكر آنفاً من قوله : فهو أحر ما ينبغي وأبرد منه باطل قطعاً بل ذلك واقع على ما ينبغي كما لا يخفى
As for the non-moderate, it is because it is either outside what is appropriate (in one quality) (and it is called simple, and it is four: hot, cold, moist, and dry) or it is outside it (in two non-opposing qualities and it is called compound, and it is also four: hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry. As for hot-cold for example or moist-dry) i.e., the compound's departure from what is right in two opposing qualities (or the combination of three or four of those qualities (it is inconceivable) because it would necessitate the combination of opposites). It cannot be said: if it is necessary that the compound has ten hot parts and five cold parts, and one finds twelve hot and six cold, then it is hotter than it should be and colder than it. And measure by that the moist and dry parts and the intellectual double-combinations). For we say: the consideration in what is medicinally non-moderate is only (by the intermediate quality and its inclination to one of the two opposing extremes; and that, i.e., its inclination (cannot be except to one side (of them (necessarily)) i.e., if the intermediate quality inclines from what is appropriate, it either inclines to the side of heat only or to the side of cold only, since its inclination to both together is impossible by intuition. The same is the case with moisture and dryness. (As for the parts, there is no consideration) in what we are discussing (of their number and amount; rather the criterion is the ratio between them. (And if the hot parts are double the cold, whatever the number, the temperament is one.) So if it is assumed that medical moderation is based on this ratio, then if the hot parts are ten and the cold five, the compound is moderate; likewise if the hot are twenty and the cold ten, and so on for numbers in which this ratio exists. And what has been said that the moderate is that which...

His statement: (As for the non-moderate, because it is, etc.) i.e., as for the division of the medicinally non-moderate into eight divisions, because it is, etc. And his statement: 'It cannot be said' is either an objection to his statement 'it is inconceivable' or a denial of it with a proof. And his statement: 'if it is necessary, etc.' i.e., if the matter is that it is necessary for the compound in its medical moderation to have ten hot parts and five cold parts, etc. And his statement: 'and one finds' i.e., if it departs from medical moderation so that twelve hot parts and six cold parts are found, etc., then the two out of twelve are outside what is appropriate in heat, and likewise the one out of six is outside what is appropriate in cold. And his statement: 'then it is' i.e., that compound. His statement: 'and the intellectual double-combinations' either by combining three of them, so the compound is hotter, colder, and moister than it should be, for example; or by combining four of them, so it is hotter, colder, moister, and drier than it should be. And his statement: 'rather the criterion' i.e., the criterion of what we are discussing. And his statement: 'based on this ratio' i.e., the ratio of half. So what was mentioned earlier from his statement: 'then it is hotter than it should be and colder than it' is absolutely false; rather that is occurring as it should be, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage discusses non-moderate temperaments and addresses an objection. The author first classifies non-moderate (ghayr al-muʿtadil) temperaments into two kinds: simple (basīṭ) and compound (murakkab). Simple non-moderate temperaments involve departure in one quality only, and they are four: hot, cold, moist, dry. Compound non-moderate temperaments involve departure in two non-opposing qualities, also four: hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry. The author then states that compound temperaments involving opposing qualities (like hot-cold or moist-dry) or three or four qualities are inconceivable because they would require the combination of opposites. This is the main argument. Then an objection is raised: if a compound should have, for example, ten hot and five cold parts for moderation, but one finds twelve hot and six cold, it seems to be both hotter and colder than it should be. The author responds by shifting the consideration from the absolute number of parts to the intermediate quality (al-kayfiyya al-mutawassiṭa) and its inclination to one side. He argues that the intermediate quality, when it departs from what is appropriate, can only incline to one side (e.g., heat or cold), not both simultaneously, because that would be impossible. Therefore, the apparent combination of excess in both heat and cold is not a real combination; it is a matter of the ratio between the parts. As long as the ratio (e.g., hot parts double the cold) is maintained, the temperament remains moderate regardless of absolute numbers. Thus, the objection is invalid. The gloss further clarifies that the division of non-moderate is into eight types (four simple and four compound), and that the objection is either a direct contradiction or a denial with evidence, and explains the objection's premise and conclusion, ultimately affirming that the earlier claim of being both hotter and colder is false because the ratio-based determination shows it is actually as it should be.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a compound temperament with an excess in both heat and cold is possible and that this would contradict the impossibility of opposites.
Correction: The author clarifies that the intermediate quality can only incline to one side, and the appearance of both excesses is actually a matter of the ratio; when the ratio remains the same, the temperament is still moderate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of compound non-moderate temperament are there?
A) Two
B) Four
C) Six
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Non-moderate temperaments are classified as simple or compound, and the criterion for moderation is the ratio between parts, not absolute numbers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own character, do you ever try to hold two opposing attitudes at the same time—like being both harsh and gentle without a clear principle? How does this lesson's truth that the intermediate quality can only incline to one side force you to choose a single, consistent orientation in your dealings? What will you do today to align your heart to one clear quality rather than wavering?
?
When you evaluate your spiritual state, do you count your good deeds (the 'hot parts') and bad deeds (the 'cold parts') as absolute numbers, or do you consider the ratio—the overall direction of your heart? If you had ten good deeds and five bad ones, would you feel moderate? What if the ratio is the same but the numbers are larger? Does your focus on absolute quantities reveal an attachment to appearances rather than the inner proportion that Allah sees? What change in your self-assessment does this demand?
?
Think of a relationship or situation where you felt pulled between two 'compounds'—wanting to be both strict and lenient, or both involved and detached. This lesson says a compound of opposites is impossible. Are you trying to hold an impossible compound? What would it look like to choose a simple or non-opposing compound, accepting the tension of a single inclination? Will you today identify one area of contradiction in your life and resolve to incline to one side sincerely for Allah's sake?

Classification of Temperaments: Moderate, Non-Moderate, Simple and Compound
Defines moderate and non-moderate temperaments, divides non-moderate into eight types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of temperaments into moderate and non-moderate, and the eight types of non-moderate temperaments according to the simple and compound divisions.

Core Idea
Non-moderate temperaments are exactly eight: four simple and four compound.

In this passage, the author defines the moderate temperament as that which ensures the healthy function of an organ. He then turns to non-moderate temperaments, explaining that they can deviate in one quality (simple) or two non-opposite qualities (compound). This yields exactly eight distinct types. Understanding this classification is fundamental to the study of humoral physiology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words set the context.
Pay attention to the terms 'moderate' (muʿtadil) and 'non-moderate' (ghayr muʿtadil).
2
Understand the definition of moderate temperament
The moderate temperament is defined as what ensures an organ's healthy existence and is uniquely suited to it. It has a quality but not as part of the personal quality.
This sets the baseline for deviations.
3
Identify the two categories of non-moderate
Non-moderate is either simple (deviation in one quality) or compound (deviation in two non-opposite qualities). Simple yields four types (hot, cold, moist, dry); compound yields another four (hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry).
Total eight types.
4
Note the impossibility of opposite combinations
The passage argues that combinations like hot-cold or moist-dry are impossible because they would require opposites to coexist. Similarly, three or four qualities together are impossible.
This limits non-moderate to exactly eight types.

Source Text

المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الثاني : في أقسام المزاج. ما يتعلق به وجود العضو سالماً، وهو اللائق به دون أمزجة سائر الأعضاء. وله أيضاً عرض إلا أنه ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي ومقيسا إلى الداخل. وهو الذي ينبغي للعضو حتى يكون على أحس أحواله وأكمل زمانه. وأما غير المعتدل فلأنه إما أن يكون خارجاً عما ينبغي في كيفية واحدة ويسمى البسيط، وهو أربعة: حار وبارد ورطب ويابس. أو يكون خارجاً عنه في كيفيتين غير متضادتين ويسمى المركب، وهو أيضاً أربعة: حار رطب وحار يابس وبارد رطب وبارد يابس. وأما الحار البارد مثلاً أو الرطب اليابس أي خروج المركب عما هو حقه في كيفيتين متضادتين أو اجتماع ثلاث أو أربع من تلك الكيفيات فلا يتصور إذ يلزم اجتماع المتضادين.
First Observation - Fourth Section: Second Objective: On the Types of Temperament. What pertains to the existence of a member healthy, and it is the one suitable for it to the exclusion of the temperaments of the other members. And it also has a quality but it is not a part of the personal quality and is measured to the interior. And it is what is proper for the member so that it may be in its best states and its most complete time. As for the non-moderate, then it is either outside what is proper in one quality and it is called the simple, and it is four: hot, cold, moist, dry. Or it is outside it in two non-opposite qualities and it is called the compound, and it is also four: hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry. As for hot-cold for example or moist-dry, i.e. the departure of the compound from what is its right in two opposite qualities, or the combination of three or four of those qualities, then it is inconceivable, because the conjunction of opposites would be necessary.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies temperaments. It begins by defining the moderate temperament (al-muʿtadil) as that which ensures the healthy existence of an organ and is uniquely suited to it; it also has a quality but not one that is part of the personal quality. Then it describes the non-moderate (ghayr al-muʿtadil) and divides it into two categories: simple (bāsiṭ), which is deviation in a single quality, yielding four types (hot, cold, moist, dry); and compound (murakkab), which is deviation in two non-opposite qualities, yielding another four types (hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry). It then argues that deviation in two opposite qualities (e.g., hot-cold) or in three or four qualities is impossible because it would require the coexistence of opposites. Thus the non-moderate temperaments are limited to exactly eight types.

Key Terms
مزاج (mizāj)
معتدل (muʿtadil)
غير معتدل (ghayr muʿtadil)
بسيط (bāsiṭ)
مركب (murakkab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that non-moderate temperaments can include more than eight types by combining opposite qualities (e.g., hot-cold).
Correction: The passage argues that such combinations are impossible because they would require opposites to coexist. Thus, only eight types exist.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of non-moderate temperament are there?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Non-moderate temperaments are exactly eight: four simple (hot, cold, moist, dry) and four compound (hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry); combinations of opposites are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Opposite qualities cannot coexist—but how often do you try to hold contradictory values or desires in your heart? What is one internal conflict you need to resolve by choosing one path over the other to achieve spiritual temperance?

Refuting Dual Deviation in Temperament
Refutes the objection that a temperament can deviate in two opposite qualities simultaneously

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a compound's temperament cannot deviate in two opposite qualities simultaneously, and how the ratio of parts preserves moderation.

Core Idea
Temperament is defined by the intermediate quality's inclination, not by part counts.

This passage addresses a potential objection against the theory of temperaments. The objection claims that a compound could be both hotter and colder than it should be if the number of hot and cold parts changes proportionally. The author explains why this is impossible and clarifies the true criterion for medical moderation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the scenario: a compound with 10 hot and 5 cold parts (moderate) is found with 12 hot and 6 cold parts. The objection says it becomes both hotter and colder than it should be.
Notice the objection assumes that absolute numbers determine moderation.
2
Identify the first response
The author states that the consideration is based on the intermediate quality (al-kayfiyya al-mutawassiṭa) and its inclination. It can only incline to one extreme, not both simultaneously.
This is a self-evident principle.
3
Understand the second response
The author argues that the ratio of hot to cold parts, not their absolute numbers, determines temperament. As long as the ratio is constant (e.g., double), the temperament remains the same.
Thus 12:6 is the same ratio as 10:5.
4
Reflect on the refutation
The objection is false because it ignores both the principle of single inclination and the role of ratio. The compound in the example is actually moderate, not hotter and colder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : إذا كان يجب أن للمركب عشرة أجزاء حارة وخمسة باردة فوجد اثنا عشر حارة وستة باردة فهو أحر مما ينبغي وأبرد منه. وقس على ذلك الأجزاء الرطبة واليابسة والازدواجات العقلية. لأنا نقول : الاعتبار فيما ليس معتدلاً طبياً إنما هو بالكيفية المتوسطة وميلها إلى أحد الطرفين المتضادين وذلك أي ميلها لا يكون إلا إلى طرف واحد منهما ضرورة. أي إذا مالت الكيفية المتوسطة عما ينبغي فإما أن تميل عنه إلى جانب الحرارة فقط أو إلى جانب البرودة فقط إذ ميلانها إليهما معا محال بديهة وكذا الحال في الرطوبة واليبوسة. وأما الأجزاء فلا عبرة فيما نحن فيه بعددها ومقدارها بل مداره على النسبة بينهما. وإذا كانت الأجزاء الحارة ضعف الباردة أي عدد كان فالمزاج واحد. فإذا فرض أن الاعتدال الطبي مبني على هذه النسبة فالأجزاء الحارة إذا كانت عشرة والباردة خمسة كان المركب معتدلاً، وكذا إذا كانت الحارة عشرين والباردة عشرة إلى غير الأعداد التي توجد فيها هذه النسبة. فما ذكر آنفاً من قوله : فهو أحر ما ينبغي وأبرد منه باطل قطعاً بل ذلك واقع على ما ينبغي كما لا يخفى.
It cannot be said: If it is necessary that for the compound there are ten hot parts and five cold parts, then it is found to have twelve hot and six cold, then it is hotter than it should be and colder than it. And analogize to that the moist and dry parts and the intellectual combinations. For we say: The consideration in what is not medically moderate is only by the intermediate quality and its inclination to one of the two opposite extremes, and that, i.e. its inclination, can only be to one extreme necessarily. That is, if the intermediate quality deviates from what it should be, then it either deviates only toward the side of heat or only toward the side of cold, because its inclination to both is impossible by self-evidence, and similarly for moisture and dryness. As for the parts, there is no consideration in what we are discussing of their number and quantity, rather the basis is on the ratio between them. And if the hot parts are twice the cold parts, whatever number, then the temperament is one. So if it is assumed that medical moderation is built on this ratio, then if the hot parts are ten and the cold are five, the compound is moderate, and likewise if the hot are twenty and the cold are ten, and so on for other numbers where this ratio exists. So what was mentioned previously of his saying: 'It is hotter than it should be and colder than it' is absolutely false; rather that occurs as it should, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that if a compound requires 10 hot and 5 cold parts but is found with 12 hot and 6 cold, it seems both hotter and colder than it should be, implying deviation in two opposite qualities. The answer is twofold. First, the criterion for being non-moderate is the deviation of the intermediate quality (al-kayfiyya al-mutawassiṭa) toward one extreme only; it cannot deviate toward both opposites simultaneously, as that is impossible by self-evidence. Second, regarding the parts, the number of hot or cold parts does not matter; what matters is the ratio between them. As long as the ratio of hot to cold is constant (e.g., double), the temperament remains the same. Therefore, if the ratio is maintained, the compound is actually moderate, not hotter and colder. The claim that it is both hotter and colder is therefore false.

Key Terms
كيفية متوسطة (kayfiyya mutawassiṭa)
نسبة (nisba)
اعتدال طبي (iʿtidāl ṭibbī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that changing the absolute number of hot or cold parts always changes the temperament.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the ratio between hot and cold parts determines temperament, not the absolute numbers.

Checkpoint
What is the primary determinant of whether a compound is medically moderate according to the answer?
A) The total number of hot parts
B) The total number of cold parts
C) The ratio of hot to cold parts
D) The deviation of the intermediate quality

Key Takeaway
A compound's temperament is determined by the intermediate quality's inclination toward one extreme, not by absolute part counts, and the same ratio of parts preserves moderation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel pulled in two opposite directions in your spiritual life—like being both too strict and too lenient—do you realize that you can only truly incline to one extreme? Which one will you choose to correct today?
?
The author shows that the same ratio can appear with different numbers. In your character, are you focusing on the 'numbers' (external actions) while ignoring the 'ratio' (intention and balance)? What one adjustment can you make to restore the right proportion?
?
You constantly judge yourself for having too much of one trait and too little of another, but perhaps you are holding the same ratio as before. Can you identify a specific area where you thought you were failing but are actually consistent? How will that change your self-talk?

The Most Balanced Human Temperament: Ibn Sina vs. al-Razi
Presents the consensus on human temperament and the differing views of Ibn Sina and al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus that humans have the most balanced temperament and the disagreement between Ibn Sina and al-Razi on which subgroup is most balanced.

Core Idea
The most balanced temperament belongs to humans, but scholars differ on which group is ideal.

This note addresses a subtle point in natural philosophy: which beings have the most balanced temperament? All agree it is the human species due to the nobility of the soul and the lack of stinginess in the outpouring from the First Principle. However, Ibn Sina and al-Razi diverge on which human group is most balanced, offering arguments based on geography and climate. The passage presents their reasoning concisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Identify the unanimous agreement that humans are the most balanced compound and understand the reasoning: the human soul's nobility and the receptivity of the temperament.
The phrase 'لا بخل في إفاضة المبدأ' emphasizes that the outpouring is not stingy but depends on preparedness.
2
Analyze Ibn Sina's argument
Study the reasons Ibn Sina gives for considering equator dwellers the most balanced: equal day and night, the sun's zenith passage, and moderate temperatures.
He adds a caveat about avoiding opposing earthly causes like mountains and seas.
3
Analyze al-Razi's argument
Al-Razi counters by pointing to the fourth clime's inhabitants, citing empirical observations: fairer color, taller stature, better intellect, and nobler character.
This shows a shift from purely theoretical reasoning to observable qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : اتفقوا على أن أعدل أنواع المركبات أي أقربها بحسب المزاج إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي نوع الإنسان لأن النفس الإنسانية أشرف وأكمل ولا بخل في إفاضة المبدأ بل هي بحسب استعدادات القوابل فاستعداد الإنسان بحسب مزاجه أشد وأقوى فيكون إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي أقرب. واختلفوا في أعدل الأصناف من نوع الإنسان فقال ابن سينا : أعدل أصنافه سكان خط الاستواء لتشابه أحوالهم في الحر والبرد وذلك لتساوي ليلهم ونهارهم أبداً فتكسر كل واحدة من هاتين الكيفيتين الحادثتين منهما بالأخرى. ولأن الشمس تلبث على سمت رؤوسهم كثيراً بل تمر به حال اجتيازها عن إحدى الجهتين إلى الأخرى وهناك حركتها في الميل عن المعدل أسرع ما يكون فلا تشتد حرارة صيفهم ولا تبعد الشمس عن سمت رؤوسهم إلا بمقدار الميل الكلي فلا يكون بردهم أيضاً شديداً، فيكون مزاجهم أقرب إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي إذا لم تعرض هناك أسباب أرضية مضادة كالجبال والبحار. وقال الإمام الرازي : هم سكان الإقليم الرابع لأنا نرى أهله أحسن ألواناً وأطل قدوداً وأجود أذهاناً وأكرم أخلاقاً.
A note: They agreed that the most balanced of the types of compounds, i.e., the closest in terms of temperament to true equilibrium, is the human species, because the human soul is nobler and more perfect and there is no stinginess in the outpouring of the First Principle, rather it is according to the preparedness of the recipients, so the preparedness of the human due to his temperament is stronger and more powerful, so it is closer to true equilibrium. And they differed regarding the most balanced of the varieties of the human species. Ibn Sina said: The most balanced of its varieties are the inhabitants of the equator because their conditions in heat and cold are similar, due to the equality of their night and day always, so each of these two qualities occurring from them breaks the other. And because the sun lingers at their zenith a lot, rather it passes over it during its crossing from one side to the other, and there its motion in inclination from the celestial equator is as fast as it can be, so the heat of their summer does not become intense, and the sun is not distant from their zenith except by the total declination, so their cold also is not intense, so their temperament is closer to true equilibrium if there do not occur there opposing earthly causes like mountains and seas. And Imam al-Razi said: They are the inhabitants of the fourth clime because we see their people as fairer in color, taller in stature, better in intellect, and nobler in character.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbīh) that presents a consensus and a disagreement. All agree that the most balanced of all compound beings, the closest to true equilibrium (al-iʿtidāl al-ḥaqīqī), is the human species. The reason is that the human soul is the most noble and perfect, and the outpouring from the First Principle (al-mabdaʾ) is without stinginess; it depends on the receptivity of the recipients. Human receptivity, due to its temperament, is strongest, thus closest to true equilibrium. Then there is disagreement on which specific variety of humans is most balanced. Ibn Sina holds that equator dwellers are most balanced because their day and night are always equal, moderating heat and cold. Moreover, the sun passes over their zenith and its motion in declination is fastest, so neither summer heat nor winter cold is extreme, making their temperament closest to balance, barring opposing earthly factors. Al-Razi argues for inhabitants of the fourth clime (including Khurasan) because they are fairer, taller, more intelligent, and more noble in character.

Key Terms
أعدل (aʿdal)
اعتدال حقيقي (iʿtidāl ḥaqīqī)
نوع الإنسان (nawʿ al-insān)
خط الاستواء (khaṭṭ al-istiwāʾ)
إقليم رابع (iqlim rābiʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the most balanced human type is determined solely by physical appearance, but the passage shows it is based on physiological and environmental reasoning.
Correction: Both Ibn Sina and al-Razi use physical and environmental arguments, not just appearance. Ibn Sina uses climatic theory, al-Razi uses empirical observations including but not limited to appearance.

Checkpoint
Who argued that the inhabitants of the equator have the most balanced temperament?
A) al-Razi
B) Ibn Sina
C) al-Katibi
D) The author of the work

Key Takeaway
Medieval scholars used climatic and empirical reasoning to debate which human group has the most balanced temperament, with Ibn Sina favoring the equator and al-Razi favoring the fourth clime.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the human soul's nobility is linked to a balanced temperament. But if your soul is so noble, why do you often neglect its purification? What one habit will you change today to honor the perfection of your soul?
?
Ibn Sina and al-Razi debated which people are most balanced based on external factors. Are you more influenced by theoretical ideals or by what you observe in practice? How can you use both to evaluate your own spiritual state?
?
The passage says that the outpouring of the First Principle depends on the preparedness of the recipient. Are you actively preparing your soul to receive divine gifts, or are you waiting passively? What will you do this week to increase your spiritual preparedness?

The Most Balanced Persons and Organs
Defining the most balanced person and organ, with a critique of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the most balanced person and organ are determined in temperament theory, and why the claim about the skin is not certain.

Core Idea
The most balanced organ is the skin, but the proof is persuasive, not certain.

In this passage, the author builds on earlier discussions of balanced types and kinds to specify which individual and which organ are considered most balanced. However, he also warns that the basis for identifying the most balanced organ lacks conclusive evidence. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from type to person to organ.
The passage uses a comparative method (by analogy with the most balanced kind).
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the definition of the most balanced person and the most balanced organ. Note how each is derived.
The most balanced person is defined in relation to the most balanced kind; the organ is specified as skin, especially the index fingertip.
3
Understand the critique
The author ends with a critique: the claim about the skin is not certain and lacks conclusive proof. Ask why this matters for the theory.
This shows the author's critical approach even to established views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما ذكر أعدل الأنواع وأعدل الأصناف أشار إلى أعدل الأشخاص وأعدل الأعضاء بقوله : ( وتعرف ) أنت على قياس أعدل الأصناف ( إن أعدل الأشخاص ) النوعية ( أعدل شخص من أعدل صنف و أما ) ( أعدل الأعضاء ) فهو ( عندهم الجلد سيما ) الجلد الذي ( للأنملة سيما) الذي للسبابة ولذلك حكم جلد أنملة السبابة أو جلد الأنامل. (طبعاً في الفرق بين الملموسات والحاكم ينبغي أن يكون متساوي الميل إلى الطرفين) ليحكم بالعدل. ) ولا يخفى) على الفطن ( أن شيئا من ذلك الذي ذكروه من حال الجلد (غير يقيني) إذ لا دلالة قاطعة عليه وحديث التحكيم إقناعي.
And when he mentioned the most balanced of types and the most balanced of kinds, he pointed to the most balanced of persons and the most balanced of organs by saying: 'And you know, by analogy with the most balanced of kinds, that the most balanced of persons (specific) is the most balanced person from the most balanced kind. As for the most balanced of organs, it is, according to them, the skin, especially the skin of the fingertip, especially that of the index finger.' Therefore they judged the skin of the fingertip of the index finger or the skin of the fingertips. 'Naturally, in distinguishing between tangible things, the judge should be equally inclined to both extremes in order to judge justly.' And it is not hidden from the intelligent that none of what they mentioned about the state of the skin is certain, for there is no conclusive evidence for it, and the story of arbitration is persuasive.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how to identify the most balanced individual and organ within the context of temperament theory. The author begins by stating that after discussing the most balanced types and kinds, the text turns to the most balanced persons and organs. The most balanced person is defined as the most balanced individual belonging to the most balanced kind. For the most balanced organ, it is specified as the skin, particularly the skin of the fingertip, and more particularly the index finger. This is because the skin is the organ of touch, and to accurately judge differences in tangible qualities, the judge (i.e., the skin) should be equally balanced between extremes. However, the author then critiques this view, stating that this is not certain and lacks conclusive proof; it is merely persuasive.

Misconception: One might think the most balanced person is simply the average; here it is defined as the most balanced of a balanced kind.
Correction: The passage defines the most balanced person as the most balanced person from the most balanced kind, not an average across all individuals.

Classification of Imbalanced Temperaments
The author classifies the eight non-moderate temperaments into material/simple and congenital/accidental

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eight imbalanced temperaments are classified into material/simple by cause and congenital/accidental by duration.

Core Idea
Imbalanced temperaments arise either from humors or external factors, and can be innate or acquired.

This passage from the text presents a systematic classification of the eight temperaments that fall outside the balanced state. Before we explore the details of each temperament, we must first grasp the author's twofold division: by cause (material vs. simple) and by duration (congenital vs. accidental). This classification is essential for understanding how imbalances are analyzed in Islamic medicine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it opens with a classification statement and provides examples for each category.
The passage is concise; read it twice to absorb the structure.
2
Identify the two binary divisions
Look for the two ways each of the eight temperaments is categorized: by cause (material vs. simple) and by duration (congenital vs. accidental). Note the examples: phlegm (coldness) and yellow bile (heat) for material; snow and sun for simple.
Material imbalances involve a dominant humor; simple imbalances involve external factors.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters. It shows that not all imbalances are caused by humors; some are due to environment or innate constitution. This affects diagnosis and treatment.
The commentary will clarify these implications further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن كلاً من الأمزجة الثمانية الخارجة عن الاعتدال قد يكون ماديا بأن يغلب على البدن خلط يغلب عليه كيفيه فيخرجه عن الاعتدال الذي هو حقه إلى تلك الكيفية كأن يغلب مثلا عليه البلغم فيخرجه إلى البرودة أو الصفراء فتخرجه إلى الحرارة، وقد يكون ساذجاً بأن يخرج عن الاعتدال لا بمجاورة خلط نافذ فيه بل بأسباب خارجية أوجبت ذلك كالمبرد بالثلج والمسخن بالشمس وقد يكون كل واحد منها جبلياً خلق البدن عليه وعرضياً عرض له بعد اعتداله في جبلته .
And know that each of the eight temperaments outside moderation may be material, by a humor dominating the body that overpowers its quality and takes it out of the moderation that is its due to that quality, e.g., phlegm dominates it and takes it to coldness, or yellow bile takes it to heat; and it may be simple, by leaving moderation not due to the presence of a penetrating humor but due to external causes that necessitate that, like cooling with snow or heating with the sun; and each of them may be congenital, the body being created upon it, or accidental, occurring after its moderation in its constitution.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the eight temperaments that fall outside the balanced state. The author explains that each of these eight can be categorized in two ways: by cause and by duration. First, by cause: a temperament may be 'material' (māddī), meaning an imbalance arises from a dominant humor (such as phlegm causing coldness or yellow bile causing heat); or it may be 'simple' (sādhdhaj), meaning the imbalance is not due to an internal humor but to external factors like snow or sun. Second, by duration: each type may be 'congenital' (jiblī), i.e., the body was created with that imbalance, or 'accidental' ('araḍī), i.e., it occurs after the body's original balanced constitution. This classification clarifies that not all imbalances are caused by humors, and some are due to environment or innate disposition.

Key Terms
الأمزجة الثمانية
الاعتدال
مادي
ساذج
جبلي
عرضي
البلغم
الصفراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All imbalances are caused by humoral excess.
Correction: Imbalances can also be caused by external factors like snow or sun, not necessarily by a dominant humor.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by a 'simple' (sādhaj) temperament outside moderation?
A) An imbalance caused by a dominant humor
B) An imbalance that is congenital
C) An imbalance due to external causes like snow or sun, not a humor
D) An imbalance that is temporary

Key Takeaway
The eight imbalanced temperaments are classified by cause (material vs. simple) and by duration (congenital vs. accidental), showing that not all imbalances are due to humors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own state: do you tend to blame your imbalances on internal moods (material) or external circumstances (simple)? Which of these is harder to change? What does that reveal about your reliance on your own control versus submission to Allah's decree?
?
This lesson shows that some imbalances are congenital—you were created with them. Is there an aspect of your temperament you have always considered a flaw? How does knowing it is part of your original constitution change how you respond to it? Will you accept it as a test or strive to moderate it?
?
The ideal is moderation (iʿtidāl). Honestly, are you comfortable with your own imbalances? Do you secretly prefer them because they feel familiar? What would it cost you to genuinely seek balance in your spiritual and physical life? Make a concrete plan to address one imbalance this week.

The Most Balanced Individual and the Organ of Touch
Argues about the most balanced person and organ, then states uncertainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the skin, particularly of the fingertip and index finger, is considered the most balanced organ for tactile judgment, and why this claim is not certain.

Core Idea
The skin of the fingertip is the most balanced organ, but its judgment is not certain.

This passage discusses the concept of the most balanced individual and organ. After critiquing an earlier inference about heat and location, the author argues that the most balanced individual comes from the most balanced kind, and the most balanced organ is the skin of the fingertip, especially the index finger, because it should be equally inclined to judge touch. However, he concludes that this is not certain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses parentheses to indicate embedded commentary.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The passage first critiques a previous inference about heat, then introduces the possibility of a more balanced temperament, then analogizes to individuals and organs. Finally, it identifies the skin of the fingertip as the most balanced organ and concludes with uncertainty.
3
Reflect on the uncertainty
The author explicitly states that the claim about the skin is not certain and the argument is persuasive, not decisive. This is a key point: even theoretical reasoning can be uncertain in Islamic theology.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and do the activity to solidify your grasp of the passage.

(واعلم أن كلاً من) الأمزجة ( الثمانية ) الخارجة عن الاعتدال ( قد يكون ماديا ) . بأن يغلب على البدن خلط يغلب عليه كيفيه فيخرجه عن الاعتدال الذي هو حقه إلى تلك الكيفية كأن يغلب مثلا عليه البلغم فيخرجه إلى البرودة أو الصفراء فتخرجه إلى الحرارة، وقد يكون ساذجاً ( بأن يخرج عن الاعتدال لا بمجاورة خلط نافذ فيه بل بأسباب خارجية أوجبت ذلك كالمبرد بالثلج والمسخن بالشمس وقد يكون) كل واحد منها ( جبلياً ) خلق البدن عليه ) وعرضياً ) عرض له بعد اعتداله في جبلته .
The first observation - the second chapter - the first division: The malleable bodies, and they are the celestial sphere, either alone or with the sun, so that causes excessive heating in the heads that are directly opposite it or near it. (And if that) which we mentioned) is possible, the inference is invalid) because it is possible that the heat in summer of those lands is due to some of these causes, not merely because of the sun's proximity to their zenith, so it does not follow that the winter of the equator is like it in heat if it is free from the mentioned causes. (Then there is no rational obstacle) that it may cause) in some places that are not on the equator nor in the fourth clime) some of these matters) i.e. in some earthly positions (either singly or combined what is (i.e. a specific temperament that is more balanced than the two) i.e. the temperaments of the inhabitants of the equator and the fourth clime. Therefore they judged the skin of the index finger or the skin of the fingertips. (Naturally, in distinguishing between tangible things, the judge should be equally inclined to both extremes) to judge justly. ) And it is not hidden) from the intelligent (that any of that which they mentioned about the state of the skin (is not certain) because there is no decisive proof for it, and the arbitration argument is persuasive. (And know that each of) the eight temperaments) outside of balance (may be material) by a humor dominating the body and overcoming it with its quality, removing it from the balance which is its right to that quality, such as phlegm dominating it and removing it to cold, or yellow bile removing it to heat. And it may be simple (by being removed from balance not by a humor permeating it but by external causes that necessitate that, like cooling with ice and heating with the sun) and each of them may be (congenital) the body was created upon it) and accidental) occurring after its congenital balance.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the most balanced human individual and the most balanced organ for tactile judgment. It begins by considering the possibility that heat in summer may be due to factors other than direct solar zenith, which undermines a previous inference about the equator's winter. It then argues that there is no rational obstacle to the existence of a place with a temperament more balanced than both the equator and the fourth clime. From this, the author moves to the concept of the most balanced individual and organ: the most balanced individual comes from the most balanced kind, and the most balanced organ is the skin, specifically of the finger tip, especially the index finger. The skin is considered the judge of touch because it should be equally inclined to both extremes to judge justly. However, the author concludes that all this is not certain; there is no decisive proof, and the argument is merely persuasive. The passage ends by noting that the eight temperaments outside balance can be either material (due to a dominant humor) or simple (due to external causes), and each can be congenital or accidental.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the claim about the skin is not certain and there is no decisive proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Your skin touches everything, but the author says its judgment is not certain. How does that affect the way you trust your senses? And what about your spiritual heart—do you treat its judgments as more certain than your physical senses?

The Eight Temperaments: Material and Simple, Congenital and Accidental
Classifies the eight temperaments into material/simple and congenital/accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eight temperaments outside balance are categorized as material or simple, and as congenital or accidental.

Core Idea
Every unbalanced temperament is either material (from humors) or simple (from externals), and either congenital or accidental.

This passage classifies the eight temperaments that fall outside the balanced state. It explains that each temperament can be material, caused by a dominant humor, or simple, caused by external factors. Additionally, each can be congenital, meaning the body was created with it, or accidental, occurring after balance.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the two axes
Read the passage and identify the two classification axes: material vs simple (based on the cause), and congenital vs accidental (based on the origin).
The passage first explains material vs simple, then notes each can be congenital or accidental.
2
Give examples from daily life
Think of examples for each category, such as cold due to phlegm (material) vs cold due to ice (simple), and a naturally cold person (congenital) vs someone who became cold later (accidental).
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does this classification matter? It helps in understanding how temperaments can be altered and what causes them.

Classification of Minerals: Malleable and Non-Malleable
Defines malleable and non-malleable minerals and lists the seven metals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic philosophers classified minerals into malleable and non-malleable, and what the seven malleable bodies are.

Core Idea
Minerals are either malleable (can be hammered) or not; the seven metals are the malleable ones.

Before diving into the formation of metals, Sanusi establishes a basic classification: minerals are either malleable (able to be hammered without breaking) or non-malleable. The first category consists of seven specific bodies (metals) which are listed by name. This lesson introduces that foundational division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the definitions.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice that minerals are first divided into malleable and non-malleable. What is the criterion?
Malleable means can be hammered without breaking, but softening and expanding.
3
Memorize the seven metals
The passage lists seven metals: gold, silver, lead, Asrub, iron, copper, and Kharsini. Remember them.
Asrub is black lead; Kharsini resembles copper or raw gold.
