Ibn Sina's Definition of the Soul and Its Universal Application
Ibn Sina's definition of soul and its application to all souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of the soul as he presented it in al-Shifa', and how it applies to both earthly and celestial souls despite the term being used homonymously.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's soul definition is inclusive: it covers all souls by focusing on actions devoid of will, not uniform pattern.

This lesson explores a key passage from al-Shifa' where Ibn Sina defines the soul. The author concludes that the term 'soul' is used homonymously for earthly and celestial souls, yet Ibn Sina provides a definition that encompasses both. We'll unpack the definition and the grammatical clarification that ensures celestial souls are included.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the homonymous usage
Understand that the term 'soul' (nafs) is applied to different types of souls only by homonymy (ishtirak lafzi). This means there is no single essence shared across all souls, but the term is used for different realities.
This is crucial for avoiding confusion about the unity of the concept of soul.
2
Read Ibn Sina's definition
Read the Arabic definition from al-Shifa': 'everything that is a principle for the issuance of actions that are not on a uniform pattern and are devoid of will'. Consider what this definition includes.
Notice the two conditions: actions not uniform AND devoid of will.
3
Analyze the pronoun reference
The commentator clarifies that the pronoun in 'devoid of will' (adimat al-irada) refers to the pattern (watira), not the actions. This is to include celestial souls whose actions are uniform but devoid of will.
If it referred to actions, celestial souls would be excluded because their actions are uniform.
4
Understand the inclusive effect
By referring to the pattern, the definition covers any soul that is a principle of actions that either are not uniform or, if uniform, lack will. Thus all souls (earthly and celestial) are included.
This shows the sophistication of Ibn Sina's definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإطلاق النفس على النفوس الأرضية والسماوية ليس إلا بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي هذا. وقد صرح ابن سينا في الشفاء بأن كل ما يكون مبدأ لصدور أفاعيل ليست على وتيرة واحدة عادمة للإرادة فإنا نسميه نفسا وهذا المعنى مشترك بين النفوس كلها لأن قوله : ( عادمة للإرادة) الظاهر أن قوله : عادمة مجرور، وأن الضمير المستتر فيه راجع إلى الوتيرة لا إلى الأفاعيل كما يوهمه ظاهر عبارته في حاشية شرح التجريد حيث قال : أو يكون مبدأ الأفاعيل تكون على وتيرة واحدة لكن لا يكون عادمة للإرادة وإنما قلنا : لا إلى الأفاعيل لأنه حينئذ يلزم خروج النفوس الفلكية عن هذا الرسم، وعلى تقدير رجوع الضمير إلى الوتيرة كما هو الظاهر. يكون إسناد عادمة إلى الضمير فيه إسناداً مجازيا ويكون المقصود ونفي المجموع. ثم إن
Therefore, the application of the term 'soul' (nafs) to earthly and celestial souls is only according to homonymy (ishtirak lafzi). And Ibn Sina has explicitly stated in al-Shifa' that everything that is a principle for the issuance of actions that are not on a uniform pattern and are devoid of will (al-irada), then we call it a soul. And this meaning is common to all souls, because – His saying: (devoid of will) – it is apparent that his saying 'devoid of will' (adimat al-irada) is in the genitive case, and that the implicit pronoun in it refers to the pattern (al-watira), not to the actions (al-afa'il), as is suggested by the apparent meaning of his expression in the gloss on Sharh al-Tajrid, where he said: 'Or it is a principle of actions that are on a uniform pattern but not devoid of will.' And we only said: not to the actions, because then it would necessitate the exclusion of celestial souls from this definition. And on the assumption that the pronoun refers to the pattern, as is apparent, the attribution of 'devoid' to the pronoun is a metaphorical attribution, and the intended meaning is the negation of the combination. Then,
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage concludes that the term 'soul' (nafs) is applied to earthly and celestial souls only by homonymy (ishtirak lafzi). It then cites Ibn Sina in al-Shifa', who defines soul as 'everything that is a principle for the issuance of actions that are not on a uniform pattern and are devoid of will (al-irada)'. This meaning is common to all souls. The commentator then explains the phrase 'devoid of will' ('adimat al-irada'), clarifying that the pronoun in 'devoid' refers to the 'pattern' (watira) not to the 'actions' (afa'il), because returning it to actions would exclude celestial souls (since their actions are uniform but they lack will? Actually, the commentator argues that if the pronoun referred to actions, then the definition would require actions not uniform and devoid of will, but celestial souls have uniform actions but are devoid of will, so they would be excluded. Instead, the intended meaning is the negation of the combination of uniformity and will: i.e., actions are either not on a uniform pattern or, if uniform, then devoid of will. Thus celestial souls, being devoid of will, are included. The commentator notes that this interpretation is a metaphorical attribution.

ابن سينا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's definition excludes celestial souls because their actions are uniform.
Correction: The definition includes celestial souls because 'devoid of will' refers to the pattern, not the actions; so actions that are uniform but devoid of will still qualify.

Checkpoint

C) The pattern (al-watira)
D) The instrument (al-ala)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definition of the soul, when correctly understood, applies to both earthly and celestial souls by focusing on the absence of will rather than the uniformity of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This lesson clarifies that celestial souls lack will. If you were stripped of your will, what would remain of your actions? Would your soul still be a principle of good actions, or would it be empty? Let this question drive you to purify your intentions.

Cessation of the Nutritive Power with Death
Commentary on the cessation of the nutritive power with death and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument for the cessation of the nutritive power with death and its basis in the corruption of temperament.

Core Idea
The nutritive power ceases with death because it requires a balanced temperament.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains why the nutritive power stops functioning at death. It argues that the cessation is necessary due to the corruption of the temperament, which undermines the bodily powers. The author provides a simile of a lamp to clarify how innate heat is extinguished.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: the passage begins with a mention of spoiling by disease, then makes the main claim, gives a reason, and ends with an explanatory simile.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ضرورة الموت' which indicates necessity.
2
Identify the two causes mentioned
The passage gives two interconnected causes for the cessation: the corruption of the temperament (فساد المزاج) and the finitude of bodily powers. Understand that these are not separate but related.
The variant reading emphasizes 'ضرورة الموت' as a proof.
3
Understand the role of innate moisture and heat
The author explains that the innate moisture decreases because of the cooperation of internal and external heat and movements. This leads to the extinguishing of innate heat.
Innate moisture is like oil; innate heat is like the flame.
4
Reflect on the lamp simile
The simile of the lamp (انطفاء المصباح) illustrates how the loss of innate moisture and the predominance of foreign moisture cause death, just as a lamp goes out when oil is gone and water prevails.
This simile makes the abstract process tangible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوامه ولونه وقد يخل به كما في البرص والبهق ( وقد يثبت وقوفها أي وقوف الغاذية عن فعلها ( ضرورة الموت)، وحينئذ لفساد المزاج ( بأن القوى الجسمانية متناهية ) في آثارها ( كما تقدم ) . وفي بعض النسخ وقد يثبت وقوفها بضرورة الموت وبأن القوى الجسمانية يعني أن ضرورة الموت تدل على وقوفها أيضاً. وإنما كان ضرورياً لأن الرطوبة الغريزية تنتقص بعد بنفس الوقوف وذلك أن الحرارة الغريزية والحرارة الخارجة والحركات النفسانية والبدنية تتعاضد في تحليلها حتى تنحل بالكلية فتغلب اليبوسة والرطوبة الغريبة وتنطفئ الحرارة الغريزية كانطفاء المصباح عند انتفاء الدهن وغلبة الماء ويحل الموت
Its consistency and color, and it may spoil it as in leprosy and vitiligo. And it may be established its stopping, i.e., the stopping of the nutritive power from its action, necessarily with death, and then due to corruption of the temperament, because the bodily powers are finite in their effects, as previously. And in some copies: 'And it may be established its stopping by the necessity of death and because the bodily powers' meaning that the necessity of death indicates its stopping also. And it was necessary because the innate moisture decreases after the stopping itself, and that is because the innate heat, the external heat, and the psychic and bodily movements cooperate in analyzing it until it is completely dissolved, then dryness and foreign moisture prevail, and the innate heat is extinguished like the extinguishing of a lamp when the oil is gone and water prevails, and death occurs.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses the cessation of the nutritive (غاذية) power with death. It begins by noting that certain bodily features like consistency and color can be spoiled by diseases such as leprosy and vitiligo, which indicates the role of the nutritive power. The main argument is that the nutritive power necessarily stops with death. The proof is that death results from the corruption of the temperament, and the bodily powers are finite in their effects. The author mentions a variant reading that emphasizes the necessity of death indicating its stoppage. Then he explains why it is necessary: the innate moisture (رطوبة غريزية) decreases after the stopping itself, because the innate heat (حرارة غريزية), external heat, and psychic and bodily movements all cooperate in analyzing the moisture until it is completely dissolved. Dryness and foreign moisture then prevail, and the innate heat is extinguished, just as a lamp goes out when its oil is exhausted and water overcomes. Thus death occurs, and with it the nutritive power ceases.

Key Terms
الغاذية
القوى الجسمانية
الرطوبة الغريزية
الحرارة الغريزية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the cessation of the nutritive power is merely a consequence of death rather than a necessary simultaneous occurrence.
Correction: The passage argues that the nutritive power stops necessarily with death due to the corruption of the temperament and the finitude of bodily powers. It is not a separate consequence but an integral part of the death process.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason for the cessation of the nutritive power with death?
A) The finitude of bodily powers
B) The corruption of the temperament
C) The exhaustion of innate moisture
D) The extinguishing of innate heat

Key Takeaway
The nutritive power ceases with death necessarily because the corruption of the temperament ends the balanced conditions required for its function.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson says death is a necessary corruption of temperament. What 'corruptions' in your spiritual temperament—such as arrogance, negligence, or love of dunya—are slowly extinguishing your spiritual life? What is one step you will take this week to correct that imbalance?

The Cessation of the Growth Power: Its Proof and Implications
Definition, distinctions, proof of cessation, and aftermath of the growth power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why and how the growth power ceases, based on the natural drying of the body, and the distinction between normal growth, tumors, and obesity.

Core Idea
Growth stops when the body dries completely, preventing the expansion needed for nutrient absorption.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif explains the mechanism behind the cessation of the growth power. The author defines the growth power, distinguishes it from abnormal conditions like tumors and obesity, and provides a physiological proof based on the body's natural drying. He also raises a subtle question about whether the power becomes completely null or persists in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the growth power
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to understand how the growth power is defined: it interlaces food among parts and increases dimensions naturally.
Note that the definition ties growth to a natural proportion specific to each person.
2
Identify the distinctions from tumor and obesity
The passage contrasts natural growth with tumors (unnatural proportion) and obesity (post-growth, limited to two dimensions and flesh). Understand why these are not considered growth.
This clarifies what is not meant by the growth power.
3
Trace the proof of cessation via drying
Follow the argument: the body starts moist, allowing easy nutrient penetration. As it dries gradually, penetration becomes difficult. Since penetration requires organ expansion, when organs are completely dry, expansion is impossible, so growth stops necessarily.
The proof is a causal chain from moisture to expansion to growth.
4
Consider the hesitation and relation to nutritive power
The passage ends with a hesitation: does the growth power become null or remain in essence? Also, note how the nutritive power (غاذية) ceases to provide material for growth when food only replaces what is lost.
This shows the complexity of the topic and the limits of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والنامية) التي لا بد منها في وصول الشخص إلى كماله ) تداخل الغذاء بين الأجزاء فتضمه إليها فتزيد في الأقطار الثلاثة بنسبة طبيعية ) أي تزيد في تلك الأقطار بنسبة تقتضيها طبيعة ذلك الشخص الذي له تلك القوة ( إلى غاية ما هي غاية النشو في ذلك الشخص ( ثم تقف ) عن فعلها ) لا كالورم فإنه ليس على النسبة الطبيعية بل خارج عن المجرى الطبيعي. ( والسمن) فإنه قد يكون بعد حال النشو أيضاً كالورم وقد مر ما قيل من أن السمن لا يكون إلا في قطرين ومن أنه مخصوص باللحم وما في حكمه دون الأعضاء الأصلية كالعظم ونظائره ( وذلك ) أي بيان وقوف النامية أنه لما كان البدن متولداً من الدم والمني ( فهو في الأول رطب ) في الغاية فيتأتى حينئذ نفوذ الغذاء بين أجزائه بسهولة (ثم يجف يسيراً يسيراً) ويتعسر النفوذ قليلاً ( ونفوذ الغذاء لا يكون إلا بتمدد الأعضاء فإذا جفت ) الأعضاء جفافاً كاملاً ( لم تقبل ذلك ) التمدد فلم يتصور نفوذ الغذاء فيها ( فوقفت ( النامية عن فعلها ( ضرورة ) وهل تبطل حينئذ بالكلية أو تبقى ذاتها فيه تردد والغاذية عدم النامية بتحصيل ما يتعلق به فعلها وهو ما زاد من على بدل مايتحلل فإذا ساواه الغذاء أو نقص عنه فات محل فعل النامية .
And the growth power, which is necessary for the person to reach his perfection. It interlocks the food among the parts, then attaches it to them, and increases in the three dimensions in a natural proportion, i.e., it increases in those dimensions in a proportion required by the nature of that person who has that power, up to the limit which is the limit of growth in that person, then it stops from its action – not like a tumor, for it is not upon the natural proportion but outside the natural course. And obesity, for it may occur after the state of growth also like a tumor, and what was said that obesity only occurs in two dimensions, and that it is specific to flesh and what is in its ruling, not the original organs like bone and its parallels. And that, i.e., the explanation of the cessation of the growth power: since the body is generated from blood and semen, it is in the beginning extremely moist, so then the penetration of food among its parts occurs easily. Then it dries little by little, and the penetration becomes slightly difficult. The penetration of food only occurs by the expansion of the organs. So when the organs dry completely, they do not accept that expansion, so the penetration of food in them is not conceivable, and the growth power stops necessarily. And whether it then becomes null completely or its essence remains is a matter of hesitation. And the nutritive power is the absence of the growth power by acquiring what its action attaches to, which is what is added over the replacement of what dissolves. So if the food equals it or falls short, the locus of the growth power's action is lost.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage explains the cessation of the growth (نامية) power. The author defines the growth power as that which allows a person to reach his full development. It interlaces food among the body's parts and increases them in three dimensions naturally, until the person reaches his full growth, then it stops. The author distinguishes this from a tumor, which is unnatural, and from obesity, which can occur after growth and is limited to two dimensions and to flesh. The proof of its cessation is that the body is initially very moist (from blood and semen), allowing easy nutrient penetration. As the body dries, penetration becomes harder. Since nutrient penetration requires organ expansion, when the organs become completely dry, they cannot expand, and growth stops. The author then raises a question: does the growth power become completely null at this point, or does its essence remain? This is left as a matter of hesitation (تردد). He further explains the relationship between the nutritive (غاذية) and growth powers: the nutritive power ceases to provide the material for growth, so when the food only replaces what is dissolved or less, the growth power has no locus to act.

الغاذية
الأقطار الثلاثة

التمدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the growth power stops because of old age rather than because of the complete drying of the organs preventing expansion.
Correction: The passage explains that cessation is due to the physical drying and loss of ability to expand, not merely the passage of time.

Checkpoint
What causes the growth power to cease according to the passage?
A) The body reaches a certain age
B) The organs dry completely and cannot expand
C) The food supply diminishes
D) The nutritive power stops completely

Key Takeaway
The growth power ceases necessarily when the body dries completely, but whether it becomes null or remains in essence is debated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body has stopped growing physically. Are you content with the limits Allah has placed on your physical form, or do you secretly wish you were different? What does this lesson's emphasis on a 'natural proportion' teach you about accepting His decree for your life?
?
The growth power stops necessarily when the body dries and cannot expand. In your spiritual life, have you allowed your heart to become dry—unwilling to expand in obedience or love for Allah? What would it take for you to become 'moist' again, soft and receptive to growth?
?
The author hesitates whether the growth power becomes null or remains in essence after cessation. Consider a good deed or virtue you once practiced but have now stopped. Have you abandoned it completely, or does its capacity remain within you, waiting to be activated again? What will you do this week to revive that capacity?

Generative and Formative Powers for Species Preservation
Description of generative and formative powers for species preservation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the roles of the generative and formative powers in species preservation and the differences between the views of Hippocrates and Aristotle regarding their nature.

Core Idea
Two powers preserve species by shaping and generating from food.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into the biological and philosophical underpinnings of how species continue through individuals. It focuses on two of the four served powers—the generative and formative—and explains their functions, with insights from Hippocrates and Aristotle. Understanding these powers helps appreciate the Islamic philosophical synthesis of medicine and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the technical terms and the two different opinions of Hippocrates and Aristotle.
The passage also references the Kulliyat al-Qanun, a famous medical text.
2
Identify the two powers and their functions
Find where the generative and formative powers are defined. What does each do? The generative separates material from food; the formative gives forms and shapes in the womb.
3
Compare the views of Hippocrates and Aristotle
Note the difference: Hippocrates says the generative power is in all organs, so semen is diverse in reality; Aristotle says it does not separate from the other powers, so semen is similar in reality.
This difference impacts how one understands the nature of semen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ومنها ) أي من الأربع المخدومة ) اثنتان يحتاج إليهما لبقاء النوع ) فقط مع كون بقائه محتاجاً إلى الأولين أيضاً بتوسط الشخص. ( وهما المولدة والمصورة فالمولدة تفصل من الغذاء) بعد الهضم الأخير. (ما يصلح أن يكون مادة للمثل) أي لمثل ذلك الشخص الذي فصلت منه البذر ) وهي في كل البدن كما ذهب إليه بقراط وأتباعه فإن المني عندهم يخرج من جميع الأعضاء فيخرج من العظم مثله ومن اللحم مثله . وعلى هذا فالمني متخالف الحقيقة متشابه الامتزاج لأن الحس لا يميز بين الأجزاء. وعند أرسطو أن تلك القوة لا تفارق الاثنتين فيكون المني المتولد هناك متشابه الحقيقة وفي كليات القانون أن المولدة نوعان نوع يولد المني في الذكر والأنثى . ونوع يفصل القوى التي في المني أي الكيفيات المزاجية لأن أجزاءه متخالفة الأمزجة فيمزجها تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو فيخص للعصب مزاجاً خاصاً وكذا للعظم والشريان وغيرهما وذلك من مني متشابه الأجزاء أو متشابه الامتزاج ) والمصورة وهي توجد في المني عند كونه ( في الرحم خاصة تفيد تلك الأجزاء ) أي الأجزاء المتخالفة الحقيقة أو الاستعداد التي في المني ( الصور والقوى والأشكال والمقادير ( التي بها تصير مثلاً بالفعل بعد ما كانت مثلاً بالقوة وهاتان القوتان أعني المولدة والمصورة تخدمهما الغاذية وهو ظاهر، والنامية أيضا وذلك بأن تعظم الأعضاء وتوسع مجاريها حتى تصير إلى الهيئة الصالحة للتوليد. ولذلك لا يتكون المني إلا بعد عظم الأعضاء
And from them, i.e., from the four served powers, two are needed for the preservation of the species only, while its preservation also needs the first two through the intermediary of the individual. They are the generative and the formative. The generative separates from the food after the final digestion what is suitable to be material for the like, i.e., for the like of that person from whom the seed is separated. And it is in the whole body, as Hippocrates and his followers held, for semen in their view comes out from all organs: from bone its like, from flesh its like. And on this basis, semen is diverse in reality but similar in mixture because the senses do not distinguish between the parts. According to Aristotle, that power does not separate from the two, so the semen generated there is similar in reality. And in the Kulliyat al-Qanun, the generative is of two types: a type that generates semen in male and female, and a type that separates the powers that are in the semen, i.e., the temperamental qualities, because its parts are diverse in temperaments, so it mixes them in mixtures according to each organ, and allocates to the nerve a specific temperament, and likewise to bone, artery, and others, and that from semen similar in parts or similar in mixture. And the formative, which exists in the semen when it is in the womb specifically, gives to those parts, i.e., the parts diverse in reality or disposition that are in the semen, the forms, powers, shapes, and measurements by which they become an actual likeness after they were a potential likeness. And these two powers, I mean the generative and the formative, are served by the nutritive, which is evident, and also by the growth, by making the organs grow and expanding their channels until they reach the suitable state for generation. Therefore, semen is not produced except after the growth of the organs.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses two powers among the four served powers: the generative (مولدة) and the formative (مصورة), which are needed for the preservation of the species, though the first two powers (nutritive and growth) are also necessary via the individual. The generative power separates from the food, after final digestion, the material suitable to become seed for a similar individual. The author presents two medical views: Hippocrates believed the generative power exists in all organs, so semen comes from every part of the body; thus semen is diverse in reality (متخالف الحقيقة) but similar in mixture because the senses cannot distinguish the parts. Aristotle held that this power does not separate from the two (probably the nutritive and growth), so the semen is similar in reality. Then the author references the Kulliyat al-Qanun, which divides the generative into two types: one that generates the semen itself in male and female, and another that separates the powers (i.e., temperamental qualities) within the semen, because the semen's parts have diverse temperaments, and it mixes them appropriately for each organ. The formative power, present in the semen specifically in the womb, gives those parts their forms, powers, shapes, and measurements, actualizing them from potentiality to actuality. Both the generative and formative powers are served by the nutritive (obviously) and by the growth power, which enlarges organs and expands channels until they are fit for generation. Hence, semen is not produced until after the organs have grown.

Key Terms
المولدة
المصورة
المني
بقراط
أرسطو
كليات القانون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the generative and formative powers are independent of the nutritive and growth powers.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the generative and formative powers are served by the nutritive and growth powers; semen does not form until the organs have fully grown.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the view of Hippocrates and Aristotle regarding the generative power?
A) Hippocrates said it is in all organs; Aristotle said it does not separate from the two
B) Hippocrates said it is only in the womb; Aristotle said it is in all organs
C) Hippocrates said semen is similar in reality; Aristotle said it is diverse
D) Hippocrates said the generative power has two types; Aristotle said one type

Key Takeaway
The generative and formative powers work together to produce offspring, drawing on materials processed by the nutritive and growth powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passages describes how your physical existence—every cell, every organ—depends on a coordinated sequence of powers working together. If you were to trace the creativity behind this design back to its Origin, what would you say to Him right now? Do not just think: take a moment to actually express gratitude for the miracle of your own formation, and commit to not taking your body for granted.
?
The text shows that even great physicians like Hippocrates and Aristotle differed on the nature of semen. In matters of your own spiritual formation—like how your heart is shaped by faith—do you actively seek knowledge from the Qur'an and Sunnah, or do you rely on assumptions and hearsay? Identify one belief you hold about Allah's creation that you have not verified from revelation, and make a plan to verify it.
?
The generative and formative powers are served by the nutritive and growth powers; nothing in your physical creation operates alone. What about your spiritual life? Are you feeding your soul with the 'nutritive' of prayer and remembrance so that spiritual fruits (good deeds) may be generated? List one spiritual practice you will increase this week to ensure your soul grows and becomes ready to produce lasting good.

Three Proofs for the Attractive Power in the Stomach
The author presents three proofs for the attractive power in the stomach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three empirical proofs from involuntary swallowing, vomiting patterns, and animal behavior that demonstrate the stomach possesses an attractive power.

Core Idea
The stomach attracts food even against one's will, proving a built-in attractive power.

Before diving into the proofs, note that they rely on common experiences: food sometimes goes down involuntarily when hungry, sweet substances linger in vomit, and some animals' stomachs actively reach for food. The author uses these to argue that a pulling force must exist in the stomach.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the first proof about food moving involuntarily from mouth to stomach when the need is severe. Notice how the author argues that this must be forced, and since it's not a push, it must be a pull from the stomach.
Focus on the logical structure: forced movement requires a force, and the alternatives are push or pull.
2
Examine the second proof
Read the second proof about sweet and bitter substances. The sweet is last to come out in vomit, indicating the stomach attracts it; bitter substances are expelled reluctantly. What does this suggest about the stomach's preferences?
This proof uses the stomach's 'natural love' for sweetness.
3
Review the third proof
Read the third proof about the crocodile's stomach rising to meet food. This active movement shows a desire to attract food.
This proof extends to animals with short esophagi, making the attraction more visible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من المغتذى فإذ قد ينقلب الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة عند شدة الحاجة إليه بلا إرادة من المغتذى بل قد يريد الإنسان منعه ليمضغه فيغلبه الغذاء وينجذب إلى داخل فوجب أن تكون قسرية فلا بد من قاسر وهو إما دفع من فوق بأن يقال: الحيوان يدفعه باختياره وقد ظهر بطلانه وإما جذب من تحت وهو أن تجذبه المعدة بقوة جاذبة فيها وهو المطلوب. الوجه الثاني: أنه متى تغذى الإنسان بغذاء ثم يتناول بعده شيئاً حلواً واستعمل القيء وجد آخر ما يخرج بالقيء الحلو وليس ذلك إلا لجذب المعدة له أي للحلو إلى قعرها بواسطة محبتها إياه طبعاً. وإذا تناول الإنسان دواءً مراً كريهاً فالمريء والمعدة يرومان نفضه ولفظه ولا يزدرردانه إلا بعسر فربما اندفع بالقيء بلا اختياره. الوجه الثالث: قد تصعد المعدة لجذب الغذاء في بعض الحيوان القصير المريء كالتمساح حتى تخرج عند الاغتذاء بحيث تلاقي فمه لكونه واسعاً وما ذلك إلا لشوقها إلى اجتذاب الغذاء. فدلت هذه الوجوه الثلاثة على أن في المعدة قوة جاذبة. الوجه الرابع: الرحم بعد انقطاع الطمث عن قريب إذا خلا عن الفضول يشتد شوقه إلى المني حتى يحس يجذب الإحليل إلى داخله جذب المحجمة الدم إلى داخلها وقد سمى بعضهم الرحم حيواناً مشتاقاً للمني. فثبت بهذا الوجه وجود الجاذبة في الرحم. الوجه الخامس: الدم الذي يكون في الكبد مخلوطاً بالفضلات الثلاث أعني البلغم والصفراء والسوداء ثم تتمايز تلك الأمور المختلطة وينصب إلى كل عضو نوع من الرطوبة يليق به فلولا أن في كل عضو قوة جاذبة لتلك الرطوبة اللائقة به لامتنع ذلك التمايز وانصباب كل رطوبة إلى عضو على حده دائماً أو أكثرياً وهذه حجة واضحة على وجود القوة الجاذبة في جملة الأعضاء.
And [the first proof is] from the one who is nourished: food may be turned from the mouth to the stomach at the time of severe need for it without the will of the one nourished, rather the human may intend to prevent it to chew it, but the food overcomes him and is drawn inside. So it must be forced, and there must be a force. It is either a push from above by saying the animal pushes it voluntarily—and its falsity has become clear—or a pull from below, which is that the stomach pulls it by an attractive power in it, and that is what is desired. The second proof: when a human nourishes himself with food, then takes something sweet afterwards and induces vomiting, he finds the last thing that comes out with the vomit to be sweet, and that is only because the stomach pulls it (i.e., the sweet) to its bottom by means of its natural love for it. And if a human takes a bitter, unpleasant medicine, the esophagus and stomach try to expel and eject it and only swallow it with difficulty, so perhaps it is expelled by vomiting involuntarily. The third proof: the stomach may rise to attract food in some animals with a short esophagus, like the crocodile, so that it comes out at the time of feeding until it meets its mouth because it is wide, and that is only due to its desire to attract the food. So these three proofs indicate that the stomach possesses an attractive power. The fourth proof: the uterus, soon after the cessation of menstruation, when it is free of residues, its desire for semen intensifies so that it is felt pulling the urethra into its interior like a cupping glass pulls blood into its interior. And some have called the uterus an animal longing for semen. So by this proof, the existence of the attractive power in the uterus is established. The fifth proof: the blood that is in the liver is mixed with the three residues, namely phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile; then those mixed matters become differentiated, and each organ receives a type of moisture suitable to it. So were there not in each organ an attractive power for that moisture suitable to it, that differentiation and the pouring of each moisture into its specific organ constantly or for the most part would be impossible. And this is a clear proof for the existence of the attractive power in all the organs.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents five proofs for the existence of the attractive power (القوة الجاذبة) in the body. The author first gives three proofs specifically for the stomach: (1) food moves involuntarily from mouth to stomach when needed, requiring a pulling force from below; (2) when vomiting after eating something sweet, the sweet substance is the last to come out, indicating the stomach attracts it due to natural affinity, while bitter substances are expelled; (3) in animals with short esophagi like the crocodile, the stomach rises to meet the food, showing desire to attract. The fourth proof is for the uterus: after menstruation, it attracts semen like a cupping glass, demonstrating an attractive power. The fifth proof is general: blood mixed with residues differentiates in the liver, and each organ attracts its suitable humor; otherwise this constant separation would be impossible. The passage concludes that these proofs clearly establish the existence of the attractive power in all organs.

Key Terms
قوة جاذبة
جذب
معدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Swallowing is always a voluntary act.
Correction: The first proof shows that in times of intense hunger, food can be drawn into the stomach involuntarily, implying an attractive force in the stomach.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof about sweet substances demonstrate?
A) The stomach pushes sweet substances out last.
B) The stomach attracts sweet substances due to natural love.
C) Sweet substances are not affected by the stomach.
D) The stomach has no preference for sweet or bitter.

Key Takeaway
Three empirical observations—involuntary ingestion, selective retention of sweet, and active reaching in crocodiles—prove the stomach has an attractive power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that your stomach pulls food even when you try to prevent it. Reflect on a time you neglected your body's need for proper nutrition—perhaps by skipping meals or eating poorly. How does the stomach's relentless yet designed drive to nourish you make you feel about the One who built that drive into your very nature? Will you from now on honor that design by choosing foods that sustain you well?
?
The stomach has a natural love for sweet things, and even in vomiting, it clings to what is sweet. What are the 'sweet' desires in your own soul that you cling to, maybe even to your own harm? Are you willing to submit those desires to the wisdom of your Creator, trusting that His guidance is better for you than your natural inclinations?
?
Consider the crocodile's stomach rising to meet its food—an almost desperate desire for sustenance. Your own soul has a built-in longing for something beyond itself. What are you truly hungry for? Are you feeding that hunger with the eternal, nourishing words of your Lord, or with temporary distractions that leave you empty? What will you change today to satisfy your deepest need?

Proof for the Attractive Power in the Uterus
The author gives a single proof for the attractive power in the uterus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the behavior of the uterus during menstruation and its attraction for semen serves as a proof for the existence of an attractive power in the uterus.

Core Idea
The uterus actively attracts semen like a cupping glass draws blood, proving its attractive power.

The fourth proof shifts focus to the uterus, which after menstruation becomes empty and intensely desires semen. The author compares this to a cupping glass that pulls blood, suggesting a similar attractive force at work.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth proof
Read the description of the uterus after menstruation. Notice the language: 'its desire for semen intensifies' and it 'pulls the urethra into its interior.'
The author uses a medical analogy with cupping to explain the attraction.
2
Understand the analogy
Compare the uterus's action to that of a cupping glass. Just as the glass creates a vacuum to draw blood, the uterus actively draws semen inward. This implies an attractive power.
The phrase 'some have called the uterus an animal longing for semen' emphasizes its active role.
3
Consider the significance
Ask yourself: why does this prove an attractive power? Because the movement is not voluntary but instinctual, and it requires a force. The author concludes that this proof establishes the attractive power in the uterus.
This proof is gender-specific but logically parallel to the stomach proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من المغتذى فإذ قد ينقلب الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة عند شدة الحاجة إليه بلا إرادة من المغتذى بل قد يريد الإنسان منعه ليمضغه فيغلبه الغذاء وينجذب إلى داخل فوجب أن تكون قسرية فلا بد من قاسر وهو إما دفع من فوق بأن يقال: الحيوان يدفعه باختياره وقد ظهر بطلانه وإما جذب من تحت وهو أن تجذبه المعدة بقوة جاذبة فيها وهو المطلوب. الوجه الثاني: أنه متى تغذى الإنسان بغذاء ثم يتناول بعده شيئاً حلواً واستعمل القيء وجد آخر ما يخرج بالقيء الحلو وليس ذلك إلا لجذب المعدة له أي للحلو إلى قعرها بواسطة محبتها إياه طبعاً. وإذا تناول الإنسان دواءً مراً كريهاً فالمريء والمعدة يرومان نفضه ولفظه ولا يزدرردانه إلا بعسر فربما اندفع بالقيء بلا اختياره. الوجه الثالث: قد تصعد المعدة لجذب الغذاء في بعض الحيوان القصير المريء كالتمساح حتى تخرج عند الاغتذاء بحيث تلاقي فمه لكونه واسعاً وما ذلك إلا لشوقها إلى اجتذاب الغذاء. فدلت هذه الوجوه الثلاثة على أن في المعدة قوة جاذبة. الوجه الرابع: الرحم بعد انقطاع الطمث عن قريب إذا خلا عن الفضول يشتد شوقه إلى المني حتى يحس يجذب الإحليل إلى داخله جذب المحجمة الدم إلى داخلها وقد سمى بعضهم الرحم حيواناً مشتاقاً للمني. فثبت بهذا الوجه وجود الجاذبة في الرحم. الوجه الخامس: الدم الذي يكون في الكبد مخلوطاً بالفضلات الثلاث أعني البلغم والصفراء والسوداء ثم تتمايز تلك الأمور المختلطة وينصب إلى كل عضو نوع من الرطوبة يليق به فلولا أن في كل عضو قوة جاذبة لتلك الرطوبة اللائقة به لامتنع ذلك التمايز وانصباب كل رطوبة إلى عضو على حده دائماً أو أكثرياً وهذه حجة واضحة على وجود القوة الجاذبة في جملة الأعضاء.
And [the first proof is] from the one who is nourished: food may be turned from the mouth to the stomach at the time of severe need for it without the will of the one nourished, rather the human may intend to prevent it to chew it, but the food overcomes him and is drawn inside. So it must be forced, and there must be a force. It is either a push from above by saying the animal pushes it voluntarily—and its falsity has become clear—or a pull from below, which is that the stomach pulls it by an attractive power in it, and that is what is desired. The second proof: when a human nourishes himself with food, then takes something sweet afterwards and induces vomiting, he finds the last thing that comes out with the vomit to be sweet, and that is only because the stomach pulls it (i.e., the sweet) to its bottom by means of its natural love for it. And if a human takes a bitter, unpleasant medicine, the esophagus and stomach try to expel and eject it and only swallow it with difficulty, so perhaps it is expelled by vomiting involuntarily. The third proof: the stomach may rise to attract food in some animals with a short esophagus, like the crocodile, so that it comes out at the time of feeding until it meets its mouth because it is wide, and that is only due to its desire to attract the food. So these three proofs indicate that the stomach possesses an attractive power. The fourth proof: the uterus, soon after the cessation of menstruation, when it is free of residues, its desire for semen intensifies so that it is felt pulling the urethra into its interior like a cupping glass pulls blood into its interior. And some have called the uterus an animal longing for semen. So by this proof, the existence of the attractive power in the uterus is established. The fifth proof: the blood that is in the liver is mixed with the three residues, namely phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile; then those mixed matters become differentiated, and each organ receives a type of moisture suitable to it. So were there not in each organ an attractive power for that moisture suitable to it, that differentiation and the pouring of each moisture into its specific organ constantly or for the most part would be impossible. Commentary
This passage presents five proofs for the existence of the attractive power (القوة الجاذبة) in the body. The author first gives three proofs specifically for the stomach: (1) food moves involuntarily from mouth to stomach when needed, requiring a pulling force from below; (2) when vomiting after eating something sweet, the sweet substance is the last to come out, indicating the stomach attracts it due to natural affinity, while bitter substances are expelled; (3) in animals with short esophagi like the crocodile, the stomach rises to meet the food, showing desire to attract. The fourth proof is for the uterus: after menstruation, it attracts semen like a cupping glass, demonstrating an attractive power. The fifth proof is general: blood mixed with residues differentiates in the liver, and each organ attracts its suitable humor; otherwise this constant separation would be impossible. The passage concludes that these proofs clearly establish the existence of the attractive power in all organs.

رحم
جذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the fourth proof compare the uterus's action to?
A) A magnet attracting iron.
B) A cupping glass pulling blood.
C) A stomach digesting food.
D) A pump pushing fluid.

Key Takeaway
The uterus after menstruation actively pulls in semen, like a cupping glass draws blood, proving it possesses an attractive power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author compares the uterus's pull to a cupping glass. Have you ever reflected on the fact that your body is constantly performing actions you cannot perceive, like the uterus drawing in semen? How does this awareness change your sense of dependence on Allah every moment?
?
If the uterus after menstruation 'longs' for semen, does your own soul long for the provision of Allah in a similar way? What would it mean for you to 'pull' towards what is good with the same intensity?
?
The proof shows that even the most intimate bodily functions are meticulously designed. Are you living as if your body is a trust from Allah, or do you misuse it? What one change can you make today to honor that trust?

intermediate
5 min
Proof for the Attractive Power in All Organs
The author presents a general proof for the attractive power in all organs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the differentiation of blood components in the liver and their distribution to specific organs proves that each organ has an attractive power for its suitable humor.

Core Idea
Each organ must attract its specific fluid from mixed blood, otherwise separation would be impossible.

The fifth and final proof is the most general, covering all organs. It observes that blood in the liver is mixed with phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile, yet each organ receives its own specific fluid. This cannot happen without an attractive power in each organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth proof
Read how blood mixed with three residues in the liver becomes differentiated, and each organ receives a fluid suitable to it. Notice the logical conclusion: without an attractive power, this would be impossible.
The proof is based on the constant and consistent distribution over time.
2
Understand the implications
If there were no attractive power, the fluids would stay mixed randomly. The fact that each organ consistently gets its appropriate humor shows there must be a selective pulling force.
This proof is similar to final cause arguments: the regular outcome implies a cause.
3
Appreciate the generality
Unlike previous proofs focusing on specific organs, this proof applies to all organs, establishing the attractive power as a general physiological principle.
This is considered a 'clear proof' by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من المغتذى فإذ قد ينقلب الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة عند شدة الحاجة إليه بلا إرادة من المغتذى بل قد يريد الإنسان منعه ليمضغه فيغلبه الغذاء وينجذب إلى داخل فوجب أن تكون قسرية فلا بد من قاسر وهو إما دفع من فوق بأن يقال: الحيوان يدفعه باختياره وقد ظهر بطلانه وإما جذب من تحت وهو أن تجذبه المعدة بقوة جاذبة فيها وهو المطلوب. الوجه الثاني: أنه متى تغذى الإنسان بغذاء ثم يتناول بعده شيئاً حلواً واستعمل القيء وجد آخر ما يخرج بالقيء الحلو وليس ذلك إلا لجذب المعدة له أي للحلو إلى قعرها بواسطة محبتها إياه طبعاً. وإذا تناول الإنسان دواءً مراً كريهاً فالمريء والمعدة يرومان نفضه ولفظه ولا يزدرردانه إلا بعسر فربما اندفع بالقيء بلا اختياره. الوجه الثالث: قد تصعد المعدة لجذب الغذاء في بعض الحيوان القصير المريء كالتمساح حتى تخرج عند الاغتذاء بحيث تلاقي فمه لكونه واسعاً وما ذلك إلا لشوقها إلى اجتذاب الغذاء. فدلت هذه الوجوه الثلاثة على أن في المعدة قوة جاذبة. الوجه الرابع: الرحم بعد انقطاع الطمث عن قريب إذا خلا عن الفضول يشتد شوقه إلى المني حتى يحس يجذب الإحليل إلى داخله جذب المحجمة الدم إلى داخلها وقد سمى بعضهم الرحم حيواناً مشتاقاً للمني. فثبت بهذا الوجه وجود الجاذبة في الرحم. الوجه الخامس: الدم الذي يكون في الكبد مخلوطاً بالفضلات الثلاث أعني البلغم والصفراء والسوداء ثم تتمايز تلك الأمور المختلطة وينصب إلى كل عضو نوع من الرطوبة يليق به فلولا أن في كل عضو قوة جاذبة لتلك الرطوبة اللائقة به لامتنع ذلك التمايز وانصباب كل رطوبة إلى عضو على حده دائماً أو أكثرياً وهذه حجة واضحة على وجود القوة الجاذبة في جملة الأعضاء.
And [the first proof is] from the one who is nourished: food may be turned from the mouth to the stomach at the time of severe need for it without the will of the one nourished, rather the human may intend to prevent it to chew it, but the food overcomes him and is drawn inside. So it must be forced, and there must be a force. It is either a push from above by saying the animal pushes it voluntarily—and its falsity has become clear—or a pull from below, which is that the stomach pulls it by an attractive power in it, and that is what is desired. The second proof: when a human nourishes himself with food, then takes something sweet afterwards and induces vomiting, he finds the last thing that comes out with the vomit to be sweet, and that is only because the stomach pulls it (i.e., the sweet) to its bottom by means of its natural love for it. And if a human takes a bitter, unpleasant medicine, the esophagus and stomach try to expel and eject it and only swallow it with difficulty, so perhaps it is expelled by vomiting involuntarily. The third proof: the stomach may rise to attract food in some animals with a short esophagus, like the crocodile, so that it comes out at the time of feeding until it meets its mouth because it is wide, and that is only due to its desire to attract the food. So these three proofs indicate that the stomach possesses an attractive power. The fourth proof: the uterus, soon after the cessation of menstruation, when it is free of residues, its desire for semen intensifies so that it is felt pulling the urethra into its interior like a cupping glass pulls blood into its interior. And some have called the uterus an animal longing for semen. So by this proof, the existence of the attractive power in the uterus is established. The fifth proof: the blood that is in the liver is mixed with the three residues, namely phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile; then those mixed matters become differentiated, and each organ receives a type of moisture suitable to it. So were there not in each organ an attractive power for that moisture suitable to it, that differentiation and the pouring of each moisture into its specific organ constantly or for the most part would be impossible. And this is a clear proof for the existence of the attractive power in all the organs.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents five proofs for the existence of the attractive power (القوة الجاذبة) in the body. The author first gives three proofs specifically for the stomach: (1) food moves involuntarily from mouth to stomach when needed, requiring a pulling force from below; (2) when vomiting after eating something sweet, the sweet substance is the last to come out, indicating the stomach attracts it due to natural affinity, while bitter substances are expelled; (3) in animals with short esophagi like the crocodile, the stomach rises to meet the food, showing desire to attract. The fourth proof is for the uterus: after menstruation, it attracts semen like a cupping glass, demonstrating an attractive power. The fifth proof is general: blood mixed with residues differentiates in the liver, and each organ attracts its suitable humor; otherwise this constant separation would be impossible. The passage concludes that these proofs clearly establish the existence of the attractive power in all organs.

A) The liver separates blood into components.

C) The stomach attracts food.
D) The uterus attracts semen.

Key Takeaway
The consistent separation and distribution of specific fluids to each organ from mixed blood proves that every organ has an attractive power for its own suitable humor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would your attitude toward health change if you saw each organ's attraction as a direct act of divine sustenance?
?
The proof says each organ 'constantly or for the most part' receives its suitable moisture. When was the last time you thanked Allah for the reliability of your bodily functions? What would a day be like if just one organ failed to attract what it needs—and how does that thought make you feel about your current state of health?
?
You have likely taken your body's coordination for granted. If you truly believed that every organ has a God-given attractive power, how would that affect your reverence for your own body and your responsibility to care for it—starting from this very moment?

Definition of the Digestive Power and Its Distinction from the Nutritive Power
The author defines the digestive power and explains its role in preparing food for nutrition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the digestive power prepares food for nutrition and how it differs from the nutritive power.

Core Idea
The digestive power prepares; the nutritive power actualizes.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif clarifies the role of the digestive power as one of the four serving powers. The author distinguishes it clearly from the nutritive power by analyzing the two stages of food transformation. Read on to grasp this essential distinction in Islamic medicine and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the literal translation. Let the author's flow come through before you analyze.
Notice the technical terms: هاضمة (digestive), غاذية (nutritive).
2
Identify the definitions
Find the definitions of the digestive and nutritive powers. The digestive prepares food until it becomes a part in actuality; the nutritive requires foodstuffs to become a part in actuality.
3
Note the two states described
The author describes two states: (1) increase of readiness for the organic form (digestion), and (2) actual obtaining of the organic form (nutrition).
This is the core distinction.
4
Understand the role of 'cooking' (طبخ)
The transformation occurs through a cooking process that decreases readiness for the blood form and increases readiness for the organic form.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself why distinguishing these powers matters for the overall understanding of bodily functions and the hierarchy of powers.
This connects to the broader cosmological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية من الأربع الخادمة الهاضمة وهي تعد الغذاء إلى أن يصير جزءاً بالفعل من العضو فهي غير الغاذية أعني القوة التي تقتضي صيرورة الأغذية جزءاً بالفعل. وفي كليات القانون: وأما الهاضمة فهي التي تحيل ما جذبته الجاذبة وأمسكته الماسكة إلى قوام مهيئاً لفعل القوة المغيرة فيه وإلى مزاج صالح للاستحالة إلى الغذائية بالفعل. قال الإمام الرازي: هذا الكلام نص في أن القوة الهاضمة غير القوة الغاذية ويؤيده أنه جعل الغاذية مخدومة للقوى الأربع التي منها الهاضمة فلنتكلم في الفرق. فنقول: إذا جذبت جاذبة عضو شيئاً من الدم وأمسكته ماسكته فللدم صورة نوعية وإذا صار شبيهاً بالعضو فقد بطلت عنه هذه الصورة وحدثت صورة أخرى عضوية، فهناك كون للصورة العضوية، وفساد للصورة الدموية وإنما يحصلان إذا كان من الطبخ ما لأجله ينتقص استعداد المادة للصورة الدموية ويشتد استعدادها للصورة العضوية إلى أن تزول عنها الأولى وتحدث فيها الأخرى، فهاهنا حالتان إحداهما سابقة وهي تزايد استعداد قبول الصورة العضوية، والأخرى لاحقة حصول هذه الصورة فالحالة الأولى فعل القوة الهاضمة، والثانية فعل القوة الغاذية. وهذا معنى قوله: ( وهي ( أي الهضم الذي هو فعل الهاضمة ) استحالات ما واقعة بين تمام فعل الجاذبة وابتداء حصول فعل الغاذية التي هي كون ما أعني حصول الصورة العضوية).
The second of the four serving [powers] is the digestive (al-hāḍima), and it prepares the food until it becomes a part in actuality of the organ. So it is other than the nutritive (al-ghādhīya), I mean the power that requires the foodstuffs to become a part in actuality. And in the Kulliyāt al-Qānūn: As for the digestive, it is that which transforms what the attractive power attracted and the retentive power retained into a consistency prepared for the action of the altering power therein, and into a temperament suitable for transformation into actual nutrition. Imām al-Rāzī said: This speech is explicit that the digestive power is other than the nutritive power, and it is supported by his making the nutritive power served by the four powers, among which is the digestive. So let us speak about the difference. We say: When an organ's attractive power attracts something of blood and its retentive power retains it, the blood has a specific form. When it becomes similar to the organ, this form becomes invalid and another organic form occurs. So there is a coming-into-being of the organic form and a corruption of the blood form. These two only occur if there is a cooking [process] by which the readiness of the matter for the blood form decreases and its readiness for the organic form intensifies, until the first form ceases and the other occurs in it. So here there are two states: one prior, which is the increase of readiness to receive the organic form, and the other subsequent, which is the actual obtaining of this form. So the first state is the act of the digestive power, and the second is the act of the nutritive power. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'And it (i.e., digestion which is the act of the digestive power) is certain transformations occurring between the completion of the act of the attractive power and the beginning of the actualization of the act of the nutritive power, which is the coming-into-being, i.e., the obtaining of the organic form.'
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage defines the digestive power (الهاضمة) as one of the four serving powers (الخادمة) that prepare food for nutrition. It distinguishes it from the nutritive power (الغاذية). The author quotes from Kulliyāt al-Qānūn, describing the digestive power as transforming what the attractive and retentive powers have handled into a proper consistency and temperament for nutrition. Imām al-Rāzī affirms this distinction, noting that the nutritive power is served by the four powers, including the digestive. The author then explains the difference through an analysis of two states: first, the matter's readiness for the organic form increases (due to cooking/digestion) — this is the act of the digestive power; second, the organic form actually obtains — this is the act of the nutritive power. Thus digestion is a preparatory transformation between attraction and nutrition.

طبخ
صورة عضوية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the digestive and nutritive powers are the same because both relate to food.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes them: the digestive prepares (first state), while the nutritive actualizes (second state).

Key Takeaway
The digestive power prepares food for nutrition by increasing its readiness for organic form, while the nutritive power actualizes that form.

Two Objections to the Digestive/Nutritive Distinction
Two objections to the separation of digestive and nutritive powers with supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key objections Imam al-Razi raised against the separate existence of digestive and nutritive powers, and how they challenge a foundational distinction in Aristotelian biology.

Core Idea
Objections that make digestion and nutrition one power challenge a foundational distinction in Aristotelian biology.

Imam al-Razi, a formidable critic, did not simply accept the standard division of faculties. In this passage, he levels two powerful objections against separating the digestive and nutritive powers. Both objections aim to collapse the distinction by showing that the same power does the work. Let's see how he argues and what the responses imply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: two objections, each followed by supporting arguments and commentary.
The passage includes a commentary after the objections; don't skip it.
2
Identify the first objection
Find how al-Razi argues that the digestive power both moves food to similarity with the organ and conveys it, making it one with the nutritive power.
He uses the principle: whatever moves something to something else is the conveyor to it.
3
Understand the support for the first objection
Al-Razi cites Ibn Sina's argument about motion and rest to show that the cause removing food from its first state must also bring it to its final state, implying the digestive power alone suffices.
This actually strengthens the objection rather than refuting it.
4
Identify the second objection
The digestive power prepares the matter for the influx of the organic form; when readiness is complete, nutrition is done. Thus no separate nutritive power is needed.
Al-Razi notes that Galen did not list the nutritive power among the four serving powers.
5
Reflect on the debate
Consider how these objections challenge traditional faculty psychology and what counter-arguments might be offered.
The passage ends with a commentary clarifying textual points about 'thinning the thick' and 'thickening the thin'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعترض الإمام عليه أولا بما أشار إليه المنصف بقوله: ( ويمكن أن يقال: المحرك إلى مشابه العضو هو القوة الموصلة إليه ). وتقريره على ما في المباحث المشرقية أن القوة الهاضمة محركة للغذاء في الكيف إلى الصورة المشابهة لصورة العضو وكل ما حرك شيئاً إلى شيء آخر فهو الموصل إلى ذلك الآخر، فيكون الفاعل للفعلين قوة واحدة. أما الصغرى فظاهرة إذ لا معنى للهضم إلا التحريك عن الصورة الغذائية إلى الصورة العضوية وأما الكبرى فظاهرة أيضاً لأن ما حرك شيئاً إلى شيء كان المتوجه إليه غاية للمحرك والمعنى بكونه غاية أن المقصود الأصلي هو فعل ذلك الشيء. وقد اعترف ابن سينا بذلك حيث احتج على أن بين كل حركتين سكوناً، فقال: محال أن يكون الواصل إلى حد ما واصلاً إليه بلا علة موجودة موصلة. ومحال أن تكون هذه العلة غير التي أزالت عن المستقر الأول، هذا كلامه وهو يقتضي أنه لما كان المزيل عن الصورة الدموية هو الهاضمة وجب أن يكون الموصل إلى العضوية أيضاً الهاضمة، فهي الغاذية لا غير. واعترض ثانياً بما ذكره المصنف بقوله: ( كيف والمراد بالقوة هنا المعدة للمادة لفيضان الصورة عليها والمفيض لها وهو واهب الصور والقوة الهاضمة هي المفيدة بطبخها ونضجها للاستعدادات المختلفة بالقوة أي الشدة والضعف التي من جملتها ما يعد المادة لفيضان الصورة العضوية وتلك القوة المفيدة لهذه الاستعدادات مغنية عن قوى أخرى في الأعضاء ) لأنه إذا تم الإعداد وكمل الاستعداد فاضت الصورة وتمت التغذية، فإذن لا فرق بين الهاضمة والغاذية ولذلك لم يذكر جالينوس في شيء من كتبه الغاذية. وقال ابن سينا: بل المسيحي على ما في سوى قوله: ( واعترض ثانياً ) أي اعترض الإمام ثانياً. وقوله: وقال ابن سينا عطف على قوله: لم يذكر أي، ولذلك قال ابن سينا وقوله: والأظهر وإنما قال: والأظهر ولم يقل: والظاهر أو الصواب لأن عد الأربعة من الغاذية يتضمن عد الهاضمة من الغاذية أيضاً لكن مع انضمام زيادة لا يحتاج إليها هاهنا وتلك الزيادة هي عد الثلاثة للأخرى من الغاذية. وقوله: بترقيق الغليظ متعلق بقوله: تعد الفضل أما ترقيق الغليظ فكما في البول والعرق وأما تغليظ الرقيق فكما في الغائط والقيء.
Then Imam [al-Razi] objected to him first by what the author indicated by his statement: 'It can be said: The mover to similarity with the organ is the conveying power [to it].' Its exposition according to what is in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya is that the digestive power moves the food in quality toward the form similar to the organ's form, and everything that moves something to something else is the conveyor to that other, so the agent of both acts is one power. As for the minor premise, it is obvious, for there is no meaning of digestion except moving from the food form to the organic form. And as for the major premise, it is also obvious, because what moves something to something has the destination as its end, and the meaning of its being an end is that the primary intention is the act of that thing. And Ibn Sina admitted this when he argued that between every two motions there is rest, saying: It is impossible for that which reaches a certain limit to reach it without an existing conveying cause. And it is impossible for this cause to be other than that which removed it from its first resting place. This is his speech, and it necessitates that since the remover from the blood form is the digestive power, then the conveyor to the organic form must also be the digestive power; thus it is the nutritive power, no other. And he objected secondly by what the author mentioned in his statement: 'How? The intended [power] here is the one that prepares the matter for the influx of form upon it, and the effuser of it is the Giver of Forms. The digestive power is the one that, by its cooking and ripening, produces the various preparations in potentiality (i.e., intensity and weakness), among which is that which prepares the matter for the influx of the organic form. And that power which produces these preparations suffices for other powers in the organs.' For when the preparation is complete and the readiness is perfect, the form flows and nutrition is completed. So then there is no difference between the digestive and the nutritive. Therefore Galen did not mention the nutritive power in any of his books [among] these four which we called the serving [powers]. And Ibn Sina said: ... (the rest is garbled).
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents two objections by Imam al-Razi against the distinction between the digestive and nutritive powers, along with responses. The first objection argues that the digestive power moves food towards similarity with the organ, and whatever moves something to something else is the conveyor to it; hence the same power that moves (digestive) is the one that conveys (nutritive), making them one. The response uses Ibn Sina's argument about motion and rest to argue that the cause that removes the food from its first state (the digestive) must also be the cause that brings it to the final state (the nutritive), implying they are the same. The second objection argues that the digestive power prepares the matter by cooking and ripening, producing the necessary readiness for the influx of the organic form; when readiness is complete, the form flows and nutrition is complete, so no separate nutritive power is needed. It notes that Galen did not list the nutritive power among the four serving powers. The passage then includes a commentary explaining the text, including the meaning of 'thinning the thick' and 'thickening the thin' in relation to excreta.

Key Terms
هاضمة
غاذية
محّرك
موصل
استعداد
فيضان صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Razi's objections are quickly dismissed as weak or irrelevant.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Razi's objections are serious and supported by arguments from Ibn Sina and Galen, challenging a foundational distinction.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection of Imam al-Razi against the distinction between the digestive and nutritive powers?
A) The digestive power is not real because food is simply absorbed.
B) The digestive power both moves food to similarity and conveys it to the organ, so it is the same as the nutritive power.
C) The digestive power cannot prepare food because it is too weak.
D) Galen never distinguished between them.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's objections challenge the independence of the nutritive power, arguing that the digestive alone suffices for both transformation and delivery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Al-Razi was willing to challenge a long-accepted distinction in natural philosophy based on logical reasoning. What change in your daily routine would reflect that trust more authentically?

The Two States of Nutrition and the Roles of Digestive and Nutritive Powers
Defines two states of nutrition and attributes them to digestive and nutritive powers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two sequential states in the process of nutrition and how the digestive and nutritive powers are responsible for each state.

Core Idea
Digestion prepares the body; nutrition completes it — two distinct powers.

This passage from a classical text on natural philosophy explains the process of nutrition by distinguishing two distinct states. It clarifies the roles of the digestive and nutritive powers in transforming food into the organic form. Understanding this distinction helps avoid confusion between these related but different functions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author introduces two states.
Pay attention to the terms for the two powers.
2
Identify the two states
In your own words, describe the first state (increase of preparedness) and the second state (attainment of the organic form).
These are sequential.
3
Match states to powers
Notice which power is active in each state: digestive power for the first, nutritive power for the second.
The passage also defines digestion as transformations between powers.

Commentary

The author then explains that digestion (action of the digestive power) consists of transformations that take place between the completion of the attractive power's action and the beginning of the nutritive power's action, which results in the organic form.

The Four Servants of the Nutritive Power
Ibn Sina's four powers of the nutritive soul enumerated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's classification of the four powers serving the nutritive power and how it differs from Galen's view.

Core Idea
The nutritive power has four servants: attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive.

This lesson examines a key classification in Islamic philosophical physiology. The discussion centers on the powers that serve the nutritive faculty. Ibn Sina, following Aristotle, distinguishes four specific powers, whereas Galen did not treat them as separate. The passage clarifies the proper enumeration.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between Galen and Ibn Sina.
Galen did not mention the nutritive power separately; he called these four 'servants'.
2
Identify the four powers
Find where Ibn Sina enumerates the four powers: attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive. Notice how the digestive power is defined as changing food to resemble the organ.
The digestive power is explicitly included as one of the four.
3
Understand the commentary's role
The phrase 'الأظهر أن يقال' indicates a preference in interpretation: the digestive power is indeed part of the nutritive power.
This resolves any ambiguity about the classification.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters in the broader context of the soul's faculties and how it impacts the understanding of nutrition in the body.
This ties into the larger theory of the soul's powers.

Preparatory Actions of the Digestive Power: Thinning, Thickening, Cutting
Explains the three preparatory actions of the digestive power and its two modes of operation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three preparatory actions of the digestive power (thinning, thickening, cutting) and the two modes of preparation (by itself or with water).

Core Idea
The digestive power prepares both food and waste through three physical processes.

The digestive power does more than break down food; it also prepares waste for expulsion. This passage explains three key actions—thinning, thickening, and cutting—that ensure waste can be eliminated efficiently. It then notes that these actions may be carried out by the digestive power alone or with the help of moisture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Let the author's description sink in before interpreting.
Focus on the three actions and the examples of animals.
2
Identify the three preparatory actions
The passage lists three actions: thinning the thick, thickening the thin, and cutting the viscous. Note the purpose of each: to make waste expellable.
3
Understand the two modes of preparation
The digestive power can prepare food either by its own heat alone (as in falcons, snakes, camels) or by mixing with moisture (as in humans).
This shows variation in animal physiology.
4
Reflect on the examples
Consider why different animals have different digestive processes. This illustrates the wisdom in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And that the digestive power, just as it prepares the food suitable for becoming part of the organ, as previously mentioned, it also prepares the excess that is not suitable for assimilation, from it (i.e., from the food) for expulsion, by thinning the thick so that it can be expelled, and thickening the thin, for the body of the organ may absorb it due to its thinness, so those absorbed parts cannot be expelled. But if it is thickened, the organ does not absorb it and it is expelled completely. And cutting the viscous, for it sticks to the organ and cannot be expelled unless it is cut. And the preparation coming from the digestive power is either by itself, as in some animals like the falcon, whose heat dissolves the incoming food without needing water, and in the snake, which may eat dirt and make it into chyme without using water, and in the camel, which eats dry plants for days and does not drink water; or by mixing with water moisture, as in humans and most animals.

It does this by three operations: thinning the thick, thickening the thin, and cutting the viscous. The purpose is to ensure that waste can be expelled. The passage then describes two modes of preparation: the digestive power may operate by its own heat alone (as in some animals like falcons, snakes, and camels) or by mixing with water (as in humans and most animals).

First Stage of Digestion: In the Stomach
Describes the first stage of digestion in the stomach, beginning in the mouth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first stage of digestion as described in classical Islamic medicine, including its location and the transformation of food into chyme.

Core Idea
Digestion begins in the mouth, not the stomach, due to the continuous surface between them.

In this passage, the author explains the first of four stages of digestion, which occurs in the stomach but actually begins in the mouth. The description uses the analogy of a gourd with a long neck to illustrate the connection between the mouth and stomach. The author also provides a practical example involving chewed wheat to demonstrate the transformative effect of chewing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Understand the four stages context
Note that digestion has four stages; this lesson covers only the first. This sets the scope.
3
Examine the analogy of the gourd
The author compares the mouth-stomach connection to a gourd with a long neck to show how their surfaces are continuous.
This analogy highlights that the mouth is not separate from the stomach in the digestive process.
4
Analyze the example of chewed wheat
Chewed wheat applied to pustules is more effective than cooked or crushed wheat mixed with saliva, proving that chewing transforms the food's quality.
This example demonstrates that digestion starts in the mouth through the act of chewing.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why understanding the beginning of digestion is important for the overall process and for medical knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم للهضم الذي هو فعل الهاضمة مراتب أربع الأولى في المعدة بأن تجعل الغذاء كيلوسا وهو جوهر كماء الكشك الثخين في بياضه وقوامه وهذه المرتبة تبتدئ في الفم لاتصال سطحه بسطح المعدة حتى كأنهما سطح واحد على طريقة السطح الباطن من القرع الذي له عنق طويل ورأس مدور ولذلك تفعل الحنطة الممضوغة في إنضاج الدماميل ما لا تفعله المطبوخة منها ولا المدقوقة المخلوطة بالريق فدل ذلك على استحالة كيفيتها بالمضغ
Then digestion, which is the action of the digestive power, has four stages. The first is in the stomach, in that it makes the food into chyme, which is a substance like thick kashk water in its whiteness and consistency. This stage begins in the mouth due to the connection of its surface with the surface of the stomach, until they become like one surface, similar to the inner surface of a gourd that has a long neck and a round head. For this reason, chewed wheat in ripening pustules does what cooked wheat does not, nor does crushed wheat mixed with saliva; so that indicates the transformation of its quality by chewing.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage describes the first stage of digestion, which occurs in the stomach and turns food into chyme. It emphasizes that this stage begins in the mouth because the surface of the mouth is continuous with the surface of the stomach, analogous to a gourd with a long neck. The author gives an example: chewed wheat applied to a pustule is more effective than cooked or crushed wheat mixed with saliva, proving that chewing transforms the food's quality. This transformation is part of the digestion that starts in the mouth.

The Four Faculties of the Vegetative Soul
Enumerates the four faculties of the vegetative soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four faculties of the vegetative (nutritive) soul as enumerated by classical Islamic philosophers.

Core Idea
The vegetative soul has four distinct faculties: attraction, retention, digestion, and expulsion.

Before delving into the higher faculties of the animal and rational souls, it is essential to understand the foundational vegetative faculties that govern nutrition. This passage, drawn from a classical philosophical text, enumerates and briefly defines the four powers of the nutritive soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its structure. Note the key terms for the faculties.
The passage is concise; each faculty is named briefly.
2
Identify the four faculties
From the text, list the four faculties: attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive. Notice that the digestive faculty is singled out for a brief definition.
The digestive faculty is described as changing food to resemble the organ.
3
Understand the function of each
Based on the passage and commentary, explain what each faculty does: attraction draws food, retention holds it, digestion transforms it, expulsion expels waste.
These functions work together in the process of nutrition.
4
Reflect on the source
The passage references 'Kitab al-Mi'a al-Ghadhiya'. Consider why the author quotes this source and how it fits into the broader discussion of the soul's faculties.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المباحث ( الغاذية أربع ) وعد هذه ( الأربع منها ) . والأظهر أن يقال : وعد الهاضمة منها حيث قال في باب القوى والأفعال والأرواح من كتاب المائة الغاذية : أربع الجاذبة والماسكة والهاضمة وهي التي تغير الغذاء وتجعله شبيهاً بالعضو المغتذى، والرابعة الدافعة

He quotes from 'Kitab al-Mi'a al-Ghadhiya' that they are: the attractive force (al-jadhiba), which draws food; the retentive force (al-masika), which holds it; the digestive force (al-hadima), which changes and assimilates food to the body; and the expulsive force (al-dafi'a), which expels waste.
